Proof for God's Oneness and Refutation of Composition
Sanusi proves God's oneness via refutation of composition and clarifies the meaning of 'not composed'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi proves God's oneness by negating composition and why this negation does not imply atomism.

Core Idea
God's oneness means no composition; composition would lead to either many gods or contingency.

Sanusi now turns from establishing God's existence to establishing His oneness. He presents a crisp argument: if God were composed of parts, two devastating consequences follow. Then he carefully clarifies what negating composition does and does not mean, avoiding a common misunderstanding that would reduce God to a physical atom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note its structure
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the translation. Notice that it opens with a chapter heading, then a proof, and ends with a clarification.
The proof uses a conditional argument and two branches.
2
Identify the two possibilities if God had parts
Focus on the line that says 'إِمَّا أنْ يقوم بكل جزء صفاتُ الألوهية، أو يختص قيامها بالبعض'. Understand the two possibilities and their implications.
3
Understand the clarification about negation of composition
Read the final part where Sanusi explains what 'التركيب' does not mean. Compare 'جزء لا يتجزأ' with the correct meaning: not accepting size.
This guards against misunderstanding God as an indivisible atom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الدليل على وجوب الوحدانية له جل وعلا و وجوب استناد الكائنات كلها إليه ابتداء بلا واسطة آلة له منها ولامعين وأنه ليس في الوجود إلا الله سبحانه وأفعاله ويلزمُ أنْ يكونَ تعالى واحداً في ذاتِهِ ؛ بمعنى : أَنَّهُ غيرُ مُركَّب ، وإلا لزم أن يكون جسماً ، وأيضاً : فلو تركَّبَ مِنْ : جزأين فأكثر لم يخلُ : إِمَّا أنْ يقوم بكل جزء صفاتُ الألوهية ، أو يختص قيامها بالبعض ، والأوَّلُ يلزم منه تعدد الآلهة ، والثاني يلزم منه الحدوث ؛ للاحتياج إلى المخصِّص بعضها بصفاتِ الألوهية ؛ لاستواء جميعها في قبول تلك الصفاتِ وليس معنى نفي التركيب في الذاتِ العليَّةِ أنَّها جزء لا يتجزأ ، وإلا لزمَ أنْ تكون جوهراً فرداً ، وقد سبق استحالةُ الجرْمِيَّةِ عليهِ مطلقاً ، وإِنَّما المقصودُ : أَنَّ الذات العليَّةَ لا تقبلُ صِغَراً ولا كِبَراً ؛ لأنَّهما مِنْ عوارض الأجرام ، وهو تعالى يستحيلُ أنْ يكونَ جِرْماً .
Chapter: The proof for the necessity of oneness (wahdaniyya) for Him, may He be glorified and exalted, and the necessity of the dependence of all things upon Him initially without any instrument being an intermediary for Him from them or any assistant, and that there is nothing in existence except Allah, the Glorified, and His actions. And it follows that the Exalted must be one in His essence; meaning that He is not composed; otherwise He would necessarily be a body. Also: if He were composed of two or more parts, it would not be free: either the attributes of divinity would subsist in every part, or their subsistence would be specific to some. The first entails multiplicity of gods (taddud al-ilaha), and the second entails temporality (huduth) due to the need for a particularizer (mukhassis) to specify some of them with the attributes of divinity because of the equality of all of them in accepting those attributes. And the meaning of negating composition (nafy al-tarkib) in the sublime essence is not that it is an indivisible part, otherwise it would entail it being an individual atom (jawhar fard), and the impossibility of corporeality (jirmiyya) for Him has preceded absolutely. Rather the intent is that the sublime essence does not accept smallness or largeness because they are accidents of bodies, and the Exalted is impossible to be a body.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is a proof for God's oneness and a refutation of any composition in His essence. The author begins by stating the chapter heading, indicating the proof for the necessity of God's oneness and that all things depend on Him directly without intermediaries or assistants. Then he argues that God must be one in essence, meaning not composed. If He were composed, He would be a body, which is impossible. He then provides a detailed argument: if God were composed of two or more parts, two possibilities arise: either each part would possess the attributes of divinity, leading to multiple gods; or only some parts possess them, requiring a particularizer to assign divinity to those parts, implying that God is caused and thus temporal, which is impossible. Finally, he clarifies what is meant by negating composition: it does not mean that God is an indivisible atom, which would be a body; rather, it means that God does not accept smallness or largeness, which are accidents of bodies. Thus, God is not a body in any sense.

Key Terms
وحدانية
ذات
تركيب
مُركَّب
جوهر فرد
جرم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Negation of composition means God is an indivisible atom (jawhar fard).
Correction: Negation of composition means God has no parts and does not accept smallness or largeness; He is not a body at all.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi say would follow if God were composed of two or more parts?
A) He would be a body.
B) Either each part has divinity (multiple gods) or only some do (contingency).
C) He would be an indivisible atom.
D) He would have no attributes.

Key Takeaway
God's oneness is established by showing that composition is impossible, and this negation does not reduce God to an indivisible atom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The argument shows that if God were composed, He would need a particularizer, making Him dependent. Do you truly live as if Allah is al-Samad—the Independent—in every aspect of your life? In what area do you still rely on yourself or others, acting as if Allah needs your help? Make a sincere repentance and place your full trust in Him alone.
?

God's Unity in Attributes: No Likeness
Sanusi argues for God's unity in attributes by showing the impossibility of a likeness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God must be unique in His attributes and why any likeness would imply contingency (ḥudūth).

Core Idea
If God had a likeness, He would be contingent—so He is absolutely unique.

Sanusi continues his exposition of the necessary attributes of God by establishing that not only is God one in essence, but He is also one in His attributes, meaning He has no likeness. This argument uses the principle of distinguishment (takhṣīṣ) to show that any two likes would require a cause to differentiate them, leading to contingency. Let's examine this precise reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on unity of attributes
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice how Sanusi defines 'one in attributes' as 'no likeness to Him'.
2
Understand the consequence of having a likeness
If there were two likes, each would need a distinguisher to account for accidental differences. This distinguisher would make them contingent.
Contingency means something that could have been otherwise, which is impossible for God.
3
Reflect on why this proves uniqueness
Since God is eternal and necessary, He cannot require a distinguisher. Therefore, He cannot have any likeness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويلزمُ أيضاً : أنْ يكونَ تعالى واحداً في صفاته ؛ بمعنى : أنَّهُ لا مثلَ لهُ ، وإلا لزم الحدوث ؛ لاحتياج كل مِنَ المِثْلَينِ إِلى مَنْ يُخصِّصُهُ بالعارض الذي يمتاز به عن مثله. وأيضاً : لو كانَ معَهُ ثانٍ في الأُلُوهِيَّةِ للزمَ أَنْ يكونَ ذلكَ الثاني عام القدرةِ والإرادةِ مثلَهُ ، وذلكَ يؤدّي إلى اتِّصافِ أحدهما بالعجز ضرورةً ، سواء اختلفا على التضاد ـ وهو ظاهر - أوِ اتَّفقا ؛ لأنَّ الفعل الواحد يستحيل انقسامه ، فلا يمكن أن يقع إلا مِنْ أحدهما ؛ فيلزم عجز الآخر الذي لم يقع منه ، وإذا عجز أحدهما وجب عجز الآخر لتماثلهما ، وذلك يؤدّي إلى ألا يُوجَدَ شيءٌ مِنَ العالم ، والعيانُ يُكذِّبُهُ
And it is also necessary that He, Exalted, be one in His attributes; meaning that there is no likeness to Him, otherwise contingency would be necessary because each of two likes would need a distinguisher by the accident by which it is distinguished from its like. And also: if there were a second with Him in divinity, it would be necessary that this second be common in power and will like Him, and that would lead to one of them being necessarily characterized by incapacity, whether they differed in opposition — which is apparent — or agreed; because a single act is impossible to be divided, so it can only occur from one of them; thus the incapacity of the other from whom it did not occur follows, and if one of them is incapacitated, the other must be incapacitated due to their similarity, and that leads to nothing of the world existing, and observation contradicts that.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments for God's unity. First, God must be unique in His attributes with no likeness to Him; otherwise, if there were two likes, each would need a distinguisher (mukhaṣṣiṣ) to differentiate them by an accident, and that would imply contingency (ḥudūth). Second, the classic proof of mutual hindrance (burhān al-tamānuʿ): if a second god existed, both would be omnipotent. Whether they disagree or agree on an act, only one can perform a single indivisible act, rendering the other incapable. Since they are alike, both must be incapacitated, resulting in no creation, which is contradicted by the observed world.

Key Terms
وحدانية
مثل
حدوث
تخصيص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think God can have a likeness in some attributes but not others.
Correction: Sanusi argues that any likeness at all would imply contingency, because any accidental distinction requires a cause. Thus, God must be absolutely unique in all attributes.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why would having a likeness imply contingency?
A) Because likes would need a distinguisher for their differences.
B) Because likeness means dependence on another.
C) Because contingency is a necessary trait of all creation.
D) Because God cannot be compared to anything.

Key Takeaway
God's unity in attributes means He is absolutely unique, with no likeness, because any likeness would entail contingency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
The argument shows that contingency means need. If you are contingent, you are in constant need of Allah. When was the last time you felt that need deeply? What would change in your daily routine if you lived with constant awareness of your need for Allah?

Proof of Mutual Hindrance: Impossibility of Two Gods
Sanusi presents the burhān al-tamānuʿ to refute the possibility of two gods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof known as burhān al-tamānuʿ (proof of mutual hindrance) that demonstrates the impossibility of two omnipotent deities.

Core Idea
Two gods would inevitably make each other powerless, so the world would not exist.

The second part of Sanusi's argument for God's unity is a classic proof in Islamic theology: the proof of mutual hindrance. It shows that if there were two gods, both possessing complete power and will, their interaction—whether in cooperation or conflict—would necessarily lead to one being incapacitated, and by similarity, both, resulting in no creation. But since the world exists, there cannot be two gods.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the proof of mutual hindrance
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the hypothetical: if there were a second god, he would be similar in power and will.
2
Understand the two scenarios
Sanusi considers two scenarios: the two gods disagree (opposition) or agree. In either case, a single act cannot be divided, so only one can perform it.
Opposition is obvious; agreement also fails because the act is indivisible.
3
See the consequence of incapacity
If one is unable to perform the act, then due to their similarity, the other must also be unable. Thus both are incapacitated, and nothing would be created.
This contradicts the observed existence of the world.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Since the world exists, the assumption of a second god is false. Therefore, there is only one God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويلزمُ أيضاً : أنْ يكونَ تعالى واحداً في صفاته ؛ بمعنى : أنَّهُ لا مثلَ لهُ ، وإلا لزم الحدوث ؛ لاحتياج كل مِنَ المِثْلَينِ إِلى مَنْ يُخصِّصُهُ بالعارض الذي يمتاز به عن مثله. وأيضاً : لو كانَ معَهُ ثانٍ في الأُلُوهِيَّةِ للزمَ أَنْ يكونَ ذلكَ الثاني عام القدرةِ والإرادةِ مثلَهُ ، وذلكَ يؤدّي إلى اتِّصافِ أحدهما بالعجز ضرورةً ، سواء اختلفا على التضاد ـ وهو ظاهر - أوِ اتَّفقا ؛ لأنَّ الفعل الواحد يستحيل انقسامه ، فلا يمكن أن يقع إلا مِنْ أحدهما ؛ فيلزم عجز الآخر الذي لم يقع منه ، وإذا عجز أحدهما وجب عجز الآخر لتماثلهما ، وذلك يؤدّي إلى ألا يُوجَدَ شيءٌ مِنَ العالم ، والعيانُ يُكذِّبُهُ
And it is also necessary that He, Exalted, be one in His attributes; meaning that there is no likeness to Him, otherwise contingency would be necessary because each of two likes would need a distinguisher by the accident by which it is distinguished from its like. And also: if there were a second with Him in divinity, it would be necessary that this second be common in power and will like Him, and that would lead to one of them being necessarily characterized by incapacity, whether they differed in opposition — which is apparent — or agreed; because a single act is impossible to be divided, so it can only occur from one of them; thus the incapacity of the other from whom it did not occur follows, and if one of them is incapacitated, the other must be incapacitated due to their similarity, and that leads to nothing of the world existing, and observation contradicts that.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments for God's unity. First, God must be unique in His attributes with no likeness to Him; otherwise, if there were two likes, each would need a distinguisher (mukhaṣṣiṣ) to differentiate them by an accident, and that would imply contingency (ḥudūth). Second, the classic proof of mutual hindrance (burhān al-tamānuʿ): if a second god existed, both would be omnipotent. Whether they disagree or agree on an act, only one can perform a single indivisible act, rendering the other incapable. Since they are alike, both must be incapacitated, resulting in no creation, which is contradicted by the observed world.

Key Terms
عجز
تضاد
تماثل
برهان التمانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that two gods could cooperate without issue.
Correction: Sanusi shows that even in agreement, only one can perform a single indivisible act, leaving the other powerless. Thus cooperation does not solve the problem.

Checkpoint
In Sanusi's proof, what happens if the two gods agree on an act?
A) They can both perform it together.
B) The act is divided between them.
C) Only one can perform it, the other becomes incapacitated.
D) They take turns.

Key Takeaway
The proof of mutual hindrance shows that a second god is impossible because it leads to the non-existence of the world, which contradicts observation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that if you acknowledged another source of power besides Allah, the world would not exist. Yet in your daily life, do you sometimes fear or hope in created beings as if they have independent power? What would it take to truly internalize that no one else shares Allah's ability to create or control? Commit to one situation this week where you will consciously rely solely on Allah.
?
Sanusi argues that a second god would be incapacitated by the very act of creation. Reflect: what if you are 'incapacitating' Allah in your heart by giving weight to worldly causes as if they operate outside His will? Where have you placed your trust in something other than Allah's decree? Describe a specific worry you have, and rewrite it as a statement of tawheed.
?
The proof rests on the fact that observation affirms the world's existence, but do you live as though you truly see Allah's hand in that existence? When you witness the order of the world, does it provoke awe and certainty in you, or do you take it for granted? What one change can you make to your daily routine to transform your observation into a constant reminder of Allah's oneness?

Creatures Have No Real Effect
Sanusi derives the impossibility of creatures' effect from the unity proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, according to Sanusi, no created thing can have any real causal effect, and how this follows from the proof of God's unity.

Core Idea
No creature can truly cause anything; God alone is the real agent.

Sanusi has just established the proof of God's unity. Now he draws a powerful consequence: if God alone is the Creator and Sustainer, then no created thing can have any real effect of its own. This includes all actions, movements, and events—nothing happens except by God's power and will. Let's see how he argues this.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Notice the flow from the proof to the conclusion.
The passage is short but dense; take your time.
2
Understand the logical consequence
Sanusi argues: if a creature had a real effect, that effect would be outside God's power and will. Ask yourself why that would be impossible.
The key is that God is eternal and all-powerful; any effect must depend on Him.
3
Note the two types of causality denied
Sanusi explicitly denies direct causation (mubāsharah) and indirect generated causation (tawallud). Make sure you understand what each term means.
Some philosophers claimed effects could be generated indirectly (e.g., fire burning cotton). Sanusi rejects this too.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
This passage is a cornerstone of Ashʿarī occasionalism. Consider how this shapes the understanding of human actions and responsibility.
Human actions are created by God, but humans acquire them (kasb). That's a later topic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا الدليل تعرفُ استحالة أن يكون لشيءٍ مِنَ العالم تأثير البتةَ في أثر ما ؛ لما يلزمُ عليهِ مِنْ خروج ذلك الأثر عن قدرةِ مولانا جلَّ وعزَّ وإرادتِهِ ، وذلكَ يُوجِبُ أنْ يغلبَ الحادث القديم ، وهو محال. فلا أثرَ إذاً لقدرةِ المخلوقِ في حركة ولا سكون ، ولا طاعة ولا معصية، ولا في أثر ما على العموم ، لا مباشرةً ولا تولداً
And by this proof you know the impossibility of anything in the world having any effect whatsoever in any effect; because what would follow from that is that effect exiting from the power of our Master, Mighty and Majestic, and His will, and that would necessitate that the originated overcome the eternal, which is impossible. So there is no effect of the creature's power in movement nor stillness, nor obedience nor disobedience, nor in any effect at all, neither directly nor by derived causation (tawallud).
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage applies the previous proof of God's unity to the question of causal efficacy. Sanusi argues that if any created thing had a real effect on anything, that effect would be independent of God's power and will. This would mean a temporal event (al-ḥādith) overcomes the eternal (al-qadīm), which is absurd. Therefore, creatures have no effect whatsoever—no direct effect (mubāsharah) and no indirect generated effect (tawallud). All actions, movements, obedience, and disobedience are solely God's creation.

Key Terms
استحالة
تأثير
قدرة
إرادة
حادث
قديم
مباشرة
تولد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that created things like fire or water have real causal power to produce effects.
Correction: Sanusi denies any real causal power to creatures. All effects are directly from God. Fire does not burn; God creates burning when fire is present.

Checkpoint
What would follow if a creature had real causal effect according to Sanusi?
A) The effect would be attributed to God as well.
B) The effect would be outside God's power and will.
C) The creature would become eternal.
D) The effect would be independent of both.

Key Takeaway
No creature, whether human, angel, or natural object, has any real causal effect; all actions and events are solely the creation of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If no creature has any real effect, then what does that mean for the things you rely on every day—your job, your health, your relationships? Are you attributing power to them in your heart? How would your trust in God increase if you truly believed that only He has any effect? What would you do differently today if you fully internalized that your own actions have no independent power?
?
Consider a time when you felt proud of an achievement or ashamed of a failure. According to this lesson, your actions did not cause the outcome—God did. Can you honestly say you attribute both success and failure entirely to Him? If not, what steps can you take to shift your reliance from your own ability to God's sole agency?
?
This passage includes 'obedience and disobedience'—even your religious acts are not caused by you. Does that make you feel a sense of relief or unease? How does knowing that God creates your obedience affect the way you approach worship? Will you now ask Him more earnestly to grant you obedience and protect you from disobedience, since you cannot produce them yourself?

Praise by States: The Universal Testimony of Creation
Sanusi explains praise by states and its universal scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what is meant by 'praise by states' (al-hal) and why this type of praise encompasses all of creation, including non-rational beings, as a silent testimony to Allah's perfection.

Core Idea
All things praise Allah by their very existence and need, even without words.

Sanusi now introduces the most universal form of praise: praise by states (ahwal). Unlike the previous modes that involve conscious acts of the heart, tongue, or limbs, this praise is the involuntary testimony of all created things to Allah's perfection. It is based on the evident signs of primordial nature (fitra) and the mark of temporality (hadath) that demonstrate every being's absolute dependence on Allah. This lesson unpacks that concept and connects it to a profound Quranic verse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Notice the flow: definition, evidence, universality, and Quranic proof.
The margin note explains why this type is more universal.
2
Understand 'praise by states'
Grasp that this praise is not an action but a state — the very existence and dependence of things point to Allah's attributes.
Contrast with earlier praise by heart, tongue, limbs.
3
Identify the two evidences
The passage mentions 'shawahid al-fitrah' (evidences of primordial nature) and 'alamat al-huduth' (mark of temporality). Reflect on how these show need.
These are rational proofs for Allah's existence and attributes.
4
Reflect on the universal scope
Note that this praise includes all things, even non-rational beings, as the Quranic verse indicates: 'There is not a thing except that it glorifies His praise.'
The margin note emphasizes this inclusivity.
5
Revisit the common misunderstanding
Check the misconception section: this praise is involuntary testimony, not active worship. The passage guards against thinking only conscious beings can praise Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومُثْنى عليهِ أيضاً بالأحوالِ ؛ بما أثبتته شواهد الفطرة (٢) ، وعلامة الحدوث في جميع العوالم جملة وتفصيلاً مِنْ عظيم افتقارها على كلِّ حال إليه جل وعزَّ ، وبما أثبتَتْهُ مِنْ وجوبِ وجودِهِ تعالى وكمال علمه وقدرته وإرادته وحياتِهِ ، وغير وغير ذلكَ ممَّا هو مُقرَّر في محله ، وهذا النوع من الثناء في العالم أعم ممَّا هو قبله (۳) ، وإليه الإشارة بقولِهِ سبحانه وتعالى : ( وَإِن مِّن شَيْءٍ إِلَّا يُسَبِّحُ بِحَدِهِ وَلَكِن لَّا تَفْقَهُونَ تَسْبِيحَهُمْ ) [الإسراء : ٤٤] • فتبيَّنَ مِنْ قولِهِ تعالى : ﴿ وَلَكِن لَّا نَفْقَهُونَ تَسْبِيحَهُمْ ﴾ - ولم يقلْ (1) في هامش ( ب ) : ( لأنه يكون من ذوي العقول وغيره ، بخلاف ما قبله الثناء ) .
And He is also praised by states: by what the evidences of primordial nature have established, and the mark of temporality in all worlds, collectively and in detail, of their immense need in every state to Him, Exalted and Majestic, and by what they have established of the necessity of His existence, Exalted, and the perfection of His knowledge, power, will, life, and others, besides that, which is established in its place. And this type of praise in the world is more universal than what comes before it, and it is indicated by His saying, Exalted: 'And there is not a thing except that it glorifies His praise, but you do not understand their glorification' [Al-Isra:44]. So it is clear from His saying 'but you do not understand their glorification' — and He did not say... (Footnote in margin: 'because it includes those with intellect and others, unlike what precedes it of praise.')
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth and most universal mode of praise: praise by states (ahwal). This praise is based on the evident signs of primordial nature (fitra) and the mark of contingency (hadath) in all created things, which demonstrate their total dependence on Allah at every moment. These states also prove the necessary existence of Allah and His perfect attributes: knowledge, power, will, life, and others. The author notes that this type of praise is more universal (a'amm) than the previous types because it applies to all things, not just rational beings. He cites the Quranic verse: 'There is not a thing except that it glorifies His praise, but you do not understand their glorification' (Q17:44). The author then comments on the verse: Allah said 'but you do not understand' and did not say something else (the rest is cut off in the OCR). A margin note explains that this type of praise includes both those with intellect and others, unlike the previous types of praise (by heart, tongue, limbs) which are specific to humans.

Key Terms
الأحوال
الفطرة
علامة الحدوث
الافتقار
وجوب الوجود
تسبيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Praise of Allah is only the active worship performed by conscious beings through heart, tongue, and limbs.
Correction: The passage teaches that all things, even inanimate objects, praise Allah by their very state of dependence and by the signs they bear of His perfection. This is an involuntary testimony that does not require consciousness.

Checkpoint
Why is praise by states considered more universal than the previous types of praise?
A) Because it is done by angels
B) Because it includes all things, even those without intellect
C) Because it is louder
D) Because it requires more effort

Key Takeaway
Praise by states is the universal, involuntary glorification of Allah by all created things through their inherent need and the marks of temporality they bear.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every atom of your being is currently glorifying Allah by its sheer existence and need, then how can you justify using your body—those same atoms—to commit an act of disobedience? What will you do the next time your hand or tongue wants to sin?
?
The verse says you do not understand the glorification of other things. Are you content with that ignorance, or does it stir a longing in you to become aware of the praise surrounding you? What concrete step can you take today to 'listen' more attentively to creation's testimony?
?
Your own state of need—your hunger, your weakness, your dependence on air, food, and water—is itself a form of praising Allah. When you feel needy, do you resent it or embrace it as worship? How would your attitude toward your limitations change if you saw them as glorification?

Proof of the World's Temporality from the Impossibility of Eternal Attributes
Sanusi proves the world's temporality via the impossibility of eternal attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that the world must be temporal because its necessary attributes are changeable and cannot be eternal.

Core Idea
If the world's necessary attributes cannot be eternal, the world itself cannot be eternal.

Sanusi presents a concise proof for the temporality of the world (ḥudūth al-ʿālam). He argues that certain attributes, such as motion and rest, are necessary for bodies but are inherently changeable. Since change is impossible for the eternal, these attributes cannot be eternal, and consequently the bodies that possess them cannot be eternal either. This passage is a classic kalām argument that establishes the temporal nature of the entire world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to understand the overall argument. Pay attention to the flow from attributes to bodies to the world.
2
Identify the key premises
Identify the premises: (1) Attributes like motion and rest are necessary for bodies. (2) These attributes accept change. (3) Change is impossible for the eternal. Therefore, these attributes cannot be eternal.
Notice that Sanusi uses 'if they were in eternity, they would not accept change' as a conditional proof.
3
Trace the conclusion
Follow the conclusion: If the attributes cannot be eternal, then the bodies that possess them also cannot be eternal. Since the world is composed of bodies and their attributes, the world itself cannot be eternal. This is the desired proof.
The phrase 'which is what was sought' marks the end of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يستحيل ثبوتها في الأزلِ؛ كالحركة والسكون ونحوهما، بدليل أنها لو كانت في الأزلِ لما قبلت التغير؛ لاستحالة التغير على القديم. وإذا كانت صفات الأجرام اللازمة لها مستحيلةً في الأزلِ، كانت الأجرام كذلك مستحيلة الوجودِ في الأزلِ، والعالم منحصر في الأجرام والصفات التي تقوم بها، وقد بانَتِ استحالة ثبوتهما في الأزلِ، فالعالم إذا مستحيلُ الثبوتِ في الأزلِ، وهو المطلوب.
It is impossible for them to be established in eternity—like motion and rest and their like—by the proof that if they were in eternity they would not accept change, because change is impossible for the eternal. And if the necessary attributes of bodies are impossible in eternity, then bodies are likewise impossible in existence in eternity, and the world is confined to bodies and the attributes that subsist in them, and the impossibility of their establishment in eternity has become clear, so the world is impossible of establishment in eternity—which is what was sought.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the temporality of the world (ḥudūth al-ʿālam) based on the impossibility of eternal attributes for bodies. Sanusi begins by stating that certain attributes—such as motion and rest—cannot be eternal. The reason is that if they were eternal, they would be immutable, but change is impossible for the eternal (al-qadīm). Since bodies possess such attributes as necessary properties, it follows that bodies themselves cannot be eternal. The world consists entirely of bodies and their attributes; therefore the world cannot be eternal. The proof concludes with 'which is what was sought' (wa huwa al-maṭlūb). This is a standard kalām argument for the temporal creation of the universe.

Key Terms
الأزل
الصفات اللازمة
الأجرام
العالم
القديم
التغير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof denies that God has any attributes.
Correction: The proof only argues that changeable attributes (like motion) cannot be eternal; it does not address divine attributes, which are unchanging.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi argue that the world cannot be eternal?
A) Because God is eternal and cannot create an eternal world
B) Because the world's necessary attributes (like motion) are changeable, so they cannot be eternal, and thus the world itself cannot be eternal
C) Because the world is composed of atoms and accidents
D) Because the philosophers claim the world is eternal and they are wrong

Key Takeaway
The world cannot be eternal because its necessary attributes are changeable and change is impossible for the eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that everything in this world is temporary because change is a sign of being created. Think of one thing you value most in this life—a relationship, a possession, or a status. If it is subject to change, what does that mean for your attachment to it? How should you hold it in your heart so that your ultimate security is in Allah alone?
?
The proof shows that even the necessary attributes of bodies, like motion and rest, are temporal. Look at your own life: your moods, health, and circumstances are constantly changing. If these changes remind you that you are not eternal, what must you do today to prepare for the life that never ends?
?
If the world is temporal, then your time here is a limited opportunity. What is one change—internal or external—that you have been postponing out of comfort or fear, but that you know is necessary for your eternal well-being? Commit to a concrete step you will take this week.

The Nobility of Knowledge of Tawhid: A Hadith on Priority
Sanusi concludes with a hadith highlighting the superiority of knowledge of Allah over action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge of Allah (tawhid) is considered more virtuous than action, as established by the Prophet's hadith.

Core Idea
Knowledge of Allah is the foundation; without it, deeds are worthless.

Sanusi concludes his treatise by affirming the supreme obligation of tawhid. He warns that only a spiritually blind person would deny this. Then he brings a powerful prophetic tradition that settles the debate: knowledge of Allah is the best deed, because even a little action benefits with knowledge, but much action is useless without it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get a sense of the author's conclusion and the hadith.
2
Identify the main claim
Notice how Sanusi declares that only someone blind of insight would deny the obligation and honor of tawhid. This sets up the hadith as evidence.
The phrase 'أعمى البصيرة مريض السريرة' emphasizes spiritual blindness.
3
Analyze the hadith structure
The hadith is a dialogue: the companion asks about the best deed, the Prophet answers 'knowledge of Allah', the companion expresses surprise, and the Prophet clarifies the relationship between knowledge and action.
Pay attention to the companion's confusion: he asked about action but got an answer about knowledge.
4
Understand the key lesson
The Prophet's final statement shows that even a little action with knowledge of Allah benefits, but much action without knowledge does not. This establishes the priority of knowledge.
This refutes the idea that action alone is sufficient.
5
Reflect on the note about qadar
The marginal note connects qadar (divine decree) to God's attributes of knowledge and will, indicating that qadar is part of the knowledge of Allah.
This is an additional point, not the main focus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة : فوجوبُ علمِ التوحيدِ وعظم شرفِهِ لا ينكره إلا أعمى البصيرة مريضُ السريرةِ ، ولا حول ولا قوة إلا بالله . ومما يدلُّ على شرف هذا العلم وعظيم فضلِهِ : ما رُوِيَ أَنَّهُ قيلَ : يا رسولَ اللهِ ؛ أَيُّ الأعمالِ أَفَضلُ ؟ قالَ : « الْعِلْمُ بِاللَّهِ عَزَّ وَجَلَّ » ، فقيل : يا رسولَ اللهِ ؛ نسألُكَ عنِ العمل ، فتجيب عنِ العلم ؟! فقالَ : « إِنَّ قَلِيلَ الْعَمَلِ يَنْفَعُ مَعَ الْعِلْمِ بِاللَّهِ ، وَإِنَّ كَثِيرَ الْعَمَلِ لا يَنْفَعُ مَعَ الْجَهْلِ بِاللهِ (۲) وإنما دخل القدر في صفاته تعالى ؛ لأنه يرجع للعلم والإرادة . « صفاقسي »
And in summary: the obligation of the science of tawhid and the greatness of its honor is denied only by one who is blind of insight, sick of conscience. And there is no power and no strength except in Allah. And among what indicates the honor of this science and its great virtue is what was narrated: that it was said: 'O Messenger of Allah, which deeds are most virtuous?' He said: 'Knowledge of Allah Almighty.' Then it was said: 'O Messenger of Allah, we ask you about action, and you answer about knowledge?!' He said: 'Verily, a little action with knowledge of Allah benefits, and verily, much action without knowledge of Allah does not benefit.' And qadar only enters into His attributes because it refers back to knowledge and will. Al-Safaqusi.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage serves as a concluding affirmation of the obligation and honor of the science of tawhid (ilm al-tawhid). The author declares that only a spiritually blind and sick person would deny this. He then supports it with a hadith: a companion asked the Prophet about the best deed, and the Prophet answered 'knowledge of Allah.' When the companion expressed surprise, the Prophet clarified that even a little action is beneficial when accompanied by knowledge of Allah, but a lot of action is useless without it. This emphasizes the supreme importance of acquiring knowledge of God over mere outward deeds. The text then adds a marginal note: qadar (divine decree) is part of God's attributes because it relates to His knowledge and will. This note, attributed to al-Safaqusi, likely clarifies that the trait of faith called 'knowledge of qadar' is included in the knowledge of God's attributes.

Key Terms
علم التوحيد
الجهل بالله
العمل
القدر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that actions (e.g., prayer, charity) are inherently superior and that knowledge is secondary.
Correction: The hadith shows that knowledge of Allah is the most virtuous deed, as even a little action with knowledge benefits, but much action without knowledge is worthless.

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited, what is the best deed?
A) Prayer
B) Charity
C) Knowledge of Allah
D) Jihad

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of Allah is the most virtuous deed, as without it, even great actions are fruitless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever busied yourself with many acts of worship while neglecting to deepen your knowledge of Allah? How has that affected the quality of your connection with Him? Will you commit today to seeking understanding before increasing your deeds?
?
The Prophet said that a little action with knowledge benefits. What is one small deed you can perform today with greater consciousness of Allah, and what specific knowledge will you bring to that deed so it becomes meaningful?
?
If much action without knowledge does not benefit, what are you truly relying on? Is your worship merely outward habit, or is it anchored in real knowledge of Allah? What concrete step will you take this week to learn one new truth about your Lord?

Knowledge of Allah and Fear of Him
Quranic verse and commentary on knowledge leading to fear of Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Quran specifically links fear of Allah to knowledge of Allah Himself, not to general religious knowledge.

Core Idea
True fear of Allah flows from knowing Him.

This lesson examines a key Quranic verse that connects knowledge and fear of Allah. The accompanying commentary clarifies what kind of knowledge is meant—not any learning, but direct knowledge of Allah's essence and attributes. Let's see how the verse and its explanation reinforce this essential principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the concise structure of the verse and the commentator's clarification.
2
Understand the commentary's clarification
The commentary specifies that the 'knowledge' here is knowledge of Allah. Ask yourself: why is this distinction important?
The verse could be misinterpreted if we think any knowledge suffices.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this verse and commentary connect to the broader theme: knowledge of Allah leads to fear, which leads to piety. This is a foundational link in Islamic theology.
This lesson is short but important for understanding the purpose of theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي القرآن العظيم: {إنما يخشى الله من عباده العلماء} (فاطر: 28). ومعلوم أن المراد بالعلم الذي يستلزم الخشية إنما هو العلم بالله تعالى.
And in the Great Quran: {Indeed, from among His servants, only the knowledgeable fear Allah} (Fatir: 28). And it is known that the intended meaning of the knowledge that entails fear is only knowledge of Allah Almighty.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage presents a Quranic verse and its interpretation. The verse states that only the 'knowledgeable' (al-'ulama') fear Allah. The commentator clarifies that the knowledge referred to here is specifically knowledge of Allah (al-'ilm bi-Allah ta'ala), not any other type of knowledge. This reinforces the previous narrations: the knowledge that leads to true fear of Allah and thereby to piety is knowledge of His essence, attributes, and actions. Other branches of knowledge, while valuable, do not necessarily produce this fear if they lack the foundation of knowing Allah.

Key Terms
يخشى (yakhsha): fear
العلم بالله (al-'ilm bi-Allah): knowledge of Allah

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People may think that any kind of religious knowledge automatically produces fear of Allah.
Correction: The verse and commentary clarify that only knowledge of Allah Himself produces that fear.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary on verse 35:28, what kind of knowledge does the verse refer to when it says 'only the knowledgeable fear Allah'?
A) Knowledge of prophetic traditions
B) Knowledge of Islamic jurisprudence
C) Knowledge of Allah
D) Knowledge of the Quran

Key Takeaway
The Quran specifies that fear of Allah results from knowledge of Allah Himself, not from any other type of knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How much time do you actually spend each day seeking to know Allah—not just facts, but with your heart? If your knowledge does not increase your fear of Him, what is the point?
?
If you truly knew Allah as He deserves to be known, what would you do differently starting today? Name one sin you would abandon immediately because of that fear.
?
You claim to fear Allah, but are you more afraid of losing your job, reputation, or comfort than of disobeying Him? Why is that, and what does it reveal about the depth of your knowledge of Allah?

Ibrahim's Rational Proof for the Creator
Ibrahim's proof from change to a Creator and its Quranic praise

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibrahim's proof based on change establishes the need for an unchanging Creator and why this proof is praised in the Quran.

Core Idea
Ibrahim used change in the world to prove its originator—this is the essence of rational theology.

The Quran presents Ibrahim as the model of rational faith. His argument from change to a changeless Creator is the foundation of kalam's proof for God's existence. This lesson examines that proof and the Quranic endorsement it received.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Ibrahim's argument is presented concisely.
2
Identify the two premises of Ibrahim's proof
The passage says Ibrahim reasoned about the origination of the world by its being subject to changes. Identify the premises: (1) the world undergoes changes, (2) what undergoes change must have an originator and manager who is unchanging.
This is a classical kalam proof known as huduth al-'alam.
3
See the Quranic endorsement and command to follow Ibrahim
Allah attributes the argument to Himself and promises to raise ranks of those given such rational proofs. Also note the command to follow Ibrahim's creed.
This shows that rational theology is not a foreign innovation but rooted in prophetic practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال تعالى بعد استدلال خليله إبراهيم على نبينا وعليه الصلاة والسلام على حدوث العالم بملازمته التغيرات، وأنه لا بد لجميعه من مخترع ومدبر له لا يتغير ولا تحل به الحوادث: {وتلك حجتنا آتيناها إبراهيم على قومه نرفع درجات من نشاء} (الأنعام: 83)، فأضاف تعالى تلك الحجة إلى نفسه إضافة تشريف، وحكم برفع درجات من فتح له في معرفة الحق ببراهينه العقلية، وقد أمرنا جل وعلا بالاقتداء بخليله إبراهيم عليه الصلاة والسلام في قوله: {ملة أبيكم إبراهيم} (الحج: 78)، وقال: {ثم أوحينا إليك أن اتبع ملة إبراهيم حنيفا} (النحل: 123). ولا شك أن أئمة السنة رضي الله تعالى عنهم هم الذين اقتدوا بهذا الأمر، وفازوا برفع الدرجات ونيل أعلى المراتب عند الله تعالى جعلنا الله منهم دنيا وأخرى. [تفضيل العارفين بالأصول على العالمين بالفروع] وقد وقع في كلام الغزالي: أن أسرار الإلهيات لا يستقل بها وبالوقوف على طرق بعضها إلا الأنبياء والأولياء.
And the Exalted said after His beloved Ibrahim (upon our Prophet and upon him peace) reasoned regarding the origination of the world by its being subject to changes, and that necessarily its entirety must have an inventor and manager who does not change and does not undergo occurrences: {And that was Our argument which We gave Ibrahim against his people; We raise in degrees whom We will} (al-An'am: 83). So the Exalted attributed that argument to Himself as an addition of honor, and decreed the raising of degrees for whom He opened the way to knowledge of the truth through intellectual proofs. And the Exalted and Majestic commanded us to follow the example of His beloved Ibrahim (upon him peace) in His saying: {The creed of your father Ibrahim} (al-Hajj: 78), and He said: {Then We revealed to you to follow the creed of Ibrahim, inclining toward truth} (al-Nahl: 123). There is no doubt that the imams of the Sunnah, may Allah be pleased with them, are those who followed this command and succeeded in attaining raised degrees and the highest ranks with Allah. May Allah make us among them in this world and the next. [Preference of those who know the fundamentals over those who know the branches] And it is found in the speech of al-Ghazali: 'The secrets of the divine matters are not independently grasped and their paths known except by the prophets and the saints.'
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage begins by referencing the proof of Prophet Ibrahim for the origination of the world (huduth al-'alam). Ibrahim argued that the world is subject to change, and anything subject to change must have an originator (mukhtari') and manager (mudabbir) who is free from change and accident. This rational proof is then praised by Allah in verse 6:83, where Allah attributes this argument to Himself and promises to raise the ranks of those who are granted knowledge of the truth through such intellectual proofs. The passage then cites commands to follow Ibrahim's 'millah' (creed/path). It asserts that the imams of the Sunnah followed this path and attained high ranks. Finally, a heading indicates the preference of those who know the fundamentals (al-usul) over those who know the branches (al-furu'). A quote from al-Ghazali notes that the deepest secrets of divinity are only known independently and their ways understood by prophets and saints, implying that scholars of usul (kalam) follow in their footsteps.

Key Terms
حدوث العالم (huduth al-'alam): origination of the world
براهين عقلية (barahin 'aqliyya): rational proofs
ملة إبراهيم (millat Ibrahim): creed of Ibrahim

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rational proofs for God's existence are a foreign Greek innovation, not part of Islam.
Correction: The Quran praises Ibrahim's rational proof from change to a Creator, showing that using reason to affirm God is deeply rooted in prophetic tradition.

Checkpoint
What was the basis of Ibrahim's proof for the Creator?
A) The beauty of creation
B) The world's subject to change
C) A direct revelation
D) The testimony of angels

Key Takeaway
Ibrahim's proof from change to an unchanging Creator is a Quranically-endorsed rational argument that forms the core of kalam theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Allah honored Ibrahim for using intellectual proof—and He commands you to follow his creed. But do you actually take the time to rationally examine your faith, or do you rely on habit and imitation? What is one belief you hold that you have never questioned with your mind? Will you today ask Allah to guide you to a rational conviction based on the Quran?
?
Ibrahim's proof begins with observing change—everything in your life changes except Allah. What change are you currently resisting or fearing? Does your attachment to that changing thing reveal a lack of trust in the unchanging Creator? How will you shift your security from what changes to the One who never changes?
?
The imams of the Sunnah attained high ranks by following this path of rational proof from revelation. But you—do you seek knowledge of Allah through proofs, or do you avoid deep thought because it feels difficult? What is one step you will take this week to strengthen your certainty about a divine attribute using the method of Ibrahim?

Preferring Scholars of Usul over Scholars of Furu'
Preference for scholars of fundamentals over branches with Ghazali's support.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why scholars of usul (fundamentals) are preferred to scholars of furu' (branches) and how al-Ghazali's statement supports this.

Core Idea
Scholars of usul follow the path of prophets in grasping divine secrets, so they rank higher than those focused on branches.

After establishing the importance of rational proofs, the text makes a methodological distinction: those who master the fundamentals (usul) of religion are to be preferred over those who only study branches (furu'). A quote from al-Ghazali reinforces this by stating that the deepest secrets of divinity are known only by prophets and saints.

Guided Path
1
Read the short passage and translation
Read the heading and the Ghazali quote. Understand the contrast between usul and furu'.
2
Understand the meaning of 'usul' and 'furu''
Usul are the foundational principles of religion, including aqidah; furu' are secondary rulings like fiqh details. The passage states that those who know usul are preferred.
This is a common theme in classical Islamic scholarship.
3
Reflect on al-Ghazali's statement
Al-Ghazali says that the secrets of divinity (asrar al-ilahiyyat) are not independently grasped except by prophets and saints. This supports the idea that usul scholars, who engage with these secrets, are on a higher path.
It implies that true knowledge of God requires divine assistance and purification.

?

?

?

If I desire closeness to Allah, what concrete step will I take today to follow their path—such as reading a book on creed or increasing in sincere supplication?

Proof of God's Eternity and Division of the Necessary
Sanusi proves God's pre-eternity and divides the necessary into two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational demonstration for God's pre-eternity and how necessary truths are classified as self-evident or theoretical.

Core Idea
If God were temporal, an infinite regress would occur — but regress is impossible.

Theologians must establish that God is pre-eternal before discussing His attributes. Sanusi presents a concise proof: assuming God's temporal origination leads to an impossible infinite chain or vicious circle. Therefore, He must be pre-eternal. The passage then introduces a crucial distinction between self-evident and theoretical necessary truths that underpins subsequent reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, then the translation. Notice the logical structure: if-then leading to a contradiction.
2
Identify the argument
The argument is: If God were originated, He would need an originator, leading to infinite regress or circularity, both impossible. Therefore, God's origination is impossible, so pre-eternity is necessary.
This is a proof by contradiction (burhān khulf).
3
Understand the division of the necessary
The author states that from this proof we learn that the necessary is of two types: self-evident (ḍarūrī) and theoretical (naẓarī). Self-evident truths are known without proof; theoretical truths require demonstration.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this division matters: it establishes that some truths about God (like His pre-eternity) are not immediately obvious but can be proven rationally.
This sets the stage for the rest of the Kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لو كان تعالى حادثاً لافتقر إلى مُحدِثٍ ، ويتسلسل أو يدور ، وكلاهما مستحيل ، فحدوثه تعالى إذا مستحيل ، فالقِدَمُ لَهُ جَلَّ وعزَّ إذاً واجبٌ ؛ إذ لا واسطة بين القدم والحدوثِ . فقد عرفت بهذا أنَّ الواجب على قسمين : ضروري ونظري .
If the Exalted were temporally originated, He would need an originator, leading either to infinite regress or circularity, both of which are impossible. Therefore, His temporal origination is impossible. So pre‑eternity is necessary for Him, since there is no intermediate between pre‑eternity and temporal origination. You have thus known that the necessary is of two types: self‑evident and theoretical.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for God's necessity (wujūb al-qidam) using the argument from impossibility of infinite regress (tasalsul) and circularity (dawr). The author states: if God were temporally originated (ḥādith), He would need a creator, leading to either infinite regress or circularity, both impossible. Therefore, His temporal origination is impossible, and pre‑eternity (qidam) is necessary for Him, as there is no middle ground. Then the author introduces the division of the necessary (al-wājib) into two types: self-evident (ḍarūrī) and theoretical (naẓarī). This sets up the framework for the subsequent discussion of rational judgments.

Key Terms
الواجب
القديم
الحادث
التسلسل
الدور
الضروري
النظري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes God's pre-eternity without evidence.
Correction: The proof does not assume pre-eternity; it assumes the opposite (origination) and shows it leads to impossibility, thereby proving pre-eternity by contradiction.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude about God's pre‑eternity based on the argument in this passage?
A) It is possible.
B) It is necessary.
C) It is impossible.
D) It is unknown.

Key Takeaway
The rational impossibility of infinite regress and circularity proves that God must be pre-eternal, and from that we learn that necessary truths are either self-evident or theoretical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the impossibility of an infinite chain of causes, do you truly feel convinced that God must be eternal, or do you harbor a hidden doubt that you have not faced? What would it mean for your entire worldview if this proof failed?
?
The passage divides necessary truths into self-evident and theoretical. Is your belief in God based on a self-evident intuition or on a theoretical proof? Which is stronger for you personally, and what does that reveal about your intellectual honesty before your Creator?
?
If you had to defend God's pre-eternity using only reason, without quoting scripture, could you present this argument convincingly? If not, what does that say about your understanding of your own faith? And will you commit to memorizing this proof so that it becomes part of your own reasoning?

The Proof of the World's Temporality
Sanusi presents the proof of the world's temporality via the impossibility of a pre-eternal body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first kalām proof for the temporality of the world, based on the impossibility of a body being pre-eternal.

Core Idea
A pre-eternal body cannot be subject to change, but all bodies are—so the world must have a beginning.

Sanusi now guides you into the first substantive proof of Islamic theology: the temporality (hudūth) of the world. This proof uses the observation that everything other than God consists of bodies and their accidents, which are always changing. By supposing a body were eternal, we find it leads to an impossibility, proving the world must have been created.

Guided Path
1
Understand the subject: the world
Note that Sanusi defines 'the world' as everything other than Allah. This is the scope of the proof.
2
Identify the structure of bodies
Observe that he states all worldly things are bodies (ajrām) that have accidents (aʿrāḍ) like motion and rest.
3
Trace the impossibility argument
Follow the conditional: if a body were pre-eternal, it must be either moving, resting, or neither. Each option is impossible for a pre-eternal entity, so no body can be eternal.
This is the classic kalām argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا عرفت هذا : فأَوَّلُ ما تبدأُ بِهِ مِنَ النظرِ : النظر في حدوث العالم ؛ وهو كلُّ ما سوى الله تعالى ، فإذا نظرتَ فيه تجد جميعَهُ أجراماً تقوم بها أعراض ؛ مِن حركة وسكون وغيرهما ، فتقولُ في برهان حدوثِهِ : لو كانَ جِرْمٌ مِنْ أَجرامِ العالَمِ كالسماء والأرضِ مثلاً موجوداً في الأزلِ لم يخلُ : إِمَّا أَنْ يكونَ في الأَزْلِ مُتحرِّكاً ، أو ساكناً ، أو لا متحركاً ولا ساكناً ، والأقسام الثلاثة مستحيلة على الجزم
If you know this, then the first thing you begin with from inquiry is inquiry into the temporality of the world; and it is everything other than Allah the Exalted. So if you look into it, you find all of it is bodies (ajrām) to which accidents (aʿrāḍ) adhere, such as motion, rest, and others. So you say in the proof of its temporality: If a body from the bodies of the world, like the sky and the earth for example, existed in pre-eternity (al-azal), it would not be free from either being in pre-eternity moving, or resting, or neither moving nor resting; and the three divisions are impossible decisively.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument for the temporality (hudūth) of the world. The author first identifies the subject of inquiry: the world, defined as everything other than God. He then notes that observation shows the world consists of bodies (ajrām) that are subject to accidents (aʿrāḍ) like motion and rest. The proof is a conditional argument: suppose a body (e.g., the sky or earth) existed in pre-eternity (al-azal). Such a body would necessarily be either in motion, at rest, or neither (i.e., static but not in motion or rest). However, each of these three states is impossible for a pre-eternal entity: motion and rest are accidents that change, and a pre-eternal being cannot be subject to change; the third option (neither moving nor resting) is also impossible because a body must be characterized by one of these two accidents. Therefore, the premise that a body is pre-eternal leads to impossibility, so the world cannot be pre-eternal—it must have come into existence (ḥadatha). This is the classic kalām proof for the createdness of the universe.

Key Terms
حدوث العالم
أزل
جرم
عرض
برهان حدوث
حركة
سكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof shows the world was created from nothing (ex nihilo) in the philosophical sense.
Correction: The proof only establishes that the world is originated (ḥādith) and not eternal (azalī); it does not address whether it was created from nothing or from pre-existing matter.

Checkpoint
In the proof of the world's temporality, what three possibilities are considered for a pre-eternal body?
A) Being composed, simple, or both
B) Moving, resting, or neither
C) Having a beginning, middle, or end
D) Being necessary, possible, or impossible

Key Takeaway
The world cannot be pre-eternal because any pre-eternal body would have to be unchanging, yet all bodies are subject to motion or rest, which are changing accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the universe had a beginning, what does that mean for your daily sense of autonomy and purpose? You live as if the world revolves around you, but this proof shows you are part of a contingent creation. How does that truth sit with your pride?
?
The proof shows that everything besides Allah is subject to change and temporality. Look at your own life—your body, your possessions, your relationships—they all change and will end. Are you attaching your heart to things that are transient, or to the Eternal?
?
Sanusi begins with 'If you know this, then the first thing you begin with...' Are you someone who truly begins with this inquiry? Or do you live most days without reflecting on the most fundamental truths? What would change if you made this proof the starting point of your day?

Setting the Foundation for the Proof of Origination
Sanusi defines the world and presents the trilemma of states for an eternal body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the world as everything other than God and the basic trilemma that forms the backbone of the proof.

Core Idea
The world is everything besides God, and its proof begins with a trilemma.

Sanusi begins the proof by defining the world and stating the central trilemma. Before examining the detailed refutations, we must grasp the logical structure: either a body is moving, at rest, or neither. The impossibility of all three leads to the conclusion that the world cannot be eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for definitions
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to see how Sanusi defines the world and its components.
Notice that the world is defined as everything other than God.
2
Identify the three divisions
Find the three possibilities for an eternal body: moving, at rest, or neither. These form the trilemma.
3
Understand the logical structure
Recognize that the argument is a reductio: if an eternal body existed, it must be in one of these states. Since all are impossible, an eternal body cannot exist.
The conclusion is stated before the detailed refutations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كيفية النظرِ الموصل إلى حدوثِ العالم وتأصيله على أربعة أركان ] فإذا عرفت هذا : فأَوَّلُ ما تبدأُ بِهِ مِنَ النظرِ : النظر في حدوث العالم ؛ وهو كلُّ ما سوى الله تعالى ، فإذا نظرتَ فيه تجد جميعَهُ أجراماً تقوم بها أعراض ؛ مِن حركة وسكون وغيرهما ، فتقولُ في برهان حدوثِهِ : لو كانَ جِرْمٌ مِنْ أَجرامِ العالَمِ كالسماء والأرضِ مثلاً موجوداً في الأزلِ.. لم يخلُ : إِمَّا أَنْ يكونَ في الأَزْلِ مُتحرِّكاً ، أو ساكناً ، أو لا متحركاً ولا ساكناً ، والأقسام الثلاثة مستحيلة على الجِرْمِ في الأزلِ ؛ فيكون وجود الجِرْمِ في الأزلِ مستحيلاً ؛ لأنَّه لا يُعقل وجوده عارياً عن تلك الأقسام الثلاثةِ . أمَّا بيانُ استحالةِ القسم الثالث : فظاهر ؛ لأنَّهُ لا يُعقَلُ جرم في الأزلِ ولا فيما لا يزالُ .. ليس ثابتاً في الحيّز ولا منتقلاً عنه وأما بيانُ استحالة القسم الثاني - وهو كونُ الجِرْمِ ساكناً في الأزلِ - فوجهُهُ : أَنَّهُ لو كانَ كذلكَ لَمَا قَبِلَ أَنْ يتحرَّكَ أبداً ؛ لأنَّ سكونَهُ على هذا الفَرْضِ قديم ، والقديم لا يقبلُ العدم ؛ إذ لو قبل العدم لاحتاج وجوده إلى مُخصص ؛ لجوازه حينئذ ، فيكونُ مُحْدَثاً ، وقد فُرِضَ قديماً ، فهذا تناقض لا يُعقَلُ ، ودليلُ قَبولِ السكونِ العدمَ : مشاهدتنا الحركة في بعض الأجرام ، وذلك يقضي بجواز الحركة على جميع الأجرام ؛ لتماثلها . وأما بيانُ استحالة القسم الأوَّلِ ـ وهو كونُ الجِرْمِ في الأزلِ مُتحرِّكاً - فالوجه فيه : ما عرفت الآنَ في القسم الثاني ، ويزيدُ هذا القسم بوجه آخرَ مِنَ الاستحالة ؛ أنَّ حقيقة الحركةِ لا تُعقَلُ قديمةً ؛ إذْ هى الانتقالُ مِنْ حيز إلى حيز ، فهي إذاً لا تكون إلا طارئة على الجِرْمِ
The manner of investigation that leads to the origination of the world and its grounding rests on four pillars. If you know this, then the first thing you begin with of investigation is the investigation into the origination of the world—and it is everything other than God, exalted. When you investigate it, you find all of it to be bodies (ajrām) in which inhere accidents (a‘rāḍ) such as motion, rest, and others. So you say in the proof of its origination: If a body (jirm) among the bodies of the world, like the heaven and earth for example, existed in pre-eternity (al-azal), it would not be free from being either moving in pre-eternity, or at rest, or neither moving nor at rest. The three divisions are impossible for the body in pre-eternity; thus the existence of a body in pre-eternity is impossible, because its existence devoid of those three divisions is inconceivable. As for the explanation of the impossibility of the third division, it is clear, because no body is conceivable in pre-eternity or in what never ceases—neither established in a locus nor moving from it. As for the explanation of the impossibility of the second division—which is the body being at rest in pre-eternity—its aspect is that if it were so, it would never be able to become moving, because its rest on this assumption is eternal, and the eternal does not accept non-existence; for if it accepted non-existence, its existence would need a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ) due to its permissibility then, and it would be originated, while it was assumed eternal; this is an inconceivable contradiction. And the evidence for rest accepting non-existence is our witnessing motion in some bodies, and that necessitates the permissibility of motion for all bodies due to their similarity. As for the explanation of the impossibility of the first division—which is the body being moving in pre-eternity—its aspect is what you now know from the second division, and this division is made more impossible by another aspect: that the reality of motion is not conceivable as eternal, because it is the transition from a locus to a locus; thus it is only ever an accident occurring to the body.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage presents the systematic proof for the origination (ḥudūth) of the world, which is a foundational argument in Kalām for establishing the existence of a Creator. The author first defines the world as 'everything other than God' and states that it is composed of bodies (ajrām) that have accidents (a‘rāḍ) like motion and rest. He then formulates the proof: Suppose a specific body, e.g., the heaven or earth, existed in pre-eternity (al-azal). That body would have to be either (1) moving, (2) at rest, or (3) neither moving nor at rest. He then demonstrates that all three are impossible. The third division is impossible because a body without motion or rest is inconceivable—it must be either in a place (rest) or moving from it. The second division (eternal rest) is impossible because if rest were eternal, it could never accept motion; but we observe motion in bodies, proving that rest is not eternal. Moreover, if eternal rest were to cease, it would require a specifier, making it originated—but eternity precludes origination. The first division (eternal motion) is impossible for the same reason as the second, with the additional point that motion is by definition a transition from one locus to another, which is inherently temporal and cannot be eternal. Since all three possibilities are impossible, the assumption that the body existed in pre-eternity is impossible; therefore the body must be originated (ḥādith). This argument, often called the argument from the temporal origination of accidents (ḥudūth al-a‘rāḍ), establishes that the world is not eternal and therefore needs a Creator.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument is about a specific moment in time, requiring a body to be either moving or at rest at that instant.
Correction: The argument refers to the state of the body in pre-eternity (al-azal), not a temporal instant. It considers the entire eternal duration.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the world composed of?
A) Only bodies without accidents
B) Bodies and the accidents that inhere in them
C) Only accidents without bodies
D) Souls and bodies

Key Takeaway
The world is everything other than God, and its origination is proven by showing that an eternal body cannot exist because it would have to be in one of three impossible states.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If everything besides God is the world, and the world is proven to have a beginning, then your own existence is not self-sufficient. Do you live as though your life is eternal in itself, or as a contingent creation that will return to its Creator? What one change will you make today to acknowledge that your existence is not your own?
?
The proof shows that no body can exist in pre-eternity. Your own body is among the bodies of the world. Does this truth bring you anxiety or peace? Why? How should it affect your attachment to your physical form and your priorities?
?
Sanusi begins by saying 'the first thing you begin with of investigation is the investigation into the origination of the world.' Is the origination of your own existence your first concern? If not, what has prevented you from making it so, and what will you do to change that starting now?

Refuting the Possibility of an Eternal Body
Sanusi refutes each of the three possible states for an eternal body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why each of the three states (eternal motion, eternal rest, or neither) is impossible for a body in pre-eternity.

Core Idea
Neither eternal rest nor eternal motion can exist; the third option is inconceivable.

In this lesson, Sanusi provides detailed refutations for each of the three divisions. He shows that the third is logically impossible, the second leads to a contradiction with observed motion, and the first is impossible because motion is inherently temporal.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of the third division
Read the explanation that a body cannot be neither moving nor at rest, as it must occupy space.
This is the shortest refutation.
2
Study the refutation of eternal rest
Understand why eternal rest is impossible: if rest were eternal, it could never change, but we see motion. Also, if eternal rest ceased, it would require a cause, making it originated.
This refutation uses observation and logical contradiction.
3
Study the refutation of eternal motion
Learn why eternal motion is impossible: motion is transition from locus to locus, which is inherently temporal and cannot be eternal.
Sanusi also notes that the same reasoning from rest applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كيفية النظرِ الموصل إلى حدوثِ العالم وتأصيله على أربعة أركان ] فإذا عرفت هذا : فأَوَّلُ ما تبدأُ بِهِ مِنَ النظرِ : النظر في حدوث العالم ؛ وهو كلُّ ما سوى الله تعالى ، فإذا نظرتَ فيه تجد جميعَهُ أجراماً تقوم بها أعراض ؛ مِن حركة وسكون وغيرهما ، فتقولُ في برهان حدوثِهِ : لو كانَ جِرْمٌ مِنْ أَجرامِ العالَمِ كالسماء والأرضِ مثلاً موجوداً في الأزلِ.. لم يخلُ : إِمَّا أَنْ يكونَ في الأَزْلِ مُتحرِّكاً ، أو ساكناً ، أو لا متحركاً ولا ساكناً ، والأقسام الثلاثة مستحيلة على الجِرْمِ في الأزلِ ؛ فيكون وجود الجِرْمِ في الأزلِ مستحيلاً ؛ لأنَّه لا يُعقل وجوده عارياً عن تلك الأقسام الثلاثةِ . أمَّا بيانُ استحالةِ القسم الثالث : فظاهر ؛ لأنَّهُ لا يُعقَلُ جرم في الأزلِ ولا فيما لا يزالُ .. ليس ثابتاً في الحيّز ولا منتقلاً عنه وأما بيانُ استحالة القسم الثاني - وهو كونُ الجِرْمِ ساكناً في الأزلِ - فوجهُهُ : أَنَّهُ لو كانَ كذلكَ لَمَا قَبِلَ أَنْ يتحرَّكَ أبداً ؛ لأنَّ سكونَهُ على هذا الفَرْضِ قديم ، والقديم لا يقبلُ العدم ؛ إذ لو قبل العدم لاحتاج وجوده إلى مُخصص ؛ لجوازه حينئذ ، فيكونُ مُحْدَثاً ، وقد فُرِضَ قديماً ، فهذا تناقض لا يُعقَلُ ، ودليلُ قَبولِ السكونِ العدمَ : مشاهدتنا الحركة في بعض الأجرام ، وذلك يقضي بجواز الحركة على جميع الأجرام ؛ لتماثلها . وأما بيانُ استحالة القسم الأوَّلِ ـ وهو كونُ الجِرْمِ في الأزلِ مُتحرِّكاً - فالوجه فيه : ما عرفت الآنَ في القسم الثاني ، ويزيدُ هذا القسم بوجه آخرَ مِنَ الاستحالة ؛ أنَّ حقيقة الحركةِ لا تُعقَلُ قديمةً ؛ إذْ هى الانتقالُ مِنْ حيز إلى حيز ، فهي إذاً لا تكون إلا طارئة على الجِرْمِ
The manner of investigation that leads to the origination of the world and its grounding rests on four pillars. If you know this, then the first thing you begin with of investigation is the investigation into the origination of the world—and it is everything other than God, exalted. When you investigate it, you find all of it to be bodies (ajrām) in which inhere accidents (a‘rāḍ) such as motion, rest, and others. So you say in the proof of its origination: If a body (jirm) among the bodies of the world, like the heaven and earth for example, existed in pre-eternity (al-azal), it would not be free from being either moving in pre-eternity, or at rest, or neither moving nor at rest. The three divisions are impossible for the body in pre-eternity; thus the existence of a body in pre-eternity is impossible, because its existence devoid of those three divisions is inconceivable. As for the explanation of the impossibility of the third division, it is clear, because no body is conceivable in pre-eternity or in what never ceases—neither established in a locus nor moving from it. As for the explanation of the impossibility of the second division—which is the body being at rest in pre-eternity—its aspect is that if it were so, it would never be able to become moving, because its rest on this assumption is eternal, and the eternal does not accept non-existence; for if it accepted non-existence, its existence would need a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ) due to its permissibility then, and it would be originated, while it was assumed eternal; this is an inconceivable contradiction. And the evidence for rest accepting non-existence is our witnessing motion in some bodies, and that necessitates the permissibility of motion for all bodies due to their similarity. As for the explanation of the impossibility of the first division—which is the body being moving in pre-eternity—its aspect is what you now know from the second division, and this division is made more impossible by another aspect: that the reality of motion is not conceivable as eternal, because it is the transition from a locus to a locus; thus it is only ever an accident occurring to the body.

Commentary
This passage presents the systematic proof for the origination (ḥudūth) of the world, which is a foundational argument in Kalām for establishing the existence of a Creator. The author first defines the world as 'everything other than God' and states that it is composed of bodies (ajrām) that have accidents (a‘rāḍ) like motion and rest. Misconception: One might think the refutation of eternal rest is about the impossibility of rest itself, rather than the impossibility of eternal rest.

Checkpoint
Why is eternal rest (the second division) considered impossible?
A) Because rest is not an accident
B) Because if rest were eternal, the body could never move, but we see motion
C) Because motion is necessary for all bodies
D) Because the body would then not need a Creator

Key Takeaway
Each of the three possible states for an eternal body is impossible, so no body can be eternal; therefore the world must be originated (ḥādith).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage concludes that nothing physical is eternal. Your body, your possessions, your very existence is temporary. In light of this, what are you investing your time and energy in that will not last? How does knowing this change your decisions today?
?
You observe motion in some bodies as evidence that rest is not eternal. In your own life, when have you been shaken out of spiritual lethargy ('rest') by a sudden change or trial? Did you see it as a sign from Allah that nothing worldly is permanent? If not, will you look for such signs now?
?
Sanusi argues that an eternal state cannot change. But your own heart changes constantly: between faith and doubt, hope and fear. If your imān can change, it cannot be eternal. Are you taking for granted that your current state of faith will last? What will you do today to actively move toward more certainty and obedience?
