The Foundation: Inseparable Things and the Necessity of Origination
Sanusi introduces the conclusive proof and its core inference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of Sanusi's conclusive proof that the entire world is originated, based on the inference from one inseparable thing to another.

Core Idea
The proof infers the origination of the world from the origination of its inseparable properties.

Sanusi begins by establishing a necessary condition: for something to come into existence, it must be preceded by its transition from another state, and the eternal cannot be temporal. He then declares that this conclusive proof shows the whole world to be originated. The proof's essence is an inference from the origination of one of two inseparable things to the origination of the other.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening lines
Read from 'ولا بدَّ أنْ يتقدَّمَ' to 'وحاصله : الاستدلال بحدوثِ أحد المتلازمين على حدوثِ الآخرِ'. Notice the conditional structure and the declaration of the proof's conclusiveness.
The phrase 'البرهان القطعي' emphasizes certainty.
2
Identify the key premise
The proof relies on the principle that if two things are inseparable (mutalāzimān), the origination of one implies the origination of the other. Find this statement in the passage.
3
Understand the significance
Sanusi says this foundation is 'أصل عظيم' for all beliefs. Reflect on why establishing the world's temporal nature is crucial for Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا بدَّ أنْ يتقدَّمَ على وجودِها الكونُ في الحيز المنتقل عنه ، والقديمُ لا يُتصوَّرُ أنْ يكونَ طارئاً ، ولا أن يتقدَّمَ على وجودِهِ غيره. فقد خرج لك بهذا البرهان القطعي : كونُ العالم كلِّه حادثاً ؛ مِنْ عرشِهِ إِلى فَرْشِهِ ، لا يُتصوَّرُ في العقلِ أنْ يكونَ شيء منه قديماً . اعلم : أنَّ معرفة حدوثِ العالَمِ أصل عظيم لمعرفة سائر العقائدِ وأُس كبير لتحقيق ما يأتي مِنَ الفوائدِ ، وهذا الدليل الذي سلكت في أصل العقيدة دليل قطعي قريب للأفهام . وحاصله : الاستدلال بحدوثِ أحد المتلازمين على حدوثِ الآخرِ ؛ وذلكَ أنَّ العالم كله أجرام لا تُعقَلُ مُنفكّةً عن الحركة والسكون ، وقد تقدَّمَ بيان ذلك في مثال المستحيل ، ودلّ البرهان القطعي على ما أشرنا إليه في أصل العقيدةِ على استحالة ثبوتِ كلِّ مِنَ الحركة والسكون في الأزلِ ، فيلزمُ ضرورة أنَّ الأجرام التي لا تُعقَلُ منفكّةً عنهما يستحيل أن يكون منها شيء في الأزلِ ، وإلا لزم أن يكونَ في الأزلِ غير مُتحرّك ولا ساكن ، وذلك لا يُعقَلُ . وبالجملة : فوجودُ الجِرْمِ في الأزلِ على أي حالةٍ فُرِضَتْ مستحيل ، وأحواله منحصرةٌ في ثلاثة أقسام : الحركة ، والسكون ، ونفيهما (١) ، والثلاثةُ مستحيلة على الجِرْمِ في الأزلِ ، وزادَ الثالثُ بأنَّهُ مستحيل عليه أيضاً فيما لا يزالُ ؛ وهو ما يقابل الأزل ، فيكون وجود الجِرْمِ مطلقاً مستحيلاً في الأزلِ . وقد عرفت انحصارَ العالم كله في الأجرامِ وصفاتِها ، فالعالم كلُّهُ إذاً يستحيلُ أنْ يثبت شيءٌ منهُ في الأزلِ ، فيجب إذا الحدوث لجميعِه ؛ وهو المطلوب
And it is necessary that existence be preceded by coming-to-be in the space from which it moves, and the eternal cannot be conceived as occurring newly, nor that something else precedes its existence. So by this conclusive proof there has emerged for you: that the entire world is originated, from its Throne to its carpet; it is inconceivable in the intellect that any part of it be eternal. Know that knowledge of the origination of the world is a great foundation for knowledge of the rest of the beliefs and a major basis for realizing the benefits that come. And this proof, which I have followed in the foundation of belief, is a conclusive proof close to understanding. Its summary: inference from the origination of one of two inseparable things to the origination of the other. That is because the entire world is bodies that are not conceivable as free from motion and rest, and the explanation of that has preceded in the example of the impossible. And the conclusive proof has indicated, as we pointed out in the foundation of belief, the impossibility of the subsistence of both motion and rest in eternity. Hence it necessarily follows that the bodies, which are not conceivable as free from them, it is impossible that any of them be in eternity; otherwise it would necessitate that in eternity there be something neither moving nor still, and that is not conceivable. In summary: the existence of a body in eternity in any state assumed is impossible, and its states are limited to three divisions: motion, rest, and their absence (1), and all three are impossible for a body in eternity, and the third is additionally impossible for it also in what has no end (future eternity), which is the opposite of pre-eternity, so the existence of a body absolutely is impossible in pre-eternity. And you have known that the entire world is confined to bodies and their attributes, so the entire world, therefore, is impossible for any part of it to subsist in pre-eternity, so origination is necessary for all of it; and that is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage presents the conclusive proof (al-burhān al-qaṭʿī) for the temporal origination (ḥudūth) of the entire world. The author begins by stating a necessary condition: for something to come into existence, it must be preceded by its existence in the space from which it moves, and the eternal cannot be temporal nor preceded by another. He then declares that this proof establishes that the whole world, from the Throne to the earth, is originated; nothing in it can be eternal. He then exhorts the reader to know that understanding the world's origination is a foundational principle for all other beliefs and a key to subsequent benefits. The proof itself, he says, is conclusive and accessible. Its summary is an inference from the origination of one of two inseparable things (al-mutalāzimān) to the origination of the other. The world consists entirely of bodies (ajrām) that are inconceivable apart from motion or rest. Since motion and rest cannot be eternal (azalī) — as proven earlier by the example of the impossible — it necessarily follows that bodies, which cannot exist without them, cannot be eternal. If a body were eternal, it would have to be neither moving nor still at some instant, which is inconceivable. The author then analyzes the states of a body: it can only be in motion, rest, or neither (the absence of both). All three are impossible for a body in pre-eternity (al-azal); moreover, the third state (absence of both) is also impossible in future eternity (mā lā yazāl). Therefore, bodily existence absolutely is impossible in pre-eternity. Since the entire world is composed of bodies and their accident-like attributes, the whole world cannot subsist in pre-eternity, so it must be originated. This is the desired conclusion (al-maṭlūb).

Key Terms
البرهان القطعي
الحدوث
المتلازمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the core inference of Sanusi's proof as stated in this passage?
A) From the existence of movement to the existence of a mover.
B) From the origination of one inseparable thing to the origination of the other.
C) From the eternal to the temporal.
D) From the Throne to the carpet.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's conclusive proof rests on the inference that if one of two inseparable things is originated, the other must also be originated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the entire world, from its Throne to its carpet, is originated and necessarily dependent on a Creator, what does this mean for the things you rely on in your daily life? Are there any objects, people, or ambitions you treat as if they are independent and eternal, forgetting that they too are created and will pass away? How will you realign your trust today?
?
Sanusi emphasizes that knowledge of the world's origination is the foundation for all other beliefs. Have you truly felt the weight of your own contingency? When you look at yourself, do you see a being who came from nothing and could return to nothing at any moment? Does this realization drive you to turn to Allah with humility and gratitude, or do you live as if you are self-sufficient?
?
The proof is simple: two inseparable things share the same attribute of origination. Your life in this world is inseparable from its fleeting moments. If each moment is originated, then your entire existence is a series of gifts from Allah. How will you use the next moment of your life—the one you are reading this in—to acknowledge that gift and act in obedience to the One who originated it?

The Detailed Argument: Bodies, Motion, and Rest in Eternity
Sanusi provides the detailed proof that bodies cannot be eternal due to their necessary states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why bodies cannot be eternal by analyzing their necessary states of motion and rest.

Core Idea
Bodies require motion or rest, both impossible in eternity, so bodies must be temporal.

Sanusi now details the reasoning behind the proof. He argues that the world consists of bodies (ajrām), which are never free from motion or rest. Since motion and rest cannot be eternal (as proven earlier), bodies cannot be eternal. He then analyzes the three possible states of a body in pre-eternity—motion, rest, and the absence of both—and demonstrates that all are impossible, concluding that the entire world is originated.

Guided Path
1
Read the detailed argument
Read from 'وذلكَ أنَّ العالم كله أجرام' to the end. Pay attention to the logic: bodies are inseparable from motion/rest; motion/rest cannot be eternal; therefore bodies cannot be eternal.
The phrase 'لا تُعقَلُ مُنفكّةً عن الحركة والسكون' is key.
2
Identify the three states
Sanusi lists three states for a body in pre-eternity: motion, rest, and absence of both. Find this enumeration.
3
Understand the impossibility of each state
He argues that motion and rest cannot be eternal (based on earlier proof), and the absence of both is also impossible for a body in pre-eternity (and future eternity). Why? Because a body cannot be conceived without either motion or rest.
4
Conclude with the necessity of origination
Since bodies cannot be eternal, and the world is entirely bodies and their attributes, the whole world must be originated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا بدَّ أنْ يتقدَّمَ على وجودِها الكونُ في الحيز المنتقل عنه ، والقديمُ لا يُتصوَّرُ أنْ يكونَ طارئاً ، ولا أن يتقدَّمَ على وجودِهِ غيره. فقد خرج لك بهذا البرهان القطعي : كونُ العالم كلِّه حادثاً ؛ مِنْ عرشِهِ إِلى فَرْشِهِ ، لا يُتصوَّرُ في العقلِ أنْ يكونَ شيء منه قديماً . اعلم : أنَّ معرفة حدوثِ العالَمِ أصل عظيم لمعرفة سائر العقائدِ وأُس كبير لتحقيق ما يأتي مِنَ الفوائدِ ، وهذا الدليل الذي سلكت في أصل العقيدة دليل قطعي قريب للأفهام . وحاصله : الاستدلال بحدوثِ أحد المتلازمين على حدوثِ الآخرِ ؛ وذلكَ أنَّ العالم كله أجرام لا تُعقَلُ مُنفكّةً عن الحركة والسكون ، وقد تقدَّمَ بيان ذلك في مثال المستحيل ، ودلّ البرهان القطعي على ما أشرنا إليه في أصل العقيدةِ على استحالة ثبوتِ كلِّ مِنَ الحركة والسكون في الأزلِ ، فيلزمُ ضرورة أنَّ الأجرام التي لا تُعقَلُ منفكّةً عنهما يستحيل أن يكون منها شيء في الأزلِ ، وإلا لزم أن يكونَ في الأزلِ غير مُتحرّك ولا ساكن ، وذلك لا يُعقَلُ . وبالجملة : فوجودُ الجِرْمِ في الأزلِ على أي حالةٍ فُرِضَتْ مستحيل ، وأحواله منحصرةٌ في ثلاثة أقسام : الحركة ، والسكون ، ونفيهما (١) ، والثلاثةُ مستحيلة على الجِرْمِ في الأزلِ ، وزادَ الثالثُ بأنَّهُ مستحيل عليه أيضاً فيما لا يزالُ ؛ وهو ما يقابل الأزل ، فيكون وجود الجِرْمِ مطلقاً مستحيلاً في الأزلِ . وقد عرفت انحصارَ العالم كله في الأجرامِ وصفاتِها ، فالعالم كلُّهُ إذاً يستحيلُ أنْ يثبت شيءٌ منهُ في الأزلِ ، فيجب إذا الحدوث لجميعِه ؛ وهو المطلوب
And it is necessary that existence be preceded by coming-to-be in the space from which it moves, and the eternal cannot be conceived as occurring newly, nor that something else precedes its existence. So by this conclusive proof there has emerged for you: that the entire world is originated, from its Throne to its carpet; it is inconceivable in the intellect that any part of it be eternal. Know that knowledge of the origination of the world is a great foundation for knowledge of the rest of the beliefs and a major basis for realizing the benefits that come. And this proof, which I have followed in the foundation of belief, is a conclusive proof close to understanding. Its summary: inference from the origination of one of two inseparable things to the origination of the other. That is because the entire world is bodies that are not conceivable as free from motion and rest, and the explanation of that has preceded in the example of the impossible. And the conclusive proof has indicated, as we pointed out in the foundation of belief, the impossibility of the subsistence of both motion and rest in eternity. Hence it necessarily follows that the bodies, which are not conceivable as free from them, it is impossible that any of them be in eternity; otherwise it would necessitate that in eternity there be something neither moving nor still, and that is not conceivable. In summary: the existence of a body in eternity in any state assumed is impossible, and its states are limited to three divisions: motion, rest, and their absence (1), and all three are impossible for a body in eternity, and the third is additionally impossible for it also in what has no end (future eternity), which is the opposite of pre-eternity, so the existence of a body absolutely is impossible in pre-eternity. And you have known that the entire world is confined to bodies and their attributes, so the entire world, therefore, is impossible for any part of it to subsist in pre-eternity, so origination is necessary for all of it; and that is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage presents the conclusive proof (al-burhān al-qaṭʿī) for the temporal origination (ḥudūth) of the entire world. The author begins by stating a necessary condition: for something to come into existence, it must be preceded by its existence in the space from which it moves, and the eternal cannot be temporal nor preceded by another. He then declares that this proof establishes that the whole world, from the Throne to the earth, is originated; nothing in it can be eternal. He then exhorts the reader to know that understanding the world's origination is a foundational principle for all other beliefs and a key to subsequent benefits. The proof itself, he says, is conclusive and accessible. Its summary is an inference from the origination of one of two inseparable things (al-mutalāzimān) to the origination of the other. The world consists entirely of bodies (ajrām) that are inconceivable apart from motion or rest. Since motion and rest cannot be eternal (azalī) — as proven earlier by the example of the impossible — it necessarily follows that bodies, which cannot exist without them, cannot be eternal. If a body were eternal, it would have to be neither moving nor still at some instant, which is inconceivable. The author then analyzes the states of a body: it can only be in motion, rest, or neither (the absence of both). All three are impossible for a body in pre-eternity (al-azal); moreover, the third state (absence of both) is also impossible in future eternity (mā lā yazāl). Therefore, bodily existence absolutely is impossible in pre-eternity. Since the entire world is composed of bodies and their accident-like attributes, the whole world cannot subsist in pre-eternity, so it must be originated. This is the desired conclusion (al-maṭlūb).

Correction: The proof specifically relies on the inseparability of bodies from motion/rest and the impossibility of these states being eternal. It does not argue from an infinite regress of events.

Conclusion: The Entire World is Temporally Originated
Sanusi concludes the proof by summarizing the logical implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi concludes the proof for the temporal origination of the entire world from the nature of bodies and their attributes.

Core Idea
The entire world is temporally originated because bodies cannot exist in pre-eternity.

Sanusi now brings the argument to its conclusion. Having established that the world is nothing but bodies and their attributes, and that bodies cannot be pre-eternal due to their dependence on temporally originating accidents, he deduces that the whole world must be temporally originated. This is the desired conclusion of the proof.

Guided Path

Bodies are substances, attributes are accidents that inhere in them.

From previous lessons, you know that bodies are always accompanied by temporally originating accidents, and an infinite regress of such accidents is impossible. Therefore, bodies cannot exist without a beginning.
This is the core of the proof.
4

If the world is entirely bodies and their attributes, and bodies cannot be pre-eternal, then no part of the world can be pre-eternal. Hence, the entire world is temporally originated (hadith).
The conclusion follows necessarily from the premises.
5

Note that the conclusion covers both bodies and their attributes, so the proof applies to the whole world without exception.

Why the Eternal Cannot Cease to Exist
Sanusi proves eternal cannot cease by contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the eternal (God) cannot cease to exist, based on the rational proof that cessation would imply contingency and contradiction.

Core Idea
If God could cease, He would be contingent; but the eternal must be necessary.

Sanusi presents a concise but powerful argument demonstrating that the eternal being cannot cease to exist. He uses a reductio ad absurdum: if the eternal ceased, its existence would be merely possible, not necessary, and thus it would be originated. But that contradicts its eternality. This passage establishes a key attribute of God: necessary permanence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the key terms: qadim, jā'iz, wājib, muḥdath.
2
Identify the conditional premise
The argument starts with the supposition 'if it ceased to exist'. Identify the consequence drawn from this assumption.
3
Understand the implication
From the assumption, Sanusi deduces that existence would become possible (jā'iz) rather than necessary (wājib). Reflect on why this follows – what is necessary cannot cease.
4
Trace the contradiction
Whatever is possible must be originated (muḥdath). So the eternal would be originated, which is a direct contradiction. See how the argument forces a contradiction.
This is the heart of the proof: impossibility of eternal cessation.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The contradiction shows the original assumption is false. Therefore, the eternal cannot cease. Ask yourself: what does this tell us about God's nature?
Contrast with the misconception that God could cease by His will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الخامس - وهو إثباتُ استحالةِ عُدْمِ القديم - : فوجهُهُ : أَنَّهُ لوِ انعدمَ لَكانَ وجوده جائزاً لا واجباً ، والجائز لا يكون إلا مُحدثاً ، فيكون هذا القديم مُحدَثاً ! وهو تناقض
And as for the fifth—which is the proof of the impossibility of the cessation of the eternal—its aspect is: that if it ceased to exist, its existence would be possible (jāʾiz) rather than necessary (wājib), and the possible is only originated (muḥdath), so this eternal would be originated! That is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage provides a proof that the eternal (al-qadīm, i.e., God) cannot cease to exist. Sanusi argues by reduction: if the eternal were to cease, then its existence would have been merely possible (jāʾiz) rather than necessary (wājib). But whatever is merely possible must have been originated (muḥdath) — that is, it came into existence after not existing. So the eternal would be originated, which contradicts its very definition as eternal. Therefore, the eternal's existence is necessary and cannot end.

Key Terms

واجب

تناقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction

Correction: Sanusi shows that cessation would imply God's existence was merely possible, not necessary, which would make Him originated and hence not eternal. Thus, necessary existence precludes cessation.

Checkpoint

A) Because it would violate the law of non-contradiction
B) Because it would imply it was originated
C) Because change is impossible for it
D) Because it is material

Key Takeaway
The eternal (God) cannot cease to exist because that would imply He is contingent and originated, contradicting His necessary and eternal nature.

Proof that Accidents Cannot Be Eternal
Sanusi proves that motion and rest cannot be eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that motion and rest cannot be beginningless, and why this implies the body itself has a beginning.

Core Idea
If accidents were eternal, existence and non-existence would coexist.

This lesson presents a core argument from Sanusi proving that the world is created. He focuses on motion and rest—two accidents that necessarily alternate. By showing that neither can be eternal, he establishes that the body (which always has one of them) also has a beginning. This is a key proof for the temporal origination of the universe.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without translating. Notice the logical structure: a premise about change, a conditional, a contradiction, and a conclusion.
The Arabic uses technical terms: أزل (pre-eternity) and لا يزال (what is without end).
2
Understand the translation
Read the English translation to grasp the argument. Identify the key steps: (1) motion and rest accept change, (2) non-existence is necessary for them in pre-eternity, (3) if one existed in pre-eternity, existence and non-existence would combine, (4) therefore they have a beginning.
3
Analyze the logical flow
Work through the reasoning: Why can't motion or rest be eternal? Because they are subject to change (they alternate). If they were eternal, they would have to exist forever, but they also must cease at some point (since they change)—a contradiction.
The key is that anything subject to change cannot be eternal because change implies non-existence of the previous state.
4
Connect to the body
The argument concludes that the body must also have a beginning because it is inseparable from motion or rest. Reflect on why that follows: a body is always either moving or at rest, so if both motion and rest have a beginning, the body cannot be eternal.
This is a standard kalām argument for the temporal origination of the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بطلان حوادث لا أَوَّلَ لها : فيُؤخَذُ مِنْ إقامة البرهان على استحالة ثبوتِ كلّ واحدٍ مِنَ السكون والحركة للجرْمِ في الأزلِ ؛ لقبولِهما التبدُّلَ فيما لا يزالُ ، وإذا وجبَ العدم لكلِّ واحدٍ منهما في الأزلِ ، فلو وُجِدَ واحد منهما في الأزلِ لاجتمع وجود الشيء وعدمه ! محال لا يُعقَلُ ، وهذا مِنْ براهين استحالة حوادث لا أوَّلَ لها ، فقد ثبت إذاً أنَّ الحركة والسكونَ لهما أوّل ، وذلك يستلزم أنْ يكونَ للجزم الملازم لهما أوّل أيضاً ، وهو المطلوب
As for the invalidity of events with no beginning: it is taken from establishing the proof of the impossibility of each of rest and motion subsisting in the body in pre-eternity; because they accept change in what is without end. And if non-existence is necessary for each of them in pre-eternity, then if one of them existed in pre-eternity, existence and non-existence of a thing would combine! An impossibility inconceivable. And this is among the proofs of the impossibility of events with no beginning. So it is established that motion and rest have a beginning, and that necessitates that the body which is inseparable from them also has a beginning, and that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is a proof that accidents (specifically motion and rest) cannot be eternal (without beginning). The argument follows: motion and rest are subject to change (they alternate), so in what is without end (the future) they must change. If they were eternal in the past (pre-eternity), they would have to be subject to non-existence in pre-eternity, but if one existed in pre-eternity, existence and non-existence would coexist—a contradiction. Therefore, each has a beginning. Since the body (jirm) is inseparable from motion or rest (it is always in one state), the body must also have a beginning. This proves the world's origination.

Key Terms
حوادث لا أول لها (events with no beginning)
الأزل (pre-eternity)
السكون (rest)
الحركة (motion)
الجرم (body)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if an accident is unchanging, it could be eternal.
Correction: Even unchanging accidents are subject to change (they are inherently temporary), so they cannot be eternal. The argument shows that motion and rest, though they may persist, must change eventually.

Checkpoint
Why does the impossibility of eternal motion and rest imply the body has a beginning?
A) Because the body is identical with motion and rest
B) Because the body is inseparable from motion or rest, so its beginning follows theirs
C) Because the body is eternal by itself
D) Because motion and rest are substances

Key Takeaway
Motion and rest cannot be eternal because their inherent changeability leads to a logical contradiction, and this forces the body to have a beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If everything you rely on—your body, your existence—has a beginning, then what does that demand of you regarding the One who brought it into being? How will you respond to that demand today?
?
The proof shows that existence and non-existence cannot coexist. Your life now is existence; one day it will be non-existence. Are you living in a way that acknowledges this inevitable transition? What one change would you make starting now?
?
This argument forces you to admit that you are not eternal. Does that humble you or unsettle you? How can you let that truth transform your priorities this week?

The Precision of 'Mukhassis' Over 'Fa'il'
Sanusi explains the preference for 'mukhassis' over 'fa'il'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'mukhassis' (specifier) is more precise than 'fa'il' (agent) in the context of proving God's existence.

Core Idea
A contingent thing needs a specifier, not just an agent.

Sanusi draws attention to a subtle but important terminological choice in the creed. While 'fa'il' (agent) might seem sufficient, the creed uses 'mukhassis' (specifier) to highlight the rational basis for needing a creator: a thing equally open to existence and non-existence requires a cause that specifies one over the other. This passage explains that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the flow of the argument.
The passage is incomplete; the full argument may continue on the next page.
2
Identify the two terms
Find the words 'المخصص' (mukhassis) and 'الفاعل' (fa'il) in the text. Understand that both refer to God, but the author prefers 'مخصص'.
3
Understand the reasoning
The passage states that 'مخصص' is used to alert to the reason for needing an agent: the essence of a contingent thing accepts both existence and non-existence, so a specifier is needed to tip the balance.
This is a key concept in kalam: the principle of determination (tarjih).
4
Reflect on precision in kalam
Consider why such terminological precision matters in Islamic theology. How does 'مخصص' avoid potential misunderstandings that 'فاعل' might introduce?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( لاحتاجَ وجودُهُ إلى مُخصص ) أي : فاعل يخترعُه ، وإنَّما عبرنا بالمخصص للتنبيه على سبب احتياج السكون إلى الفاعل ؛ وهو أنَّ ذاتَهُ لمَّا قبلَتِ الوجود والعدم، فلو وقع الوجود بدلاً مِنَ العدمِ
His saying: '(its existence needed a mukhassis)' — that is, an agent (fa'il) who invents it. And we expressed by 'mukhassis' only as a notification of the reason for rest's need for an agent; which is that its essence, when it accepted existence and non-existence, then if existence occurred instead of non-existence...
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage explains why the creed uses the term 'mukhassis' (specifier) instead of 'fa'il' (agent) in the context of proving the need for a creator. The author states that 'mukhassis' means an agent who creates (yakhtera'uhu). The reason for choosing 'mukhassis' is to draw attention to the underlying cause: the contingent thing (here, rest) has an essence that is equally receptive to existence and non-existence. For existence to occur rather than non-existence, a 'specifier' (mukhassis) is needed to tip the balance. The explanation is cut off, but the point is that 'mukhassis' more directly conveys the need for a cause that specifies one of two equally possible states.

Key Terms
المخصص (the specifier)
الفاعل (the agent)
الوجود (existence)
العدم (non-existence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think 'mukhassis' and 'fa'il' are synonyms and interchangeable.
Correction: The author shows that 'mukhassis' is preferred because it specifically emphasizes the contingency and the need for a cause that chooses existence over non-existence, making the rational argument clearer.

Checkpoint
Why did the author prefer the term 'mukhassis' over 'fa'il'?
A) Because 'fa'il' is not Arabic
B) To highlight that the essence is equally open to existence and non-existence, requiring a specifier
C) Because 'mukhassis' is a longer word
D) To avoid anthropomorphism

Key Takeaway
The term 'mukhassis' emphasizes that God's act is not merely creating but specifying what would exist from among equally possible states, making the contingency argument more precise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your very existence could have been non-existence. Do you live each day with the awareness that you were chosen to exist, and that this choice demands a response? How does that change your next decision?
?
If every detail of your life—your faith, your health, your circumstances—is the result of a divine specifier choosing from infinite possibilities, what does that imply about how you should treat these blessings? Are you acting as if they were random or purposeful?

Allah's Existence
beginner

Bodies Require a Creator (Mukhassis)
Sanusi defines mukhassis and argues for the necessity of a creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why every body (and any contingent thing) needs a mukhassis (specifier) and how this forms the basis of the kalam argument for a creator.

Core Idea
A body that could equally exist or not cannot exist without a specifier.

This lesson presents a foundational argument in Islamic theology: why the existence of any body cannot be taken for granted. Sanusi explains that since bodies are contingent—they could either exist or not—their actual existence requires a cause that tips the balance. This argument, known as the mukhassis argument, is a cornerstone of the cosmological proof for God's existence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the key term mukhassis
Read the passage and note how Sanusi defines 'mukhassis' as an agent that invents or creates. This term is specific to the argument for a creator.
The term 'mukhassis' is related to 'takhsis' (specification).
2
Grasp the principle of equal possibility
Notice the premise: the essence of the body admits both existence and non-existence equally. This is the reason it needs a specifier.
This principle applies to all contingent things, not just bodies.
3
Follow the logical deduction
If existence occurred without a cause, it would be a specification without a specifier, which is impossible. Therefore, an agent must exist that specifies existence.
This is a classic kalam contingency argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( لاحتاجَ وجودُهُ إلى مُخصص ) أي : فاعل يخترعُه ، وإنَّما عبرنا بالمخصص للتنبيه على سبب احتياج السكون إلى الفاعل ؛ وهو أنَّ ذاتَهُ لمَّا قبلَتِ الوجود والعدم، فلو وقع الوجود بدلاً مِنَ العدمِ مِنْ غيرِ فاعل .. لكانَ تخصيصاً مِنْ غيرِ مُخصص ، وهو ظاهرُ الاستحالة ، فلا بدَّ إذاً مِنْ فاعل يُخصِّصُ وجود السكون بالحدوث بدلاً عن العدمِ الذي يزاحم الوجودَ ؛ لقَبولِ الذاتِ له على حسَبِ قَبولِها الوجود .
His saying: 'its existence would need a mukhassis (specifier)' — meaning: an agent that invents it. And we only used the term 'mukhassis' to indicate the reason for the need of rest for an agent; and that is because its essence, since it admits existence and non-existence, if existence occurred instead of non-existence without an agent, it would be a specification without a specifier, and that is manifestly impossible. So necessarily there must be an agent that specifies the existence of rest as temporal instead of non-existence that rivals existence, because the essence admits that (non-existence) in the same way it admits existence.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage presents a key kalam argument for the necessity of a creator. It begins by explaining the phrase 'its existence would need a mukhassis' — mukhassis means an agent (fa'il) that invents the body. The author uses the term mukhassis to highlight why bodies (here specifically 'rest' as a state) require an agent: the essence of the body equally admits (qabila) both existence and non-existence. It is not inherently existent. If existence were to occur instead of non-existence without an agent, that would be a 'specification without a specifier' (takhsis min ghayr mukhassis), which is obviously impossible. Therefore, there must be an agent that specifies the existence of the body (here 'rest') as a temporal occurrence, rather than non-existence, because the essence is equally receptive to both. This argument is a classic formulation of the contingency argument in Ash'ari kalam.

Key Terms
مخصص
فاعل
الوجود
العدم
الاستحالة
الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mukhassis argument only applies to rest (sukun), not to bodies in general.
Correction: Sanusi uses 'rest' as an example, but the reasoning applies to any body or state that is contingent. The term 'mukhassis' is used to highlight the general principle.

Checkpoint
What does the author mean by 'mukhassis' in this passage?
A) A cause that destroys the body
B) An agent that invents or creates the body
C) A property inherent in the body
D) The body itself

Key Takeaway
Every contingent thing requires a mukhassis (specifier) to bring it into existence, otherwise its existence would be an unjustified preference for existence over non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own existence, do you truly feel that you are contingent and that you owe your existence entirely to a Specifier? What would change if you lived with that awareness every moment?
?
The argument says that without a specifier, existence would be an unjustified preference. In your life, what are some areas where you act as if your existence is self-justified, without reference to Allah? How can you correct that starting today?
?
The essence of the body admits non-existence just as much as existence. Do you genuinely believe that at any moment you could cease to exist if the Specifier did not sustain you? How would that change your priorities this week?

All parts are temporal, none can be eternal
Sanusi explains the phrase 'from His throne to His carpet' and the rational status of temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the phrase 'from His throne to His carpet' means every part of the world is temporal, and that this truth is established by proof, not self-evident.

Core Idea
No part of the world can be eternal; this is proven, not assumed.

In this passage, Sanusi clarifies an evocative phrase and a key rational principle. He explains that when we say 'everything is temporal', we mean every part without exception, and that this truth is established by proof, not obvious at first glance. This helps us distinguish between necessary and theoretical knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the two distinct parts: first about the phrase, second about rational conceivability.
The passage is concise; each sentence carries a precise meaning.
2
Understand the phrase 'from His throne to His carpet'
Identify what 'throne' and 'carpet' refer to. The commentator explains they mean the highest and lowest ends, generalizing temporality to all parts.
3
Grasp the condition for not conceiving eternity
Note that the statement 'not rationally conceivable' is conditioned on the proof being clear. The passage distinguishes between before and after the proof becomes evident.
This prevents the misunderstanding that it is self-evident.
4
Reflect on daruri vs. nazari
Consider why the commentator insists that if it were self-evident, the knowledge would be necessary (daruri), but it is actually theoretical (nazari). This highlights the role of proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( من عرشِهِ إِلى فَرْشِهِ ) ، أي : مِنْ طرفِهِ الأعلى إلى طرفه الأسفل ، والمراد : تعميم الحدوثِ لجميع أجزائه .
قوله : ( لا يُتصوَّرُ في العقلِ أنْ يكونَ شيءٌ منه قديماً ) يعني : لا يُدرَكُ في العقلِ قدمُهُ بعد ما اتَّضحَتْ ملازمته للحركة والسكون اللذين قام البرهان على استحالة وجودِهما في الأزلِ ، وليس المعنى : أنَّهُ لا يُتصوَّرُ في العقلِ قدمُهُ بدءاً قبلَ اتّضاحِ هذا البرهان ، وإلا كانَتْ معرفة حدوثِهِ ضروريةً لا نظريةً ، وهو باطل باتفاق العقلاء
His saying: 'from His throne to His carpet' — meaning: from its highest end to its lowest end, and the intent is to generalize temporality (al-huduth) to all its parts.
His saying: 'It is not rationally conceivable that any part of it be eternal' — meaning: its eternity is not rationally perceived after its inseparability from motion and rest, which the proof has established the impossibility of their existence in pre-eternity, has become clear. And the meaning is not that its eternity is not rationally conceivable at the outset before this proof becomes clear, for then knowledge of its temporality would be necessary (daruriyya) rather than theoretical (nazariyya), and that is false by consensus of rational people.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage contains two final clarifications. First, the commentator explains the phrase 'from His throne to His carpet': 'throne' (arsh) refers to the highest part, 'carpet' (farsh) to the lowest part, meaning from top to bottom. The intent is to generalize the property of being temporal (huduth) to every part of the world, not just some. Second, the commentator explains the statement 'It is not rationally conceivable that any part of it be eternal.' He clarifies that this inconceivability is after the proof has been established—namely, that every part is inseparable from motion and rest, and both cannot exist in pre-eternity. It does not mean that one cannot initially conceive of a part being eternal before the proof is understood; if that were the case, knowledge of the world's temporality would be necessary (daruri) rather than theoretical (nazari), which contradicts the consensus (ittifaq) that it is a matter of reasoned theology. This distinction guards against the misunderstanding that the world's temporality is self-evident without proof.

Key Terms
العرش
الفرش
الحدوث
القدم
ضرورية
نظرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the impossibility of an eternal part is immediately self-evident without proof.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that this inconceivability is only after the proof (the inseparability from motion and rest) has been established. Before that, it is conceivable, meaning the knowledge is theoretical, not necessary.

Checkpoint
What does the phrase 'from His throne to His carpet' mean in this context?
A) A literal description of God's throne
B) From the highest to the lowest part, meaning the entire body
C) From beginning to end of time
D) From East to West

Key Takeaway
Every part of the world is temporal, and this truth is established by proof, not by immediate self-evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the world around you, do you actually feel the weight of its temporality, or do you just accept it as a fact without it shaking your heart? How can you train yourself to see every moment as a sign of the Creator's eternity?
?
The lesson says knowledge of the world's temporality is theoretical, not self-evident. Have you ever truly followed the proof from motion and rest to its conclusion, or do you rely on assumptions? What would change if you internalized that the world had a beginning?
?
If every part of the universe—from the highest throne to the lowest carpet—is temporal, then nothing in creation deserves your ultimate attachment. Is there anything in your life that you treat as if it were eternal, holding on to it as if it will never end? What would it look like to release that grip today?

The True Knower Sees Only Allah
Sanusi defines the true knower and explains the contingency of all creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the true knower of Allah and why creation is not considered independent existence.

Core Idea
The true knower sees only Allah because creation has no independent causal power.

This passage from Sanusi explains the spiritual station of the true knower (al-‘ārif). It builds on the principle that nothing in existence is truly independent except Allah. The passage clarifies how created things, being Allah's actions, are entirely dependent and thus not considered real existence alongside Him.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's words.
Notice that the passage ends mid-sentence at the page break.
2
Identify the definition of the knower
Find the phrase that defines the true knower (al-‘ārif). What does he see perpetually?
3
Understand the reason given
The passage gives a reason: 'since there is nothing in existence except Allah, His attributes, and His actions.' Reflect on what this means for creation.
The footnote marker (2) attributes the saying to al-Ghazali.
4
Grasp the implications of creation's status
Sanusi explains that created things (as Allah's actions) possess no power and their existence in affecting things is like non-existence. Understand why this leads to creation being counted only as needy.
5
Note the contrast with the heedless
The passage contrasts the knower with one who is heedless of Allah and looks at creation. How does this contrast reinforce the definition?
The sentence is cut off, but the contrast is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولذلك قيلَ : العارفُ مَنْ لا يرى إلا الله تعالى على الدوام ؛ إذْ ليس في الوجودِ إلا الله تعالى وصفاته وأفعاله (۲) ، وكلُّ ما في الوجود سواه تعالى وسوى صفاته فهو فعله ، وهي لا تَملِكُ ولا تُوجِدُ ، ووجودها في التأثير في الأشياء كعدمها ، فلا يُعَدُّ ما هذا سبيلُهُ معَ اللهِ تعالى مِنَ الوجودِ إلا على حكم الافتقار إليه ، والتعلُّق بإمدادِهِ وإبقائه ، ومَنْ غفلَ عنِ اللهِ تعالى ، ونظر إلى خَلْقٍ مِنَ المخلوقاتِ
And therefore it is said: The knower (al-‘ārif) is one who sees only Allah Almighty perpetually, since there is nothing in existence except Allah Almighty, His attributes, and His actions (2). And everything in existence other than Him, glorified, and other than His attributes, is His action, and they possess nothing and bring nothing into existence, and their existence in affecting things is like their non-existence. So what is like this, when considered with Allah Almighty, is not counted as existence except on the basis of neediness to Him and attachment to His supply and preservation. And whoever is heedless of Allah Almighty and looks at a created being among created things...
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage defines the true knower of Allah (al-‘ārif) and explains the nature of created existence. The author begins: 'And therefore it is said: The knower is one who sees only Allah Almighty perpetually.' The reason given is that 'there is nothing in existence except Allah Almighty, His attributes, and His actions.' Note the footnote marker (2), which here attributes the saying to al-Ghazali. The author then clarifies: everything other than Allah and His attributes is His action (fi‘l). These actions—meaning created things—possess no power and do not bring anything into existence; their existence in terms of causing effects is like their non-existence. Therefore, such things are not counted as genuine existence alongside Allah; they are merely needy and dependent on His supply and preservation. The passage ends mid-sentence with a contrast: whoever is heedless of Allah and looks at creation (as independent) is described, but the sentence is cut off at the page break. The core teaching is the absolute dependence of all creation on Allah and the annihilation of seeing anything as independently existent.

Key Terms
عارف
وجود
صفات
أفعال
افتقار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'nothing exists except Allah' implies pantheism (that everything is Allah).
Correction: The passage means creation has no independent existence or causal power; it is entirely dependent on Allah, not that creation is identical to Allah.

Checkpoint

C) Because Allah forces him to ignore creation
D) Because the knower only meditates on Allah's names

Key Takeaway
The true knower recognizes that all creation is entirely dependent on Allah and has no independent causal power, thus seeing only Allah perpetually.

?

Allah's Existence
beginner
5 min
Proof of the contingency of celestial bodies and all bodies of the world
Sanusi proves the temporal origination of the world through the observed alternation of celestial bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi argues for the temporal origination of all bodies in the world through the observable phenomena of appearing and setting.

Core Idea
The alternating cycles of celestial bodies prove that the universe is created, not eternal.

Sanusi presents a powerful argument based on direct observation: the sun, moon, and stars alternate between appearing and setting. If these properties were eternal, they could never change—but we see them change constantly. This forces the conclusion that the bodies themselves must have a beginning. In this lesson, we follow his logical steps and see how he extends the proof from a few celestial bodies to the entire physical world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the logical connectors like 'if...then' and 'because'.
2
Identify the key observation
Sanusi starts from the observation that celestial bodies appear and set. Ask: Why does this observation lead to temporal origination?
3
Follow the logical chain
Trace the reasoning: from properties (appearing/setting) to bodies, then from three bodies to all bodies. Note the use of 'similarity' (tamāthul).
4
Reflect on the Quranic citation
Read the verse from Surat al-An'am and consider why Sanusi cites it. How does it support the argument and the value of rational proof?
The verse connects this proof to Prophet Abraham, highlighting the honor of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأنَّ الظُّهور إذا كان قديماً استحال عدمه ، كيف وقد شُوهدَ عدمُهُ فيما لا يزالُ بمشاهدة ضدّهِ وهو الأفولُ ؟! وكونه أيضاً في الأزلِ آفلاً مستحيل ، وإلا لَمَا قَبلَ أُقولُهُ الزوالَ لقدمِهِ ، ومشاهدة ظهوره فيما لا يزال يدلُّ على حدوثِ ذلكَ الأُفول . وإذا استحال ثبوتُ كلّ واحدٍ مِنَ الظُّهورِ والأُفول في الأزلِ لقبولهما التبديل.. وجبَ حدوثهما ؛ إذْ لا واسطة بين القدم والحدوثِ ، وإذا وجبَ حدوثهما وجبَ حدوث تلك الأجرام المتصفةِ بهما ؛ لاستحالةِ عُرُوها عنهما ، وإذا وجبَ الحدوث لتلك الأجرام الثلاثة وجبَ الحدوث لجميعِ أجرام العالم ؛ للتماثل ، ولأنَّ هذا الدليل بعينه ينهضُ في الجميعِ حسَبَ نهوضِهِ في الأجرامِ الثلاثةِ فخرج بهذا الدليل : وجوب الحدوثِ لجميع العالم ؛ وهو كلُّ سوى الله تعالى ، وذلك هو المطلوب ولظهور هذهِ الحجَّةِ وحصولِ العلمِ عنها بحدوث العالم ضرورةً . . قال تعالى : ﴿ وَتِلْكَ حُجَّتُنَا ءَاتَيْنَهَا إِبْرَاهِيمَ عَلَى قَوْمِهِ ، ثم قال تعالى : نَرْفَعُ دَرَجَتِ مَّن نَّشَاءُ ﴾ [الأنعام : ٤٨٣ أي : بمعرفة الحقِّ ببرهانه - لا بالتقليد - نرفع درجاتٍ مَنْ نشاءُ دنيا وأخرى ، والله تعالى أعلم ، وهذهِ الآيةُ ممَّا يدلُّ على شرفِ علم الكلام ، والبحثِ على أدلَّةِ العقائدِ ، وخِيَّةِ التقليدِ فيها ، وبالله سبحانه التوفيق
Because if [the property of] appearing was eternal, its non-existence would be impossible. How could it be [otherwise] when its non-existence is observed repeatedly by observing its opposite, which is setting?! Also, its being eternally in a state of setting is impossible; otherwise it would not accept removal due to its eternity. And the observation of its appearing repeatedly indicates the temporal origination of that setting. And when the existence of each one of appearing and setting in eternity is impossible because they accept change, their temporal origination is necessary; for there is no middle ground between eternity and temporal origination. And when their temporal origination is necessary, then the temporal origination of those bodies characterized by them is necessary, because it is impossible for them to be devoid of them. And when the temporal origination of those three bodies is necessary, then the temporal origination of all bodies of the world is necessary, due to similarity, and because this same proof stands for all just as it stands for the three bodies. Thus by this proof is derived: the necessity of temporal origination for all the world; and it is everything other than God, the Exalted. And that is what was sought. And because this argument is evident and knowledge of the temporal origination of the world is obtained from it necessarily... He the Exalted said: {And that is Our argument which We gave Abraham against his people} [Al-An'am 6:83] then He said: {We raise degrees whom We will}, meaning: by knowledge of the truth through its proof – not by blind following – We raise degrees whom We will in this world and the next. And God the Exalted knows best. And this verse indicates the honor of the science of kalam, and research into proofs of beliefs, and the condemnation of blind acceptance in them. And with God, the Exalted, is all success.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage presents a complete proof for the contingency (temporal origination) of the world, specifically focusing on celestial bodies. The author (Sanusi) argues that observable celestial phenomena—namely, the changing states of appearing (ẓuhūr) and setting (afūl)—demonstrate that these properties cannot be eternal. If either property were eternal, its opposite could never be observed, but we repeatedly observe the alternation. Therefore, both properties are temporally originated (ḥādith). Since it is impossible for bodies to exist without being characterized by either appearing or setting, the bodies themselves must also be temporally originated. The argument then extends from three celestial bodies (presumably the sun, moon, and stars) to all bodies of the world on the basis of similarity (tamāthul) and the same reasoning applying universally. The conclusion is that the entire world (everything other than God) is temporally originated. The passage then cites Quran 6:83 as testimony that this proof was given to Abraham, highlighting the value of rational demonstration over blind acceptance (taqlīd) and affirming the noble status of the science of kalam (ʿilm al-kalām).

Key Terms
ظهور
أفول
حدوث
قدم
تماثل
تقليد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof only applies to the sun and moon, not to other bodies.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly extends the argument to all bodies of the world using similarity (tamāthul) and the universality of the reasoning.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi conclude that the three celestial bodies must be temporally originated?
A) Because they are made of fire (nār)
B) Because their properties (appearing and setting) are temporally originated
C) Because they have shadows
D) Because the Quran says so without reason

Key Takeaway
The observable alternation of celestial properties (appearing and setting) forces the conclusion that the entire physical universe is temporally originated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just seen a rational proof that everything around you is temporally originated—nothing is eternal except Allah. In what specific area of your life have you been treating something created (a person, a possession, a status) as if it will never end? What will you do today to shift your heart’s reliance from that to the Eternal One?
?
This argument forces you to admit that the world is not self-sufficient. What is one thing you have been depending on—perhaps your health, wealth, or relationships—that this proof reveals is contingent and perishable? How will you surrender that dependency to Allah today?
?
Sanusi condemns blind acceptance (taqlīd) and honors those who seek proof. In which belief of yours—about God, the world, or yourself—are you simply following what you were taught without having thought through the evidence? What one step will you take this week to move from inherited belief to personal certainty?

The Contingent World Requires a Creator
Sanusi proves that the contingent world must have an eternal Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the contingent world cannot cause its own existence and must have a free agent Creator.

Core Idea
The world's existence after non-existence proves it needs a cause — and that cause must be a free choosing Creator.

Having established that the world is temporally originated (ḥādith), Imam Sanusi now proves that it requires a Creator. The argument goes beyond simple causality: it uses the principle that without a free agent, the world would remain in non-existence forever because all possibilities are equal. In the following passage, Sanusi presents a rigorous proof that only a voluntary Creator can bring the world into existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Let the flow of the argument sink in before analysing.
2
Identify the main premise
Locate where Sanusi states that the world is temporally originated (ḥādith). This is the foundation of the entire proof.
The premise is built on the previous lesson.
3
Understand the equality argument
Notice how Sanusi argues that times, measures, and attributes are equal relative to the world's essence. Without a choosing agent, no particular state can be preferred.
This is the core of the proof against self-causation.
4
Follow the two views on existence vs. non-existence
Sanusi presents two scholarly views: either existence and non-existence are equal, or non-existence is more appropriate. Both lead to the same conclusion: a cause is needed.
The second view (non-existence is prior) is actually stronger for the argument.
5
Grasp the final conclusion
The argument ends with the assertion that preferring an outweighed existence (marjūḥ) without a cause is even more impossible than preferring an equal one. Therefore, a Creator is absolutely necessary.
This is a powerful rational proof for divine existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في إقامة البرهان القاطع على وجوده تعالى و بيان احتياج العالم اليه جل وعز وإذا كان العالم حادثاً بعدما تقرَّر عدمُهُ فلا بدَّ لَهُ مِنْ مُحدِث ؛ إذ لا يُتصوَّرُ في العقل انتقاله مِنَ العدم الذي كانَ عليهِ إلى الوجودِ الطارئ بلا سبب ، ولولا الفاعل المختار لوجودِهِ فيما شاءَ مِنَ الأزمانِ على ما شاءَ مِنَ المقادير والصفاتِ . . لكانَ يجب أن يبقى على ما كانَ عليهِ مِنَ العدم أبد الآبادِ ؛ لاستواء المقادير والصفاتِ والأزمانِ بالنسبة إلى ذاتِهِ وأما الوجود والعدمُ : فقيل : هما بالنسبة إلى ذاتِهِ سواء ، فيستحيل أن يترجّحَ الوجود المساوي الطارئ بلا سبب ، وقيل : العدمُ السابق أولى بهِ ؛ لأصالته فيه ، وعدم افتقاره إلى سببٍ ، وإذا كان ترجيح أحد المتساويين بلا سبب محالاً .. فاستحالة ترجيح الوجود المرجوح بالنسبة إلى العالم على هذا بلا سبب أحرى
In establishing the definitive proof for His existence, the Exalted, and clarifying the world's need of Him, the Mighty and Majestic. And if the world is temporally originated after its non-existence was established, then it must have an originator; for it is not rationally conceivable that it moves from the non-existence it was in to the accidental existence without a cause. And were it not for the free agent for its existence in whatever time He wills, in whatever measures and attributes He wills, it would have to remain upon what it was in of non-existence forever; because measures, attributes, and times are equal with respect to its essence. As for existence and non-existence: it is said that they are equal with respect to its essence. So it is impossible that the equal, accidental existence be preferred without a cause. And it is said that prior non-existence is more appropriate for it, due to its being original in it, and its lack of need for a cause. And if preferring one of two equals without a cause is impossible, then the impossibility of preferring the outweighed existence—relative to the world—without a cause is all the more certain.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage begins a new section titled 'Establishing the definitive proof for His existence and clarifying the world's need for Him.' The argument builds on the preceding conclusion that the world is temporally originated (ḥādith). Sanusi states that since the world came into existence after non-existence, it must have a creator (muḥdith) because it is rationally inconceivable for something to transition from non-existence to existence without a cause. He then presents a subsidiary argument: if there were no free agent (fāʿil mukhtār) who brings the world into existence at a specific time and with specific quantities and qualities, then the world would remain in non-existence forever. This is because all possible times, measures, and attributes are equal with respect to the world's essence—none has a preferential property that would necessitate existence. He then considers the relative status of existence and non-existence: some say they are equal for the world, so existence cannot be chosen without a cause; others say non-existence is more appropriate because it is original and requires no cause. The conclusion is that if choosing one of two equals without a cause is impossible, then choosing the outweighed (marjūḥ) existence—which is even less likely than equal—without a cause is even more impossible. Therefore, a cause (i.e., God's free choice) is absolutely necessary to bring the world into existence.

محدث
فاعل مختار
ترجيح
متساويان
مرجوح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument for a Creator here relies solely on the world's temporal origination.
Correction: Sanusi also uses the principle of indifference (equality of possibilities) to rule out self-causation, showing that even if the world is temporal, it cannot choose its own existence without a free agent.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The world's existence after non-existence necessarily requires a free Creator because no part of the world can prefer itself by eliminating its original non-existence.

This lesson proves that without Allah's free choice, you would be nothing forever. Do you truly feel that dependency when you sin, or do you assume you can 'borrow' existence to disobey? What does it mean for your next decision that your existence is sustained by His preference?

Proof of the Creator from the World's Origination
Sanusi presents the proof for God's existence from the world's origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam argument that the originated world necessarily has a creator, based on the principle that a transition from non-existence to existence requires a cause and that without a free agent, the world would have no reason to exist.

Core Idea
The world's existence demands a cause because it came from nothing and had no reason to exist on its own.

Having established that the world is originated (ḥādith), Sanusi now builds the decisive proof for the existence of its Creator. This argument is the cornerstone of Islamic theology: if the world came into being after not existing, it must have an originator, and the impossibility of a preference without cause seals the case. Let us follow the logical steps carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the logical flow: premise, hypothetical, discussion of equivalents, conclusion.
2
Identify the core premise
The argument begins: since the world is originated (after non-existence), it must have an originator. Why? Because the transition from non-existence to existence cannot occur without a cause.
3
Understand the principle of equivalents
The passage explains that without a free agent, the world would remain non-existent, because all times, measures, and attributes are equivalent with respect to its essence. There is no inherent reason for existence over non-existence.
This is a key kalam principle: preference without a cause is impossible.
4
See the conclusion
Since preferring one equivalent over another without cause is impossible, and existence and non-existence are equivalent (or non-existence is more fitting), the world's existence requires an external cause – the Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام على الإلهيات
باب في إقامة البرهان القاطع على وجوده تعالى وبيان احتياج العالم إليه جل وعز
وإذا كان العالم حادثاً بعدما تقرَّر عدمُهُ فلا بدَّ لَهُ مِنْ مُحدِث؛ إذ لا يُتصوَّرُ في العقل انتقاله مِنَ العدم الذي كانَ عليهِ إلى الوجودِ الطارئ بلا سبب، ولولا الفاعل المختار لوجودِهِ فيما شاءَ مِنَ الأزمانِ على ما شاءَ مِنَ المقادير والصفاتِ . . لكانَ يجب أن يبقى على ما كانَ عليهِ مِنَ العدم أبد الآبادِ؛ لاستواء المقادير والصفاتِ والأزمانِ بالنسبة إلى ذاتِهِ
وأما الوجود والعدمُ: فقيل: هما بالنسبة إلى ذاتِهِ سواء، فيستحيل أن يترجّحَ الوجود المساوي الطارئ بلا سبب، وقيل: العدمُ السابق أولى بهِ؛ لأصالته فيه، وعدم افتقاره إلى سببٍ، وإذا كان ترجيح أحد المتساويين بلا سبب محالاً.. فاستحالة ترجيح الوجود المرجوح بالنسبة إلى العالم على هذا بلا سبب أحرى
Discussion on the Divine Matters
Chapter on establishing the decisive proof for His existence, exalted is He, and explaining the world's need for Him, glorious and mighty.
And since the world is originated after its non-existence was established, it must have an originator; for it is inconceivable in the intellect that it transition from the non-existence which it was in to the contingent existence without a cause. And were it not for the free agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār) for its existence in whatever times He willed, in whatever measures and attributes He willed, it would have to remain in the state of non-existence forever, because measures, attributes, and times are equivalent with respect to its essence.
As for existence and non-existence: it is said that they are equivalent with respect to its essence, so it is impossible for the equivalent contingent existence to be preferred without a cause. And it is said that prior non-existence is more appropriate for it, due to its originality in it and its lack of need for a cause. And if the preference of one of two equivalents without a cause is impossible, then the impossibility of preferring the inferior existence over the world without a cause is even more fitting.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the existence of God based on the world's origination. It begins by stating the premise that the world is originated (ḥādith), i.e., it came into existence after not existing. The argument proceeds: since it transitioned from non-existence to existence, it must have an originator (muḥdith) because such a transition cannot occur without a cause. The author then considers a hypothetical: if there were no free agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār) to bring it into existence at a specific time and with specific measures and attributes, the world would have remained in non-existence forever. This is because all times, measures, and attributes are equivalent with respect to its essence—there is no inherent reason for it to exist at one time rather than another or with one set of attributes rather than another. Next, the passage examines the relationship between existence and non-existence. Two opinions are cited: some say existence and non-existence are equivalent in relation to the world's essence, so it is impossible for existence to be preferred without a cause. Others say non-existence is more fitting because it is original and does not need a cause. The conclusion is that if preferring one of two equivalents without a cause is impossible (a well-established principle), then preferring the inferior (i.e., existence over non-existence, which is contrary to the original state) without a cause is even more impossible. Thus, the world's existence requires a cause, which is the Creator.

Key Terms
حادث (originated)
محدث (originator)
فاعل مختار (free agent)
ترجيح بلا سبب (preference without a cause)
متساويان (equivalents)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes that everything that exists must have a cause, similar to the scientific notion of causality.
Correction: The argument is specifically based on the world being originated (coming into existence after non-existence). It does not claim that every existing thing needs a cause; rather, that which transitions from non-existence to existence requires a cause. The principle of 'preference of equivalents without a cause' is also unique to kalam.

Checkpoint

B) Because it is an accident
C) Because it transitioned from non-existence to existence, which requires a cause
D) Because it has a beginning in time

Key Takeaway
The originated world necessarily has a creator because its existence from non-existence requires a cause, and without a free agent, it would have no reason to exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are an originated being—you came from non-existence into existence by the will of Allah. If He had not willed to create you at this specific time and with these specific attributes, you would be nothing. Does your daily life reflect that gratitude and dependence? What is one habit today that denies this total dependence?
?
The passage argues that it is impossible for something to prefer its own existence without a cause. Have you ever acted as if your existence or your next breath is automatic? What would it mean for you to wake up tomorrow remembering that your very existence is a gift that must be renewed every moment?
?
The proof demands a single cause for all that exists. If you truly internalized that everything in your life—your health, wealth, relationships, and even your next thought—is directly caused by Allah, what worry would you abandon? What sin would become unthinkable? What act of worship would become urgent?

The Disagreement on Necessary vs. Theoretical Knowledge of the Creator
Sanusi reports the disagreement on whether knowledge of the creator is necessary or theoretical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate among Sunni theologians about whether knowledge of the creator is immediate (necessary) or requires reflection (theoretical).

Core Idea
Knowledge of the creator may be ingrained in human nature or acquired through reasoning.

After establishing that the world originated, theologians debated how we know it has an originator. Al-Rāzī argued this knowledge is innate, even in children and animals, while al-Juwaynī maintained it requires rational reflection. This passage presents both positions with their evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Read the passage and note the two views: necessary vs. theoretical. Pay attention to who held which view.
Al-Rāzī is on the necessary side; al-Juwaynī on the theoretical side.
2
Examine al-Rāzī's evidence
Consider the examples of the child and the donkey. How do they support the claim that the need for a cause is ingrained?
These examples appeal to instinctive reactions.
3
Examine al-Juwaynī's counterargument
Focus on his reasoning: an event's occurrence at a specific time does not itself entail causality. Understand why this undermines the necessary view.
He uses the principle that the relation of existence to any time is equivalent.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this disagreement matters for theology and epistemology. Does it change how we argue for God's existence?
The debate touches on the role of reason vs. innate disposition in religious knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لما ثبت حدوث العالم بما سبق من البرهان .. استدل به في هذا الباب على وجود محدثه
وقد اختلف أئمتنا بعد العلم بحدوث العالم: هل العلم بمحدثه ضروري لا يفتقر إلى نظر، أو هو نظري ؟
فذهب الفخر إلى الأول، وزاد: أن العلم بافتقار كل حادث إلى محدث مركوز حتى في طباع الصبيان؛ فإنك لو لطمت وجه صبي من حيث لا يراك وقلت له: إنه حدثت فيك هذه اللطمة من غير فعل فاعل .. لقطع بكذب ذلك، ولهذا تجده يبحث عن عين الضارب له، بل زاد: أنه مركوز في فطرة البهائم؛ فإن الحمار إذا سمع صوت الخشبة فزع، وما ذاك إلا لأنه قد تقرر في طبعه أن حدوث صوت الخشبة من غير فاعل له محال.
وذهب إمام الحرمين وجماعة إلى الثاني؛ أنه نظري؛ وذلك أن حدوث الحادث في زمان مخصوص لا يقتضي من حيث ذاته ذلك الزمان المخصوص، بل نسبة وجوده إلى ذلك الزمان وإلى غيره سواء؛ بدليل أن أمثاله وجدت قبل ذلك الزمان وبعده، وكذا وجوده
When the origination of the world was established by the preceding proof, he used it in this chapter to prove the existence of its originator.
And our imams differed after knowledge of the world's origination: is the knowledge of its originator necessary (ḍarūrī) not requiring reflection, or is it theoretical (naẓarī)?
Al-Fakhr (al-Rāzī) adopted the first view and added: that the knowledge of every originated thing's need for an originator is ingrained even in the nature of children; for if you slapped a child's face from where he does not see you and said to him: 'This slap occurred in you without the act of an agent,' he would absolutely consider that false, and for this reason you find him searching for the eye of the one who struck him. He even added that it is ingrained in the nature of animals; for when a donkey hears the sound of a stick, it is startled, and that is only because it is established in its nature that the occurrence of the sound of a stick without an agent is impossible.
And Imām al-Ḥaramayn (al-Juwaynī) and a group adopted the second view: that it is theoretical; because the occurrence of an event at a particular time does not, by its very essence, necessitate that particular time, but rather the relation of its existence to that time and to other times is equivalent; as evidence that similar events occurred before and after that time, and similarly its existence...
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage records a theological disagreement among Sunni scholars regarding the epistemic status of the knowledge that the world has a creator. After establishing the world's origination (ḥudūth) by proof, the question arises: is the knowledge that the world has an originator (muḥdith) necessary (ḍarūrī) — i.e., immediate and not requiring reflection — or theoretical (naẓarī) — requiring reflection and reasoning?
The author (Sanusi) reports that al-Fakhr al-Rāzī held the first view, and he even added that this knowledge is ingrained (markūz) in human nature (fiṭra). He gives an example: if you slap a child's face from behind so he doesn't see you, and then claim it happened without an agent, the child would know it's false and would search for the person who hit him. This shows the child has an innate awareness that every event needs a cause. Al-Rāzī further extends this to animals: a donkey startled by the sound of a stick perceives that the sound must have a cause, indicating this knowledge is instinctual.
On the other hand, Imām al-Ḥaramayn (al-Juwaynī) and a group (jamāʿa) held that this knowledge is theoretical. Their reasoning is that the occurrence of an event at a specific time does not, by its own essence, imply that specific time; the relationship of the event's existence to any time is equivalent. Since similar events occur at different times, there is no inherent link to a particular time that would make the need for a cause immediately apparent without reflection. Thus, a person must reflect (naẓar) to infer the necessity of a cause.

Key Terms
ضروري
نظري
مركوز
فطرة
إمام الحرمين
فخر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this debate questions the existence of God.
Correction: The disagreement is only about the epistemological pathway, not the fact of existence.

Checkpoint

D) The argument from design

Key Takeaway
The debate between necessary and theoretical knowledge of the creator hinges on whether the mind instinctively grasps causality or must infer it through reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Al-Razi says even a donkey instinctively knows that a sound needs a cause. When you experience a blessing or a trial today, do you instinctively turn to the One who caused it, or do you need to remind yourself through reasoning?

The child searches for the one who struck him. When was the last time you searched for God's hand in an event that happened to you – not just acknowledged it intellectually, but truly sought Him with urgency?

Al-Fakhr's Argument: Instinctive Knowledge of the Creator
Al-Fakhr argues knowledge of a creator is instinctive through child and animal examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Fakhr uses innate human and animal behaviour to argue that belief in a creator is instinctive.

Core Idea
Even a child instinctively knows every event needs a doer.

Al-Fakhr al-Razi offers a powerful argument that knowledge of the need for a creator is not acquired through reasoning but is ingrained in our very nature. He presents two vivid examples: a child's reaction to an unseen slap and a donkey's startle at a sound. These examples show that the intellect naturally recognizes causation. Let's examine his argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two examples al-Fakhr uses.
The passage is dense with meaning despite its brevity.
2
Analyze the child example
Consider why the child instinctively knows the slap must have a doer. What does this reveal about human nature?
3
Analyze the donkey example
The donkey's startle is not rational but instinctive. How does this strengthen the argument?
Animals lack rational faculties, yet show this awareness.
4
Understand the conclusion
Al-Fakhr concludes that knowledge of a creator is necessary (ḍarūrī) and instinctive. Reflect on the implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزادَ : أنَّ العلم بافتقارِ كلِّ حادث إلى محدث مركوز حتى في طباع الصبيان؛ فَإِنَّكَ لو لطمْتَ وَجْهَ صبيٌّ مِـنْ حيثُ لا يراك وقلت له : إِنَّهُ حدثَتْ فيكَ هذه اللطمةُ مِنْ غير فعل فاعل .. لقطعَ بكذب ذلكَ، ولهذا تجده يبحث عن عين الضاربِ له، بل زادَ : أَنَّهُ مركوز في فطرةِ البهائم؛ فإنَّ الحمار إذا سمع صوت الخشبة فزع، وما ذاكَ إلا لأنَّهُ قد تقرَّرَ في طبعِهِ أَنَّ حدوث صوتِ الخشبة مِنْ غيرِ فاعل له محال
And he added: that knowledge of the dependence of every temporal event upon a creator is ingrained even in the nature of children; for if you slap a child's face from where he does not see you and say to him: 'This slap came about in you without the act of a doer,' he would certainly deem that false, and for this reason you find him searching for the very person who struck him. He further added: that it is ingrained in the instinct of animals; for the donkey, when it hears the sound of wood, is startled, and that is only because it is established in its nature that the occurrence of the sound of wood without a doer is impossible.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage provides al-Fakhr's evidence that knowledge of the need for a creator is necessary (ḍarūrī) and not theoretical. He makes two points: (1) Even a child, when slapped without seeing the slapper, instinctively knows that the slap must have come from an agent. If told it happened by itself, the child rejects it and looks for the slapper. (2) This instinct is also present in animals, e.g., a donkey startled by the sound of a piece of wood. The donkey's fright arises from its innate recognition that the sound must have a cause. Thus, al-Fakhr argues, this knowledge is not gained through reasoning but is ingrained in human and animal nature.

Key Terms
مركوز
طباع
فطرة
بهائم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Animals lack any sense of causation, so their behaviour is irrelevant to the argument.
Correction: Al-Fakhr uses the animal example to show that even without rationality, instinctive recognition of cause exists, strengthening the argument that it is innate.

D) A scholar and a philosopher.

Key Takeaway
Al-Fakhr's argument shows that belief in a creator is not theoretical but instinctive, rooted in our very nature and even shared with animals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What would change in your life if you lived with that instinctive certainty every moment of every day?
?
When you commit a sin, do you instinctively feel that you have offended your Creator, just as the child instinctively knows there is someone to blame? Or do you rationalize it away, deadening your fitra? What steps must you take today to awaken that innate awareness so that it becomes your first response?
?
The donkey is startled by a sound it cannot see, yet it knows a doer exists. When you hear or read about trials in the world—pandemics, wars, natural disasters—do you react with the same instinctive recognition of Allah’s hand, or do you explain them away by secondary causes? How can you train yourself to immediately see His decree in everything that happens?
