The Need for a Specifier: Temporal Origination Requires a Specifier
Argument that temporal origination requires a specifier for time, quantity, and quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why temporal beings require a specifier and how the argument from specification leads to the existence of God.

Core Idea
An event's specific features cannot be explained by its own essence alone.

This lesson introduces one of the most powerful arguments in classical Islamic theology for the necessity of a creator. The argument focuses on the specific characteristics of temporal events—their time, quantity, and quality—and demonstrates that these cannot be determined by the event itself. By following the reasoning step by step, you will see why a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ) is logically required.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem
Recognize that an event has specific features (time, quantity, quality) that are not determined by its own essence. Read the first part of the passage and note how the argument begins with the impossibility of self-determination.
The passage starts by stating that the essence does not necessitate a specific time.
2
Examine the three aspects
Identify the three aspects mentioned: time, quantity, and quality. For each, the passage argues that the relation to any specific value is equal to its opposite, as evidenced by similar events occurring at different times, sizes, etc.
3
Recognize the equality of possibilities
Understand that without a specifier, the event would have to choose among equal possibilities by itself, leading to a contradiction. The passage states that an event cannot be both equal and preponderating by its own essence.
This step requires you to grasp the logical principle behind the argument.
4
Conclude: necessity of a specifier
The passage concludes that a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ) is unavoidable. Reflect on why this specifier is understood to be God in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذلكَ أَنَّ حدوث الحادثِ في زمانٍ مخصوص لا يقتضي مِنْ حيثُ ذاتُهُ ذلكَ الزمانَ المخصوص، بل نسبةُ وجودِهِ إلى ذلك الزمانِ وإلى غيرِهِ سواء؛ بدليل أنَّ أمثالَهُ وُجِدَتْ قبلَ ذلكَ الزمان وبعده، وكذا وجوده على مقدار مخصوص دون غيرِهِ مِنْ سائر المقادير، وعلى صفة مخصوصةٍ دونَ غيرِها مِنْ سائر الصفاتِ لا يقتضي بشيءٍ مِنْ ذلكَ، فلا بدَّ إذاً مِنْ مُخصص، وإلا لزم اجتماع متنافيين؛ وهو أنْ يكونَ أحد الأمرين المتساويين مساوياً لذاته راجحاً لذاته، وهو محال.
And that is that the occurrence of an event at a specific time does not necessitate that specific time from its own essence, but rather its existence's relation to that time and to another is equal; as evidenced by the fact that similar events have been found before that time and after it. Likewise, its existence in a specific quantity to the exclusion of others, and in a specific quality to the exclusion of other qualities, does not necessitate any of that. Therefore, a specifier is necessary, otherwise the coexistence of two contradictories would follow: that one of two equal things is, by its own essence, equal and preponderating, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage presents the argument from specification (ikhtiṣāṣ), a core argument in kalām for the necessity of a creator. The reasoning proceeds stepwise: (1) An event occurs at a particular time. But that specific time is not entailed by the event's own essence, because similar events have occurred at other times. (2) Similarly, the event has a specific quantity and quality, but its essence alone does not determine these specifics. (3) If the essence of the event were sufficient to explain all its specifics, then it would have to produce them necessarily, but we observe that similar events have different specifics. (4) Therefore, there must be an external specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ) that chooses these specifics over others. (5) Without such a specifier, we would have to say that the event, in its essence, is both equal (to all possibilities) and preponderating (to one specific), which is a contradiction. Thus a specifier is unavoidable — this specifier is God.

Key Terms
مخصص
زمان
مقدار
صفة
متساويين
راجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about temporal causality—that everything needs a cause in time.
Correction: The argument is not about temporal causality but about why this specific event has these specific features—it is an argument from contingency of attributes.

Checkpoint
What would be the consequence if an event's own essence determined its specific time, quantity, and quality?
A) The event would be eternal.
B) All similar events would have the same features.
C) It would be impossible for similar events to differ.
D) The event would not exist.

Key Takeaway
Without a specifier, a temporal event would involve a logical contradiction: it would be both equal to all possibilities and preponderating toward one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every detail of your life — your time, abilities, and circumstances — requires a deliberate Specifier, then are you living with the awareness that your existence is not random? How will you acknowledge the One who specified your exact life today?
?
The argument shows that without a specifier, contradiction arises. In what areas of your faith or daily choices are you trying to maintain a position that is logically inconsistent with tawhid? What step will you take to resolve it?
?
When you consider that your own existence could have been different in time, place, and form, does that humble you? How will you respond practically to the Creator who chose these specifics for you?

Why an Event Needs a Doer
Sanusi argues that a temporal event needs a doer and distinguishes necessary non-existence from contingent events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a temporal event requires a doer and why prior non-existence does not require a cause.

Core Idea
A temporal event cannot bring itself into existence; it needs an external agent.

This lesson examines a foundational kalam argument: every temporal event (ḥādith) requires a doer (fāʿil). Sanusi presents refined reasoning that even if existence and non-existence are equally possible for a thing, the occurrence of existence demands an agent. He also clarifies that prior non-existence—being necessary and not an event—requires no cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Understand the two views
The passage presents two positions on the relation of existence and non-existence to the essence: equality or prior non-existence being more appropriate. Identify both.
3
Grasp the contradiction argument
If existence were to arise by itself, it would be both equal (to non-existence) and preponderant, which is a contradiction. This forces the need for a doer.
This is the core logical move.
4
Notice the clarification about non-existence
Sanusi explains that the pre-eternal non-existence of the world is necessary, not contingent, so it needs no cause. Future non-existence also needs no cause because it is not a new occurrence.
Only the temporal origination requires a doer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا يحتاجُ أيضاً الحادثُ إلى فاعل إن نظرنا ذاتَهُ إلى مطلقِ الوجود والعدم، سواء قلنا : إنَّهما سواء بالنسبة إلى ذاتِهِ؛ وهو مذهب المحققين، أو قلنا : إنَّ العدمَ السابق أولى بذاتِهِ؛ لأصالتِهِ فيه، وعدم افتقارِهِ إلى سببٍ أمَّا على الأوَّلِ: فلأنَّهُ لو طرأ الوجود المساوي للعدم بنفسِهِ بلا فاعل .. لكان الوجود مساوياً راجحاً ! وهو تناقض، فتعيَّنَ أنْ يكونَ طريانه لفاعل اختار اختراعَهُ، وإنَّما لم يحتج العدمُ السابق إلى فاعل لأنَّه ليس بحادث؛ أمَّا العدمُ للعالم في الأزلِ: فليس بممكن الإمكانَ الخاص حتى يحتاج إلى فاعل، بل هو واجب، وأما فيما لا يزالُ: فلا يحتاج إلى فاعل أيضاً وإنْ كانَ ممكناً؛ لأنَّه لم يطرأ بعد أن لم يكن.
And likewise, the temporal event also needs a doer if we consider its essence relative to absolute existence and nonexistence, whether we say that they are equal in relation to its essence — which is the doctrine of the verifiers — or we say that prior nonexistence is more appropriate to its essence due to its primacy in it and its not needing a cause. As for the first [view]: because if existence equal to nonexistence were to come about by itself without a doer, existence would be both equal and preponderating, which is a contradiction. Thus it is necessary that its coming about be from a doer who chose to bring it into existence. Prior nonexistence did not need a doer because it is not an event. As for the nonexistence of the world in pre-eternity, it is not possible with specific possibility that it would need a doer; rather, it is necessary. And as for what is in the future, it also does not need a doer, even though it is possible, because it has not come about after not having been.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage argues that a temporal event (ḥādith) requires a doer (fāʿil) because its existence is not inherent. Two positions are considered: (1) The verifiers' view that existence and non-existence are equally possible relative to the thing's essence. (2) The view that prior non-existence is more appropriate due to its primacy. Either way, if existence were to arise without a cause, then existence would be both equal (to nonexistence) and preponderant (choosing itself) — a contradiction. Therefore, a doer must choose to bring existence. The passage then clarifies that the pre-eternal non-existence of the world is necessary, not contingent, and so needs no cause; future non-existence after the world's end also needs no cause because it is not a new occurrence. Thus only the temporal origination requires a doer.

Key Terms
فاعل
الوجود والعدم
طريان
إمكان خاص
واجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that non-existence also needs a cause because it is a state that exists.
Correction: The author explains that only the coming to be of existence (the event) requires a cause; non-existence itself, being prior and not a new occurrence, does not need a cause.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does prior non-existence (of the world in pre-eternity) not require a cause?
A) Because it is necessary, not possible with specific possibility.
B) Because it is not a temporal event.
C) Because it is eternal.
D) Both A and B.

Key Takeaway
A temporal event requires an external doer because it cannot bring itself from non-existence to existence without contradiction; non-existence, being necessary or not a new occurrence, needs no cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your very existence is a temporal event that came from non-existence. Have you truly internalized that you are not self-sufficient but depend entirely on a Doer who chose to bring you into being? How does this change the way you live today?
?
The proof shows that equal possibility alone cannot produce existence without a Preponderator. In your daily choices, when you decide between alternatives, do you recognize that your ability to choose is itself a gift from the One who preponderates? What would it mean to thank Him for every choice you make?
?
Sanusi emphasizes that non-existence in pre-eternity is necessary, not contingent—but you were once non-existent and then brought into existence. Does this stark reminder of your origin stir awe and dependence on the One who created you from nothing? What must change in your heart so that this awareness is not just intellectual but transforms your relationship with your Creator?

The Proof of the Specifier (Mukhassis)
Sanusi proves the necessity of a specifier for the world's quantities and qualities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's proof that the world's specific attributes require an external specifier, and why its absence leads to a logical contradiction.

Core Idea
Without a specifier, equal possibilities would collapse into contradiction.

Sanusi now presents a concise but powerful argument for the existence of a specifier (mukhassis). He reasons that the world's particular quantities and qualities cannot be explained by its own essence, because the essence alone does not necessitate any one specific measure or attribute. Therefore, an external factor must decide which possibility is realized. This argument is a cornerstone of kalam proof for a necessary being.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the full translation. Let the logical flow of the argument settle in your mind before analysing it.
Notice the compact style of kalam reasoning.
2
Identify key terms
Find the terms مخصص (mukhassis), متنافيين (mutanāfi'ayn), مساوٍ (musāwin), and راجح (rājiḥ) in the text. Understand their roles in the argument.
These terms are essential for the proof.
3
Map the logical structure
Break down the argument: premise (essence does not entail specifics) → conclusion (need for specifier) → reductio (without specifier, contradiction follows). Identify each part in the Arabic.
The contradiction is that one of two equal things would be both equal and preponderant by essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذاته الاختصاص على مقدار مخصوص دون غيرِهِ مِنْ سائر المقادير ، وعلى صفة مخصوصةٍ دونَ غيرِها مِنْ سائر الصفاتِ . . لا يقتضي بشيءٍ مِنْ ذلكَ ، فلا بدَّ إذاً مِنْ مُخصص ، وإلا لزم اجتماع متنافيين ؛ وهو أنْ يكونَ أحد الأمرين المتساويين مساوياً لذاته راجحاً لذاته ، وهو محال .
Its essence of specification to a specific quantity to the exclusion of other quantities, and to a specific quality to the exclusion of other qualities... does not entail any of that by itself, so there must necessarily be a specifier, otherwise the coexistence of two contradictories would follow: namely, that one of two equal things is equal to its essence and preponderant by its essence, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of a mukhassis (specifier) for the world. Sanusi argues that the world has specific quantities and qualities that are not inherent in its essence, because the essence alone does not necessitate any particular measure or quality. Therefore, an external specifier is required. If there were no specifier, then two equal possibilities would both be realized or one would be preferred without a preference, leading to a contradiction (mutanāfi'ayn). This contradiction is that one of two equal things would be both equal to its essence and preponderant by its essence, which is impossible.

Key Terms
مُخصِّص (mukhassis)
متنافيين (mutanāfi'ayn)
مساوٍ (musāwin)
راجح (rājiḥ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The specifier is only needed for compound things, not for simple essences.
Correction: Sanusi's argument shows that even a simple essence with specific attributes requires a specifier, because the essence alone does not necessitate those attributes.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what would happen if there were no specifier for the world's specific quantities and qualities?
A) The world would not exist
B) Two contradictories would coexist
C) The world would have all possible quantities simultaneously
D) The world's essence would be simple

Key Takeaway
The world's specific attributes cannot be explained by its own essence, so an external specifier is necessary; otherwise, a logical contradiction follows.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the world's specific qualities cannot be explained by its own essence, what does that say about the meaning and purpose of your own specific qualities—your body, mind, circumstances? Are you living as if your existence is self-sufficient, or do you acknowledge the Specifier in every detail of your life? What would change if you truly believed that every detail of your being is chosen by Allah?
?
The argument shows that without a specifier, contradiction follows. In what areas of your life are you trying to be self-sufficient, ignoring that your very existence requires constant specification from Allah? What would it look like to surrender the illusion of self-sufficiency and rely on the Mukhassis in your decisions today?
?
Sanusi says that equal possibilities cannot prefer themselves. When you face choices in life, do you act as if your own preferences are self-caused, or do you recognize that even your inclinations are specified by Allah? How would remembering this change the way you make decisions and seek His guidance?

The Proof of an Agent for Temporal Events (First View)
Sanusi argues for the necessity of an agent using the equality premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why temporal events require an agent, based on the equality of existence and non-existence.

Core Idea
If existence and non-existence are equal, existence cannot occur by itself—it needs an agent.

Before delving into theological proofs, Sanusi establishes a fundamental principle: temporal events cannot bring themselves into existence. This lesson unpacks his key argument using the premise that existence and non-existence are equal in relation to a temporal event's essence. You will see how he concludes that an external agent must choose to create.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the two views presented at the beginning.
2
Identify the two views
Sanusi presents two positions: existence and non-existence are equal, or prior non-existence is more appropriate. Understand both.
3
Focus on the first view argument
Understand why if existence occurred by itself, it would be both equal and preponderant—a contradiction. Hence an agent is needed.
This is the core logical proof.
4
Understand why prior non-existence is excluded
Prior non-existence does not need an agent because it is not temporal. Sanusi distinguishes pre-eternal non-existence (necessary) and perpetual non-existence (not newly occurring).
5
Note the terms 'equal' and 'occurring'
Sanusi uses 'the equal occurring' (al-musawi al-tari') to refer to existence that is both equal to non-existence and new. This distinguishes it from prior non-existence.
These terms will be important in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا يحتاجُ أيضاً الحادثُ إلى فاعل إن نظرنا ذاتَهُ إلى مطلقِ الوجود والعدم ، سواء قلنا : إنَّهما سواء بالنسبة إلى ذاتِهِ ؛ وهو مذهب المحققين ، أو قلنا : إنَّ العدمَ السابق أولى بذاتِهِ ؛ لأصالتِهِ فيه ، وعدم افتقارِهِ إلى سببٍ أمَّا على الأوَّلِ : فلأنَّهُ لو طراً الوجود المساوي للعدم بنفسِهِ بلا فاعل .. لكان الوجود مساوياً راجحاً ! وهو تناقض ، فتعيَّنَ أنْ يكونَ طريانه لفاعل اختار اختراعَهُ ، وإنَّما لم يحتج العدمُ السابق إلى فاعل لأنَّه ليس بحادث ؛ أمَّا العدمُ للعالم في الأزلِ : فليس بممكن الإمكانَ الخاص حتى يحتاج إلى فاعل ، بل هو واجب ، وأما فيما لا يزالُ : فلا يحتاج إلى فاعل أيضاً وإنْ كانَ ممكناً ؛ لأنَّه لم يطرأ بعد أن لم يكن . ولهذا احترزنا عن العدم - وإنْ كانَ مساوياً للوجودِ في هذا القول - بقولنا : ( المساوي الطارى ) أي : بخلافِ العدم السابقِ ؛ فإِنَّهُ وإنْ كانَ مساوياً فليس بطاري ؛ أي : مُتجدِّدٍ بعد أنْ لم يكن ، فلا يحتاج إلى فاعل ، وهذا بناءً على أنَّ سبب الاحتياج إلى الفاعل الإمكانُ معَ الحدوثِ ، أو الإمكانُ بشرط الحدوثِ ، أو الحدوث فقط ، وأما إن قلنا : سبب الاحتياج إلى فاعل الإمكان فقط .. فيأتي عليه أن يحتاج العدمُ السابق في استمراره فيما لا يزالُ إلى الفاعل ، ولا يلزمُ مِنِ احتياجه إليهِ أنْ يكونَ موجوداً ، بل بمعنى : أَنَّهُ وقد قادر على إزالتِهِ ؛ إذْ لو شاءَ أن يجعل في مكانِهِ الوجود لفعل سلكنا في هذه العقيدةِ مِنَ الأقوال الأربعة في سبب الاحتياج إلى الفاعل ما يحتمل القولين الأولين ؛ وهما : كونُ الحدوثِ لا بدَّ وأنْ يُضم إلى الإمكانِ ؛ إِمَّا على أنَّهُ شرطٌ ، أو جزء ، فقولنا : ( المساوي ( إشارة إلى الإمكان ، وقولنا : ( الطارى ( إشارة إلى الحدوث ، هذا كله على القولِ الأَوَّلِ ؛ وهو أنَّ الوجود والعدم بالنسبة إلى العالم سواء وأما على القول الثاني : فأظهرُ في احتياج الوجودِ إلى الفاعل؛
And likewise the temporal event also needs an agent if we consider its essence in relation to absolute existence and non-existence, whether we say: they are equal with respect to its essence—which is the doctrine of the verifiers—or we say: prior non-existence is more appropriate to its essence due to its originality in it and its lack of need for a cause. As for the first: because if existence, which is equal to non-existence, were to occur by itself without an agent, then existence would be both equal and preponderant—a contradiction. So it is determined that its occurrence is due to an agent that chose to create it. Prior non-existence does not need an agent because it is not temporal; as for the non-existence of the world in pre-eternity, it is not possible with specific possibility such that it needs an agent, but rather it is necessary. And as for what will be in perpetuity, it does not need an agent either, even though it is possible, because it does not occur after not having been. And for this reason we were cautious about non-existence—even if it is equal to existence in this statement—by our saying: 'the equal occurring' meaning in contrast to prior non-existence; for it, even if equal, is not occurring, i.e., new after not having been, so it does not need an agent. This is based on the fact that the cause of need for an agent is possibility with temporality, or possibility with the condition of temporality, or temporality alone. And if we say: the cause of need for an agent is possibility alone, then it would follow that prior non-existence in its continuation in perpetuity needs the agent, and its need for it does not entail that it exists, but rather in the sense that He is able to remove it; for if He willed to put existence in its place, He would do so. We follow in this creed, among the four opinions on the cause of need for an agent, what accommodates the first two opinions: that temporality must be combined with possibility, either as a condition or as a part. So our saying 'the equal' refers to possibility, and our saying 'the occurring' refers to temporality. All this is according to the first opinion, which is that existence and non-existence are equal with respect to the world. As for the second opinion, it is more apparent in the need of existence for the agent.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of a fa'il (agent) for temporal events (hawadith). Sanusi considers two positions: (1) existence and non-existence are equal with respect to the essence of a temporal event (view of the verifiers), and (2) prior non-existence is more appropriate to the essence. On the first view, if existence occurred by itself without an agent, it would be both equal and preponderant—a contradiction. Therefore, an agent must have chosen to bring it into existence. Prior non-existence does not need an agent because it is not a temporal event; it is either necessary (in pre-eternity) or not newly occurring (in perpetuity). Sanusi then explains that the cause of need for an agent can be: (a) possibility combined with temporality, (b) possibility with the condition of temporality, or (c) temporality alone. If the cause were possibility alone, then prior non-existence would also need an agent for its continuation, but that need is only in the sense that the agent can remove it. Sanusi adopts a position that combines temporality and possibility, as indicated by the terms 'the equal' (al-musawi) for possibility and 'the occurring' (al-tari') for temporality. The argument is more straightforward on the second view (that non-existence is prior), because then the occurrence of existence clearly needs an external cause.

Key Terms
فاعل (fa'il)
حادث (hādith)
وجود (wujūd)
عدم ('adam)
مساوٍ (musāwin)
طارئ (tāri')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only the first view (equality) leads to the need for an agent.
Correction: Sanusi shows that both views lead to the same conclusion; the second view makes the need even more apparent.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why does prior non-existence (al-'adam al-sābiq) not need an agent?
A) Because it is eternal and necessary
B) Because it is not a temporal event (ḥādith)
C) Because it is equal to existence
D) Because it is possible

Key Takeaway
Temporal events cannot arise without a voluntary agent because their existence is not self-explanatory; an external chooser is required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You exist right now as a temporal being. According to this lesson, your existence and non-existence are equal in themselves. If you were to claim you exist by your own power, would that not be a contradiction? What does this force you to admit about your dependence on a Creator?
?
Consider the moments of your life when you felt self-sufficient. Does the logic of this passage expose that feeling as an illusion? How could you live today with the constant awareness that you are not your own source?
?
This lesson proves that temporal events—including your own birth and every moment of your existence—require a voluntary agent who chose to bring them about. If that is true, then your very existence is a gift from God. How should that change the way you use your time today?

The Cause of Need for an Agent and the Second View
Sanusi explains the cause of need for an agent and the second view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different opinions on why something needs an agent and how the second view (priority of non-existence) makes the proof more apparent.

Core Idea
The need for an agent arises from possibility combined with temporality, not from possibility alone.

Sanusi now delves deeper into the metaphysical principle underlying the need for an agent. He presents multiple opinions on the cause of need and explains why he adopts a position combining possibility and temporality. This lesson also covers the second view, which argues that prior non-existence is more fitting to the essence, making the need for an agent even more obvious.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage focusing on opinions
Read the Arabic and translation, paying attention to the list of opinions on the cause of need for an agent.
Sanusi mentions three or four opinions.
2
Understand the four opinions
The cause can be: possibility with temporality, possibility conditioned by temporality, temporality alone, or possibility alone. Understand each.
Possibility alone leads to a different conclusion.
3
See why possibility alone is rejected
If cause were possibility alone, prior non-existence would need an agent for its continuation. Sanusi explains this is only in the sense that the agent can remove it.
4
Map terms 'equal' and 'occurring'
Sanusi says 'the equal' refers to possibility and 'the occurring' refers to temporality, showing his adoption of the first two opinions.
This connects back to the previous lesson.
5
Note the second view
The second view (non-existence more appropriate) makes the need of existence for an agent more apparent because existence is clearly new.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا يحتاجُ أيضاً الحادثُ إلى فاعل إن نظرنا ذاتَهُ إلى مطلقِ الوجود والعدم ، سواء قلنا : إنَّهما سواء بالنسبة إلى ذاتِهِ ؛ وهو مذهب المحققين ، أو قلنا : إنَّ العدمَ السابق أولى بذاتِهِ ؛ لأصالتِهِ فيه ، وعدم افتقارِهِ إلى سببٍ أمَّا على الأوَّلِ : فلأنَّهُ لو طراً الوجود المساوي للعدم بنفسِهِ بلا فاعل .. لكان الوجود مساوياً راجحاً ! وهو تناقض ، فتعيَّنَ أنْ يكونَ طريانه لفاعل اختار اختراعَهُ ، وإنَّما لم يحتج العدمُ السابق إلى فاعل لأنَّه ليس بحادث ؛ أمَّا العدمُ للعالم في الأزلِ : فليس بممكن الإمكانَ الخاص حتى يحتاج إلى فاعل ، بل هو واجب ، وأما فيما لا يزالُ : فلا يحتاج إلى فاعل أيضاً وإنْ كانَ ممكناً ؛ لأنَّه لم يطرأ بعد أن لم يكن . ولهذا احترزنا عن العدم - وإنْ كانَ مساوياً للوجودِ في هذا القول - بقولنا : ( المساوي الطارى ) أي : بخلافِ العدم السابقِ ؛ فإِنَّهُ وإنْ كانَ مساوياً فليس بطاري ؛ أي : مُتجدِّدٍ بعد أنْ لم يكن ، فلا يحتاج إلى فاعل ، وهذا بناءً على أنَّ سبب الاحتياج إلى الفاعل الإمكانُ معَ الحدوثِ ، أو الإمكانُ بشرط الحدوثِ ، أو الحدوث فقط ، وأما إن قلنا : سبب الاحتياج إلى فاعل الإمكان فقط .. فيأتي عليه أن يحتاج العدمُ السابق في استمراره فيما لا يزالُ إلى الفاعل ، ولا يلزمُ مِنِ احتياجه إليهِ أنْ يكونَ موجوداً ، بل بمعنى : أَنَّهُ وقد قادر على إزالتِهِ ؛ إذْ لو شاءَ أن يجعل في مكانِهِ الوجود لفعل سلكنا في هذه العقيدةِ مِنَ الأقوال الأربعة في سبب الاحتياج إلى الفاعل ما يحتمل القولين الأولين ؛ وهما : كونُ الحدوثِ لا بدَّ وأنْ يُضم إلى الإمكانِ ؛ إِمَّا على أنَّهُ شرطٌ ، أو جزء ، فقولنا : ( المساوي ( إشارة إلى الإمكان ، وقولنا : ( الطارى ( إشارة إلى الحدوث ، هذا كله على القولِ الأَوَّلِ ؛ وهو أنَّ الوجود والعدم بالنسبة إلى العالم سواء وأما على القول الثاني : فأظهرُ في احتياج الوجودِ إلى الفاعل؛
And likewise the temporal event also needs an agent if we consider its essence in relation to absolute existence and non-existence, whether we say: they are equal with respect to its essence—which is the doctrine of the verifiers—or we say: prior non-existence is more appropriate to its essence due to its originality in it and its lack of need for a cause. As for the first: because if existence, which is equal to non-existence, were to occur by itself without an agent, then existence would be both equal and preponderant—a contradiction. So it is determined that its occurrence is due to an agent that chose to create it. Prior non-existence does not need an agent because it is not temporal; as for the non-existence of the world in pre-eternity, it is not possible with specific possibility such that it needs an agent, but rather it is necessary. And as for what will be in perpetuity, it does not need an agent either, even though it is possible, because it does not occur after not having been. And for this reason we were cautious about non-existence—even if it is equal to existence in this statement—by our saying: 'the equal occurring' meaning in contrast to prior non-existence; for it, even if equal, is not occurring, i.e., new after not having been, so it does not need an agent. This is based on the fact that the cause of need for an agent is possibility with temporality, or possibility with the condition of temporality, or temporality alone. And if we say: the cause of need for an agent is possibility alone, then it would follow that prior non-existence in its continuation in perpetuity needs the agent, and its need for it does not entail that it exists, but rather in the sense that He is able to remove it; for if He willed to put existence in its place, He would do so. We follow in this creed, among the four opinions on the cause of need for an agent, what accommodates the first two opinions: that temporality must be combined with possibility, either as a condition or as a part. So our saying 'the equal' refers to possibility, and our saying 'the occurring' refers to temporality. All this is according to the first opinion, which is that existence and non-existence are equal with respect to the world. As for the second opinion, it is more apparent in the need of existence for the agent.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of a fa'il (agent) for temporal events (hawadith). Sanusi considers two positions: (1) existence and non-existence are equal with respect to the essence of a temporal event (view of the verifiers), and (2) prior non-existence is more appropriate to the essence. On the first view, if existence occurred by itself without an agent, it would be both equal and preponderant—a contradiction. Therefore, an agent must have chosen to bring it into existence. Prior non-existence does not need an agent because it is not a temporal event; it is either necessary (in pre-eternity) or not newly occurring (in perpetuity). Sanusi then explains that the cause of need for an agent can be: (a) possibility combined with temporality, (b) possibility with the condition of temporality, or (c) temporality alone. If the cause were possibility alone, then prior non-existence would also need an agent for its continuation, but that need is only in the sense that the agent can remove it. Sanusi adopts a position that combines temporality and possibility, as indicated by the terms 'the equal' (al-musawi) for possibility and 'the occurring' (al-tari') for temporality. The argument is more straightforward on the second view (that non-existence is prior), because then the occurrence of existence clearly needs an external cause.

Key Terms
إمكان (imkān)
حدوث (hudūth)
فاعل (fa'il)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility alone is sufficient reason for needing an agent.
Correction: Sanusi shows that prior non-existence is possible but does not need an agent because it is not temporal; the cause must include temporality.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the cause of need for an agent?
A) Possibility alone
B) Temporality alone
C) Possibility combined with temporality
D) Existence alone

Key Takeaway
The need for an agent is not due to mere possibility but due to possibility together with temporal newness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your existence depends on possibility combined with temporality—meaning you are contingent and newly arrived. Do you truly feel that dependence every moment, or do you live as if you are self-sufficient? What one concrete habit can you start today to remind yourself of your constant need for Allah's power to keep you in existence?
?
The passage teaches that prior non-existence—the nothing you came from—does not need an agent to sustain it; it is mere absence. But your existence is a gift that could be removed at any instant. How attached are you to worldly things that will vanish? Write one thing you can detach from this week as an acknowledgment of your temporality.
?
If the need for an agent is not just for creation but for continued existence (since you are always possible/huduth?), examine your prayer: do you ask Allah to sustain you in faith and life as if without Him you would cease to be? Or do you pray as if He merely initiated your existence? Resolve now to say 'Ya Hayyu Ya Qayyum, bi rahmatika astaghith' daily to actualize that dependence.

Clarifying the Equal Occurrence and the Four Opinions on the Need for an Agent
Sanusi clarifies the term 'equal occurrence' and explains the four scholarly opinions on why things need a creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the term 'al-musāwī al-ṭārī' (the equal occurrence), why it excludes prior non-existence from needing an agent, and the four scholarly opinions on the cause of the need for a creator, including which opinions the author adopts.

Core Idea
The need for an agent arises from new events, not eternal non-existence.

Sanusi introduces a precise term, 'al-musāwī al-ṭārī' (the equal occurrence), to avoid a common mistake: thinking that prior non-existence also requires a cause. He then grounds this term in the broader debate about what exactly makes a possible thing need a creator. This passage is essential for understanding the foundational principle that only new occurrences—not eternal absence—demand an agent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly and then the translation. Let the author's own wording establish the concept before interpretation.
Notice how the author uses the term 'equal occurrence' to modify the earlier statement about equality.
2
Understand why prior non-existence does not need an agent
Reflect on the distinction between prior non-existence (al-ʿadam al-sābiq) and new occurrence (ṭārī). The author argues that what is eternal—even if equal—does not need a cause because it has always been absent.
This clarifies that only events that become new after not being require a creator.
3
Identify the four opinions and the author's choice
List the four opinions on the cause of need for an agent: possibility with occurrence, possibility conditioned by occurrence, occurrence alone, and possibility alone. Notice that the author follows the first two, which combine possibility and occurrence.
The author’s terminology ('musāwī' for possibility, 'ṭārī' for occurrence) reflects his alignment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهذا احترزنا عن العدم - وإن كان مساوياً للوجود في هذا القول - بقولنا : ( المساوي الطارى ) أي : بخلاف العدم السابق ؛ فإنه وإن كان مساوياً فليس بطاري ؛ أي : متجدد بعد أن لم يكن ، فلا يحتاج إلى فاعل ، وهذا بناءً على أن سبب الاحتياج إلى الفاعل الإمكان مع الحدوث ، أو الإمكان بشرط الحدوث ، أو الحدوث فقط ، وأما إن قلنا : سبب الاحتياج إلى فاعل الإمكان فقط .. فيأتي عليه أن يحتاج العدم السابق في استمراره فيما لا يزال إلى الفاعل ، ولا يلزم من احتياجه إليه أن يكون موجوداً ، بل بمعنى : أنه قادر على إزالته ؛ إذ لو شاء أن يجعل في مكانه الوجود لفعل سلكنا في هذه العقيدة من الأقوال الأربعة في سبب الاحتياج إلى الفاعل ما يحتمل القولين الأولين ؛ وهما : كون الحدوث لا بد وأن يضم إلى الإمكان ؛ إما على أنه شرط ، أو جزء ، فقولنا : ( المساوي ) إشارة إلى الإمكان ، وقولنا : ( الطارى ) إشارة إلى الحدوث ، هذا كله على القول الأول ؛ وهو أن الوجود والعدم بالنسبة إلى العالم سواء
And for this reason we guarded against non-existence—even though it is equal to existence in this statement—by our saying: 'the equal occurrence'; i.e., unlike prior non-existence; for it, even though it is equal, is not occurring, i.e., it is not something new after not having been, so it does not need an agent. And this is based on the fact that the cause of the need for an agent is possibility with occurrence, or possibility conditioned by occurrence, or occurrence alone. And if we say: the cause of the need for an agent is only possibility, then it follows that prior non-existence in its continuation forever would need an agent, and its need for Him does not necessitate that it exists, but rather meaning that He is able to remove it; for if He willed to put existence in its place, He would do it. We have followed in this creed, from among the four opinions on the cause of the need for an agent, what accommodates the first two opinions: they are that occurrence must be joined to possibility, either as a condition or as a part. So our saying 'the equal' indicates possibility, and our saying 'the occurring' indicates occurrence. All this is according to the first opinion, which is that existence and non-existence are equal with respect to the world.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'al-musāwī al-ṭārī' (the equal occurrence) as a way to avoid the mistake of thinking that prior non-existence also needs a cause. The author explains that the need for an agent (fāʿil) is based on one of four opinions: (1) possibility with occurrence, (2) possibility conditioned by occurrence, (3) occurrence alone, or (4) possibility alone. He states that the first two opinions are more appropriate because they combine occurrence (ḥudūth) with possibility (imkān). The author then shows how his terminology reflects these opinions: 'musāwī' indicates possibility (since possibility is equal in all possible things) and 'ṭārī' indicates occurrence, because it is the new event that requires a cause. The passage concludes by noting that this discussion assumes the first major opinion, that existence and non-existence are equal in relation to the world (i.e., the world is equally possible to exist or not exist).

Correction: Only new occurrences (ṭārī) need an agent; prior non-existence has always been absent and is not a new event.

Proof from the Second Opinion: The Less Likely Needs an Agent
Sanusi presents the second opinion's proof for a creator via the impossibility of the less likely occurring without an agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument from the second opinion that the improbable occurrence of the world requires a creator, and why the alternative leads to a contradiction.

Core Idea
If the less likely occurs without a cause, it would be both less likely and more likely — an impossibility.

In this passage, Sanusi presents the second opinion's proof for a creator. He uses a reductio ad absurdum: if the world's less likely existence (marjūḥ) came to be without an agent, then it would have to be both marjūḥ and rājiḥ simultaneously — a contradiction. He then strengthens the argument with an a fortiori reasoning, concluding that the world must have an originator.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and Translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage slowly, then the literal translation. Notice the key terms like المرجوح and الراجح.
The passage uses concise logical language.
2
Understand the Key Concepts
Identify al-marjūḥ (less likely) and al-rājiḥ (more likely). The world's non-existence was more likely before it existed; its existence is less likely.
These are relative probabilities, not absolute.
3
Analyze the Reductio Argument
Follow the logic: if the less likely occurs without an agent, then it would be both less likely and more likely in itself — a contradiction. This proves an agent is necessary.
This is a form of tarjīḥ bilā murajjiḥ.
4
See the A Fortiori and Conclusion
Sanusi uses a fortiori: if an equally likely event needs an agent, the less likely does even more so. Conclude that the world must have an originator.
The conclusion applies regardless of which opinion you hold.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما على القول الثاني : فأظهر في احتياج الوجود إلى الفاعل؛ لأنه لا يعقل أن يطرأ الوجود المرجوح ويزول العدم الراجح بلا فاعل ، وإلا كان المرجوح في نفسه راجحاً في نفسه ! وهو تناقض لا يعقل ، وإذا استبانت استحالة طروء المساوي بلا فاعل . . فأحرى طروه المرجوح . فقد عرفت بهذا : وجوب إسناد وجود العالم الذي اتضح حدوثه إلى موجد على كل تقدير
And as for the second opinion, it is more evident in the need of existence for an agent, because it is inconceivable that the less likely existence occurs and the more likely non-existence disappears without an agent; otherwise the less likely in itself would be more likely in itself! That is an inconceivable contradiction. And if the impossibility of the occurrence of the equal without an agent is established, then the occurrence of the less likely is even more so. So you have known by this: the necessity of attributing the existence of the world, whose temporality is clear, to an originator in any case.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage presents the argument according to the second opinion, which holds that the mere fact of occurrence (al-ḥudūth) is the cause of the need for an agent. The argument proceeds as follows: The existence of the world after non-existence is less likely (marjūḥ) compared to its prior non-existence, which is more likely (rājiḥ). If the less likely existence occurs without an agent, then the less likely would be more likely in itself, which is a contradiction. This is a form of the argument from 'preponderance without a preponderator' (tarjīḥ bilā murajjiḥ). The author then uses a fortiori reasoning: if the equal occurrence (i.e., a new event that is equally possible) requires an agent, then the less likely event requires one even more so. The conclusion is that the world must have an originator (mūjid) on any account.

Key Terms
المرجوح
الراجح
طروء
تناقض
موجد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument is circular or assumes the need for a cause without proof.
Correction: It is a reductio ad absurdum: assuming no cause leads to a contradiction, which proves a cause must exist.

Checkpoint
What would be the self-contradictory consequence if the less likely (al-marjūḥ) occurred without an agent?
A) It would be necessary
B) It would be equal in probability
C) It would be both less likely and more likely in itself
D) It would be impossible to conceive

Key Takeaway
The less likely occurrence of the world cannot happen without an agent, otherwise a logical contradiction arises, proving a creator is necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the less likely cannot occur without an agent, then what about the moments in your life that you attribute to 'luck' or 'coincidence'? Are you truly convinced that nothing happens without the permission and power of Allah, or do you still have areas where you assume chance? What would it take for you to see every event—especially the seemingly random ones—as divinely ordained?
?
The proof forces you to accept that your own existence, being less likely than non-existence (since you were not always here), requires a Creator. Do you live with that awareness daily, or do you take your own existence for granted? When you wake up, do you consciously attribute your continued being to Allah, or do you act as if it is self-explanatory?
?
Sanusi says that if the less likely occurred without a cause, it would be both less likely and more likely—a contradiction. Are there contradictions you tolerate in your faith, such as claiming tawakkul while worrying about provision? How can you align your actions with the logical certainty that every outcome comes from Allah alone?

Is Knowledge of God Necessary or Theoretical?
Sanusi presents and refutes alternative views on the epistemological status of knowledge of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge of God's existence is classified as theoretical (naẓarī) yet so close to self-evident that even children grasp it, and why it is not present in animals.

Core Idea
Knowledge of God is theoretical but extremely near, not necessary nor instinctive.

Sanusi tackles a foundational question in theology: how do we know that God exists? Some scholars claimed this knowledge is necessary (ḍarūrī), meaning it requires no reasoning. Others said it is theoretical (naẓarī), requiring reflection. Sanusi argues for the latter but emphasizes that the reasoning is so simple that even children can do it. He then refutes the idea that animals share this knowledge, showing that their responses are based on association, not reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice how Sanusi sets up a disagreement and then gives his own position.
The passage has a clear structure: statement of disagreement, truth, refutation of Rāzī's necessity claim, refutation of animal reasoning.
2
Identify the key positions
Find where Sanusi states that the truth is theoretical (naẓarī) but near, and where he explains why Fakhr al-Rāzī mistakenly thought it is necessary.
The phrase 'due to its extreme nearness' is key.
3
Analyze the refutation of animal reasoning
Read the part about the animal and the sound of the stick. Understand Sanusi's argument that the animal's fear is not from rational deduction but from associative learning.
This example shows the difference between true rational knowledge and conditioned response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهل العلم بذلك ضروري أو نظري ؟ فيه خلاف ، والحق أنه نظري ، إلا أنه بنظر قريب لا يفتقر إلى كبير تأمل ، ولذلك أدركه مطلق المميزين من الصبيان ، ولقربه جدا توهم الفخر أنه ضروري ، وأما ما زاد من أنه من طباع البهائم فلا يخفى فساده ، وما ذكره من فزع البهيمة من صوت الخشبة فليس لإدراكها أن الحادث لا بد له من محدث ، بل لإدراك خيالي نشأ عن إلفها مقارنة ذلك الصوت لألمها لما تكرر تألمها بالضرب بالخشبة ، وإلا فإسناد الأنظار الفكرية والاستدلالات بالمقدمة العقلية للحيوانات البهيمية . . من أعجب ما يسمع ! والله تعالى أعلم ، وبه التوفيق
And is knowledge of that necessary or theoretical? There is disagreement, and the truth is that it is theoretical, except that it is a near reasoning that does not require much reflection. Because of that, all discriminating people, even children, grasp it. Due to its extreme nearness, Fakhr (al-Rāzī) mistakenly thought it is necessary. As for his addition that it is from the nature of animals, its corruption is not hidden. And what he mentioned about the animal's fright from the sound of a stick is not because it perceives that the event must have a cause, but because of an imaginary perception arising from its habituation to the association of that sound with pain, due to the repeated occurrence of its being pained by being hit with a stick. Otherwise, attributing intellectual reflections and rational deductions to brute animals is among the most astonishing things one can hear! And Allah knows best, and with Him is success.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage addresses the epistemological status of the knowledge that the world has a creator. The author states there is disagreement: some say it is necessary (ḍarūrī), others say it is theoretical (naẓarī). The truth, he says, is that it is theoretical but so close (qarīb) that it does not require extensive reflection (taʾammul). This is why even children, who have not learned formal reasoning, can grasp it. Because of its nearness, Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī mistakenly claimed it is necessary. The author refutes Rāzī's further claim that this knowledge is also present in animals by instinct (ṭibāʿ al-bahāʾim). He gives the example of an animal that is frightened by the sound of a stick, which Rāzī might have cited as evidence that animals know that a sound requires a cause. The author explains that this fear is due to associative learning (ulfa), not rational deduction. The animal has previously experienced pain from being hit with a stick, and so it associates the sound with pain, not from any rational inference that every event has a cause. Attributing intellectual reasoning to animals, he says, is one of the most astonishing claims one can hear.

Key Terms
ضروري
نظري
تأمل
طباع البهائم
إدراك خيالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge of God's existence is necessary (self-evident) or instinctive in animals.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that it is theoretical but so near it appears necessary; animals only exhibit learned association, not rational inference.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī thought knowledge of God is necessary?
A) Because it is self-evident
B) Because even children grasp it easily
C) Because animals have it by instinct
D) Because it requires no proof

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of God's existence is theoretical but so close to self-evident that even children can attain it; animals do not possess this knowledge through reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that even children can attain knowledge of the Creator through a simple, near reasoning. When did you personally arrive at that knowledge? Was it through a sudden insight, or did it require effort? And if children can do it, what does that say about your own responsibility to deepen that knowledge? Are you content with a child's understanding, or are you striving for certainty?
?
The author describes the animal's fear as mere habit, not reason. You possess the gift of rational reflection that animals lack. How are you using that gift daily to recognize the Creator's presence in your life? Think of the last time you faced a difficulty: did your mind immediately turn to the One who controls all events, or did you rely on habit and emotion like the animal? What concrete step can you take today to train your mind to reason towards God?
?
If knowledge of God is so close that it requires 'no extensive reflection', then the obstacle is not in the proof but in your heedlessness. What distractions in your life are preventing you from taking that short step of reflection? Write down one specific change you will make this week to remove that barrier and bring yourself face to face with the evidence of your Creator.

Proof of the Necessity of a Creator
Sanusi presents the proof from preponderance for a Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's argument from preponderance that proves the world must have a Creator.

Core Idea
The world's existence cannot arise without a Creator.

Sanusi presents a concise logical proof for the necessity of a Creator. The argument hinges on the concepts of preponderance (rajah) and preponderated (marjuh). He shows that if an agent were not required, the same thing would be both preponderated and preponderant, which is inconceivable. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Understand the key terms
Identify the terms 'الراجح' (preponderant) and 'المرجوح' (preponderated) and how they relate to existence and non-existence of the world.
Non-existence is said to be preponderant because it is the default state; existence is preponderated.
3
Follow the logical argument
Notice the conditional: if existence could occur without an agent, then the preponderated would be preponderant—a contradiction. Therefore, an agent is necessary. Then see how the argument generalizes to any change.
The phrase 'أحرى طروء المرجوح' extends the impossibility from equal possibilities to the preponderated.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The conclusion is that the world's existence must be attributed to an Originator (مُوجِد) in every case. This is the proof of a Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأنَّهُ لا يُعقَلُ أنْ يطراً الوجود المرجوحُ ويزول العدمُ الراجح بلا فاعل ، وإلا كان المرجوح في نفسِهِ راجحاً في نفسه ! وهو تناقض لا يُعقل ، وإذا استبانَتِ استحالةُ طروء المساوي بلا فاعل . . فأحرى طروه المرجوح .
فقد عرفت بهذا : وجوب إسنادِ وجودِ العالم الذي اتضح حدوثه إلى مُوجِدٍ على كل تقدير
وهل العلم بذلك ضروري أو نظري ؟
فيه خلافٌ ، والحق أنه نظري ، إلا أنه بنظر قريب لا يفتقر إلى كبير تأتُلٍ، ولذلك أدركَهُ مطلقُ المميّزينَ مِنَ الصبيانِ ، ولقربِهِ جداً توهَّمَ الفخرُ أَنَّهُ ضروري ، وأمَّا ما زادَ مِنْ أَنَّهُ مِنْ طباع البهائم فلا يخفى فساده ، وما ذكرَهُ مِنْ فزع البهيمةِ مِنْ صوتِ الخشبة فليس لإدراكها أنَّ الحادث لا بدَّ لَهُ مِنْ مُحدِثٍ ، بل لإدراك خيالي نشأ عن إِلْفِها مقارنةً ذلك الصوت لألمها لما تكرّر تألُّمُها بالضرب بالخشبة ، وإلا فإسناد الأنظار الفكريَّةِ والاستدلالاتِ بالمقدّمة العقلية للحيواناتِ البهيميَّةِ . . من أعجب ما يُسمَعُ ! والله تعالى أعلم ، وبه التوفيق .
Because it is not rationally conceivable that the preponderated existence should occur and the preponderant non-existence should cease without an agent; otherwise the preponderated in itself would be preponderant in itself! That is a contradiction not rationally conceivable. And if the impossibility of the occurrence of an equal without an agent is clear, then the occurrence of the preponderated is more fitting. So you have known by this: the necessity of attributing the existence of the world, which has become clear as originated, to an Originator in every case. Is this knowledge necessary or theoretical? There is disagreement on it, and the truth is that it is theoretical, but it is a near theoretical (reasoning) that does not require much reflection. Therefore all discerning ones, including children, grasp it. And because of its extreme nearness, al-Fakhr (al-Razi) imagined that it is necessary. As for his additional claim that it is from the nature of animals, its corruption is not hidden. And what he mentioned about the animal's fright from the sound of a stick is not because it perceives that an originated event must have an originator, but rather from an imaginative perception arising from its habit of associating that sound with its pain due to repeated experience of being hurt by beating with a stick. Otherwise, attributing intellectual reflections and rational-premise-based inferences to brute animals is among the most astonishing things one hears! And Allah Almighty knows best, and in Him is all success.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of a creator for the world, followed by a discussion of the nature of this knowledge. The author, Sanusi, presents the argument from preponderance: it is inconceivable that a preponderated state (existence) should occur and a preponderant state (non-existence) should cease without an agent. If that were possible, then the same thing would be both preponderated and preponderant, a contradiction. Since equal possibilities (like the world's existence and non-existence) also require an agent, the preponderated is even more in need. Therefore, the world must be attributed to an Originator. Then Sanusi asks whether this knowledge is necessary (self-evident) or theoretical (acquired by reasoning). He states that the correct view is that it is theoretical, but it requires only a very short reasoning, so much so that all discerning persons, including children, attain it. Fakhr al-Din al-Razi mistakenly thought it was necessary due to its extreme clarity. Sanusi refutes al-Razi's additional claim that animals also have this knowledge; he explains that an animal's fear of a stick is not rational inference but based on association of the sound with past pain, not on the principle that an event needs a cause.

Key Terms
المرجوح
الراجح
فاعل
مُوجِد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what would be the consequence if the existence of the world occurred without an agent?
A) The world would be eternal.
B) The preponderated would be preponderant in itself, a contradiction.
C) The world would have multiple creators.
D) Non-existence would remain preponderant.

Key Takeaway
The argument from preponderance proves that the world's existence must be attributed to a Creator, because without an agent a logical contradiction would result.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have seen that your own existence is a preponderated state—two equally possible outcomes, and you were brought into being by a Creator. Do you live as if your existence is a gift from Him every moment, or do you take it for granted? What would change if you began each day by acknowledging that you are being continuously created by the Originator?
?
The proof shows that even if the world's existence were preponderated (more likely), it still cannot happen without an agent. This means that nothing in your life—not your successes, your health, or your faith—occurs without His direct intervention. How does this realization challenge your sense of self-sufficiency? Identify one area where you rely more on your own efforts than on the Creator, and commit to turning to Him in that area today.
?
Sanusi says the contradiction of the preponderated being preponderant is 'not rationally conceivable.' This means that the need for a Creator is as certain as the impossibility of a square circle. Do you treat this certainty as something that should shape your daily decisions, or do you compartmentalize it as mere theology? What would it look like to let this certainty drive you to constant gratitude and obedience?

Knowledge of the Creator: Necessary or Theoretical?
Sanusi classifies knowledge of the Creator as theoretical and refutes al-Razi on animals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi considers knowledge of the Creator theoretical rather than necessary, and his refutation of al-Razi's claim that animals share this knowledge.

Core Idea
This knowledge is a short theoretical inference, not self-evident.

After establishing the proof for a Creator, Sanusi addresses a methodological question: Is this knowledge necessary (self-evident) or theoretical (acquired by reasoning)? He argues that it is theoretical but extremely easy, so easy that even children grasp it. Fakhr al-Din al-Razi mistakenly thought it was necessary, and even claimed animals know it. Sanusi refutes this by explaining that animal behavior is based on association, not rational inference.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the epistemological discussion and refutation.
2
Identify the two views
Notice that Sanusi presents a disagreement: is knowledge of the Creator necessary or theoretical? He states the truth that it is theoretical but close reasoning.
The key terms are 'ضروري' (necessary) and 'نظري' (theoretical).
3
Understand why al-Razi made his mistake
Sanusi explains that al-Razi imagined it was necessary because of its extreme nearness (clarity). Even children grasp it, but that does not make it self-evident.
4
Analyze the refutation of animal knowledge
Read how Sanusi refutes al-Razi's claim that animals have this knowledge. He argues that an animal's fear of a stick sound comes from associative learning (habitual pain), not rational deduction.
Sanusi calls the attribution of intellectual reasoning to animals 'among the most astonishing things.'

The Proof of God's Pre-Eternity and Everlastingness
Sanusi proves that the Creator must be eternal and necessarily everlasting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Creator must be both pre-eternal (no beginning) and necessarily everlasting (no end), based on rational arguments that show these attributes are necessary to avoid logical contradictions.

Core Idea
The Creator cannot have a beginning or an end; both would lead to logical absurdities.

In this passage, Sanusi presents the classical kalam argument for God's pre-eternity and everlastingness. He shows that if God had a beginning, He would need a creator, leading to absurdities like infinite regress or circularity. Similarly, if God could cease to exist, He would be contingent, contradicting His necessary existence. We will unpack this proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the two main claims: pre-eternity (qidam) and everlastingness (baqa').
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the proof for pre-eternity
Sanusi argues that if the Creator had a beginning, He would need an originator, leading to either infinite regress or circular reasoning—both impossible. Therefore, He must be pre-eternal.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Understand the proof for everlastingness
Sanusi then argues that if the Creator could cease to exist, His existence would be contingent, requiring a cause, making Him originated—contradicting His pre-eternity. So He must be necessarily everlasting.
This uses the definition of necessary vs. contingent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام على الصفات العدمية ( السلوب )
باب
الدليل على وجوب قدمه جل و عز و وجوب بقائه ثم يجب أن يكونَ مُحدِثُ العالم قديماً ؛ أي : لا أوَّليَّةَ لوجودِهِ ، وإلا لافتقر إلى مُحدِثِ ، ويلزم التسلسل فيؤدي إلى فراغ ما لا نهاية له ، أو الدَّورُ ، فيؤدي إلى تقدم الشيء على نفسِهِ ، وكلاهما مستحيل لا يُعقَلُ
ويلزمُ أنْ يكونَ واجبَ البقاء ؛ أي : لا آخريَّة لوجودِهِ ؛ إذْ لو قَبلَ أنْ يلحقه العدمُ لكان وجوده جائزاً لا واجباً ؛ لما عرفتَ أنَّ حقيقة الواجب : ما لا يتصوَّرُ في العقل عدمُهُ ، وهذا الوجود قد فُرِضَ أَنَّهُ يقبلُ العدمَ ، فيكون جائزاً ؛ إذ الجائز : ما يصح فيه الوجود والعدم ، والجائز يستحيل أن يقع بلا سببٍ ، فيحتاجُ إذاً هذا الوجود الجائز إلى سبب فيكونُ مُحدَثاً ، وقد قام البرهان على وجوب قدمِهِ
•
فإذاً ؛ فَرْضُ عدمِ وجوب البقاء في كل ما قام البرهانُ على وجوب قدمِهِ .. تناقض لا يُعقَلُ .
Discussion on the negative attributes (the negations). Chapter: The proof for the necessity of His pre-eternity, Mighty and Majestic, and the necessity of His everlastingness. Then it is necessary that the originator of the world be pre-eternal; i.e., no beginning for His existence, otherwise He would need an originator, and an infinite regress would follow leading to a void with no end, or circularity leading to a thing preceding itself, both of which are impossible and inconceivable. And it follows that He must be necessarily everlasting; i.e., no end for His existence; because if He were susceptible to non-existence, His existence would be contingent, not necessary; since you know that the reality of the Necessary is that whose non-existence is inconceivable in the intellect, and this existence has been assumed to accept non-existence, so it is contingent; for the contingent is that in which existence and non-existence are both possible, and the contingent is impossible to occur without a cause. So this contingent existence would need a cause and thus be originated, while proof has established the necessity of His pre-eternity. Therefore, assuming the non-necessity of everlastingness for that which proof has established the necessity of its pre-eternity is an inconceivable contradiction.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a proof for two divine attributes: pre-eternity (qidam) and everlastingness (baqa'). The author begins by stating the chapter topic on negative attributes. Then he argues: the originator of the world must be pre-eternal (no beginning), because if He had a beginning, He would need an originator, leading to either an infinite regress or circular reasoning—both absurd. Next, He must be necessarily everlasting (no end), because if He could cease to exist, His existence would be contingent (ja'iz), not necessary. The contingent requires a cause for its existence, so it would be originated (muhdath). But proof has already established His pre-eternity, so assuming He could cease is a contradiction. The argument follows the standard kalam method of showing that the absence of these attributes leads to logical impossibilities.

Key Terms
قدم
بقاء
واجب الوجود
جائز الوجود
التسلسل
الدور
مُحدَث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think pre-eternity merely means 'very old' in time, but here it means necessary existence without beginning.
Correction: Pre-eternity (qidam) in this context means having no beginning at all, not simply being ancient. The proof is based on logical necessity, not temporal duration.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what two logical problems follow if the originator of the world is not pre-eternal?
A) Incorporeality and indivisibility
B) Infinite regress and circularity
C) Temporal limitation and change
D) Dependence on matter and form

Key Takeaway
The Creator must be both pre-eternal and necessarily everlasting; assuming otherwise leads to logical absurdities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah has no beginning and no end, then your existence is entirely dependent on Him. Are you living as if your life is a fleeting breath or as if it is anchored in the Eternal? What will you change today to align your priorities with this truth?
?
The argument shows that assuming a beginning for Allah leads to logical absurdities. Have you ever entertained even a subtle doubt about Allah's pre-eternity? If so, are you willing to confront that doubt and strengthen your certainty using the rational proof given here?
?
If Allah's existence is necessary and cannot cease, then your relationship with Him is the only permanent reality. What temporary thing are you holding onto that competes with your commitment to the Eternal? Make a concrete decision to release it today.

Three Proofs of God's Independence from a Place
Sanusi gives three proofs that God cannot be an attribute subsisting in a substrate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three rational proofs that God cannot be an attribute subsisting in a substrate, establishing His independence from a place.

Core Idea
God cannot be an attribute in a substrate; three proofs confirm His self-subsistence.

Sanusi presents three concise proofs to establish that Allah is not an attribute (ṣifa) or meaning (maʿnā) inhering in a substrate. These proofs rely on logical impossibility and the consequences of assuming otherwise. Understanding these proofs strengthens the conviction that Allah is self-subsistent and free from spatial dependence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three proofs
Look for the three distinct arguments Sanusi makes. Notice how each begins with a conditional statement like 'if He were an attribute...'
The third proof is incomplete due to OCR issues but still important.
3
Understand the logic of each proof
For each proof, trace the logical steps and why it leads to an impossibility. Use the commentary for clarification.
The first proof hinges on the principle that an attribute cannot subsist in another attribute.
4
Reflect on implications for tawḥīd
Consider how these proofs reinforce the belief that Allah is self-subsistent and not dependent on a place or substrate.
This lesson establishes a key foundation for Islamic theology.

وعلى استغنائِهِ عنِ المحلَّ : أَنَّهُ لو كانَ صفةً لاستحال اتصافه بالصفاتِ المعنويةِ والمعاني ؛ إذ الصفةُ لا تقوم بالصفةِ ، ولأنَّهُ أيضاً لو كانَ صفةً لافتقر إلى محلّ يقومُ بهِ ، ثم إنْ كانَ المحلُّ إلهاً مثل الصفة لزم تعدُّدُ الآلهة ، وإن انفردَتِ الصفةُ بالألوهية وأحكامها لزم جواز قيام صفةٍ بمحلَّ ولا يتّصفُ المحلُّ بحكمها ! وهو محال ، وأيضاً : فليس كون الصفةِ إلهاً بأولى من كون محلها [؟]
And regarding His independence from a place: that if He were an attribute, it would be impossible for Him to be characterized by the meaningful attributes and the meanings, because an attribute does not subsist in an attribute; and also because if He were an attribute, He would need a substrate in which to subsist; then if the substrate is a god like the attribute, multiplicity of gods would follow, and if the attribute alone were characterized by divinity and its rulings, it would entail the permissibility of an attribute subsisting in a substrate while the substrate is not characterized by its ruling—and that is impossible. Also: it is not more likely that the attribute is a god than that its substrate is [a god] [؟]
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs for God's independence from a place (maḥall), i.e., that it is impossible for God to be an attribute (ṣifa) or a meaning (maʿnā) inhering in a substrate. The first proof: if God were an attribute, He could not be characterized by the meaningful attributes (al-ṣifāt al-maʿnawiyya) like knowledge, power, will, life, because an attribute cannot subsist in another attribute. The second proof: if God were an attribute, He would need a substrate to subsist in; if that substrate is also a god, then there would be multiple gods, and if the attribute alone has divinity while the substrate does not, then it would be possible for an attribute to subsist in a substrate without the substrate sharing its ruling, which is absurd. The third proof (incomplete due to OCR corruption): it is not more likely that the attribute is a god than that its substrate is a god, implying symmetry or infinite regress. These proofs collectively establish that God cannot be a dependent attribute, but must be self-subsistent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is possible for one attribute to subsist in another attribute.
Correction: The author argues that an attribute cannot subsist in an attribute; this is a logical impossibility.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof that God cannot be an attribute (sifa) or meaning (ma'na)?
A) If God were an attribute, He would need a specifier.
B) If God were an attribute, He could not be characterized by the meaningful attributes.
C) If God were an attribute, He would be multiple.
D) If God were an attribute, He would be divisible.

Key Takeaway
Allah is not an attribute or meaning residing in a substrate; He is self-subsistent and independent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does this challenge any subtle shirk in your concept of God? Will you consciously remind yourself daily that He is self-subsistent, not dependent on anything, not even your worship?
?
The proof against multiple gods shows that if God were an attribute, it would lead to absurdities. What are the 'gods' you have set up alongside Allah—success, comfort, reputation—that you treat as independent substrates of meaning? This lesson calls you to destroy them logically and practically. Which one will you actively dismantle today?
?
The argument that an attribute cannot subsist in an attribute forces you to see God as utterly simple and indivisible. Have you ever divided your trust among many sources, as if they were independent attributes of security? How does Allah's absolute oneness demand that you consolidate your reliance solely on Him? What is the first step you will take to cut off your dependence on a created 'substrate'?

Proof of God's Independence from a Specifier
Sanusi proves God's independence from a specifier using pre-eternity and permanence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God cannot depend on any specifier or agent for His existence, based on the necessity of His pre-eternity and permanence.

Core Idea
God's pre-eternity and permanence logically exclude any need for a specifier.

Sanusi has defined self-subsistence (al-qiyām bi al-nafs) as independence from a place and a specifier. Having already established God's pre-eternity and permanence, he now uses those attributes to prove that God cannot possibly need a specifier. This argument is crucial because it rules out the idea that God's existence could be caused or specified by anything else.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Recall that Sanusi previously defined self-subsistence as independence from a place and a specifier. Now he must prove that God indeed possesses this independence.
The passage explicitly refers to the second interpretation of self-subsistence.
2
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the key terms: al-istighnā', al-mukhaṣṣiṣ, al-qidam, al-baqā', al-ḥudūth. Try to follow the logical flow.
Pay attention to the connection between need for a specifier and origination.
3
Analyze the proof
The proof proceeds: need for a specifier entails origination → God is pre-eternal and permanent → origination is impossible for God → therefore need for a specifier is impossible. Identify each step.
The phrase 'taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil' (obtaining the already existent) is a key logical term.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why this proof is necessary. It shows that God's existence is necessary and uncaused, which is foundational for tawḥīd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و الاستغناء لما ذكر أن معنى القيام بالنفس على التفسير الثاني : هو المحل والمخصص .. احتاجَ أنْ يقيم البرهان على وجوبِ استغنائِهِ تعالى عن الأمرين . أما دليل استغنائِهِ تعالى عنِ المُخصَّص ؛ أي : الفاعل : فهو ما تقدَّمَ مِنْ وجوبِ قدمِهِ تعالى ووجوب بقائِهِ؛ يعني : أنَّ الاحتياج إلى المخصص يستلزم الحدوثَ؛ لأنَّ أثرَ المُخصّص لا يكون إلا حادثاً؛ لأنَّ القديم حاصلُ الوجودِ واجبُه ، وتحصيل الحاصل محال ، والحدوث على ذاتِ مولانا جلَّ وعزَّ وعلى صفاتِهِ مستحيل ؛ لوجوب القدمِ والبقاء للذات العليَّةِ وصفاتِها ، فاحتياجُهُ تعالى إلى المخصّص مستحيل .
And [as for] independence, when he mentioned that the meaning of self-subsistence according to the second interpretation is [independence from] a place and a specifier, he needed to establish a proof for the necessity of His, may He be exalted, independence from the two matters. As for the proof of His, may He be exalted, independence from the specifier, that is, the agent, it is what preceded from the necessity of His pre-eternity, may He be exalted, and the necessity of His permanence; meaning: that need for a specifier entails origination; because the effect of the specifier is only originated; because the pre-eternal is actual in existence and necessary, and obtaining the already existent is impossible, and origination upon the essence of our Master, Glorified and Exalted, and upon His attributes is impossible, due to the necessity of pre-eternity and permanence for the supreme essence and its attributes. So His, may He be exalted, need for a specifier is impossible.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage provides the proof for God's independence (al-istighnā') from a specifier (al-mukhaṣṣiṣ), i.e., an agent that would specify His existence. The author first notes that the second interpretation of 'self-subsistence' (al-qiyām bi al-nafs) includes independence from both a place and a specifier. Then he presents the proof: the necessity of God's pre-eternity (qidam) and permanence (baqā') directly implies independence from a specifier. The reasoning is that need for a specifier entails origination (ḥudūth), because the effect of a specifier is always originated; the pre-eternal is already necessary in existence, and 'obtaining the already existent' (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil) is logically impossible. Since origination is impossible for God's essence and attributes due to their necessary pre-eternity and permanence, any need for a specifier is impossible. This argument shows that God's existence is necessary and uncaused.

Key Terms
الاستغناء
المخصص
الفاعل
القدم
البقاء
الحدوث
تحصيل الحاصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Independence from a specifier implies God has no cause whatsoever in any sense.
Correction: The proof is specifically about a specifier that would originate God's existence. It does not deny that God has no cause at all; rather it shows that He is uncaused in the sense of not being brought into existence by an external agent.

Checkpoint
Why is God's need for a specifier impossible according to Sanusi?
A) Because God is self-sufficient and needs nothing.
B) Because need for a specifier entails origination, and God is pre-eternal and permanent.
C) Because God has no attributes that could need a specifier.
D) Because specifiers only apply to contingent things, not to God.

Key Takeaway
God's pre-eternity and permanence logically necessitate His independence from any specifier that would bring Him into existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does the truth that God has never needed any specifier challenge your tendency to look to created things as if they were ultimate causes? What would it look like for you this week to live as if God alone is the source of every moment of your existence?
?
If God's pre-eternity means He never came to be, then every breath you take is a direct gift from the One who is independent of all. How would your prayer life change if you truly believed that nothing—not your efforts, not your circumstances—adds to God's sovereignty? Will you let this truth turn your gratitude into action?
?
The argument says 'obtaining the already existent is impossible.' In what subtle ways do you try to 'obtain' God's favors as if He were not already giving them? What is one dependency—on people, on wealth, on your own abilities—that you need to surrender today to the One who needs nothing?
