Why the Creator Must Be Powerful
Sanusi states the necessity of divine power and its non-identity with essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational necessity for the Creator to possess power and why this power cannot be identical with His essence.

Core Idea
God must be powerful because creation exists, and His power is distinct from His essence.

Sanusi begins the chapter on divine power by establishing two foundational points: that the Originator of the world must be powerful, and that His power is not identical with His essence. Without these principles, any further discussion of God's attributes would be incoherent. This lesson unpacks the first part of the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then the English. Notice the concise logical style.
Sanusi uses the phrase 'لا يُعقَل' (inconceivable) to mark logical impossibility.
2
Identify the two main claims
The passage makes two claims: 1) The Creator must be powerful, otherwise He could not create. 2) His power is not identical with His essence, else two would be one.
These are separate but linked.
3
Understand the necessity argument
The necessity of power is derived from the existence of creation: if the Creator were not powerful, He could not have brought anything into existence.
This is a simple conditional argument.
4
Analyze the non-identity argument
If power were identical with essence, then the attribute and the essence would be absolutely the same, making two distinct concepts one. That is logically impossible.
This prevents pantheism or monism of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفضل الأول في وجوب القدرة وأحكامها ويلزم أيضاً أنْ يكونَ مُحدِثُ العالم قادراً - وإلا لَمَا أوجد شيئاً مِنَ العالم - بقدرة ؛ لأنَّهُ لا يُعقَلُ قادرٌ لا قدرة لهُ ، غيرِ مُتَّحِدةٍ بذاتِهِ ، وإلا لزم كون الاثنين واحداً ، وهو محال لا يُعقَلُ ، قديمةٍ ، وإلا كان ضدُّها ـ وهو العجز - قديماً ، فلا ينعدم أبداً ؛ لما عرفت أنَّ القديم لا يقبلُ العدم، فيلزمُ ألا يقدر أبداً ، ومصنوعاته تشهد باستحالة ذلك ، وأيضاً : لو كانَتِ القدرة حادثة لاحتاجَتْ في إحداثها إلى قدرة أخرى ، ولزم التسلسل ويلزم أن تكون هذه القدرة مُتعلّقةً بجميع الممكنات ؛ إذْ لو تعلَّقَتْ ببعضها دونَ بعض لاحتاجَتْ إِلى مُخصص لاستوائها في حقيقة الإمكان ، فتكون حادثة ، وقد عرفت وجوب قدمها ، وإنْ فُرِضَ تخصيصها بغيرِ مُخصِّص لزمَ انقلاب الجائز مستحيلاً ذكر في هذا الفصل أربعة مطالب
The first chapter on the necessity of power (القدرة) and its rulings. It is also necessary that the originator of the world be powerful – otherwise he would not have brought into existence anything from the world – with a power; because a powerful one without power is inconceivable, not identical with His essence, otherwise two would be one, which is impossible and inconceivable; eternal, otherwise its opposite – which is impotence – would be eternal, so it would never cease to exist, because you know that the eternal does not admit non-existence, so it would follow that He would never be powerful, and His created things bear witness to the impossibility of that. Also, if power were temporal, it would need another power to bring it into existence, and an infinite regress would follow. And it is necessary that this power be related to all possible things; for if it were related to some to the exclusion of others, it would need a specifier due to their equivalence in the reality of possibility, so it would be temporal, and you have known the necessity of its eternity; and if its specification were assumed without a specifier, then the transformation of the possible into the impossible would follow. This chapter mentions four demands.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage opens the first chapter (الفضل الأول) on the necessity of power (القدرة) and its rulings. It presents a multi-step proof that the Creator (محدث العالم) must be powerful. First, otherwise He would not have brought the world into existence. Second, this power cannot be identical with His essence, else two would be one. Third, it must be eternal, because if it were temporal, its opposite (impotence) would be eternal and never cease, which is refuted by the existence of created things. Also, a temporal power would require another power, leading to infinite regress. Fourth, this power must be related to all possible things (جميع الممكنات), because if limited, it would need a specifier (مخصص), making it temporal and contradictory to its eternity. The passage concludes by stating that this chapter contains four demands (أربعة مطالب).

Key Terms
القدرة
قادر
الذات
المحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's power is identical with His essence (like the Mu'tazilite view of attributes being identical with essence).
Correction: Sanusi argues that if power were identical with essence, then two (the essence and the attribute) would be one, which is rationally inconceivable. Thus, power is a distinct attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why must the Creator be powerful?
A) Because power is eternal.
B) Otherwise He would not have brought anything into existence.
C) Because power is identical with His essence.
D) Because power is temporal.

Key Takeaway
The Creator must possess power, and this power is a distinct attribute not identical with His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you struggle with your own lack of power, do you take comfort in the fact that your Creator possesses power as an eternal attribute, not a mere title? How can you actively remind yourself of this when you feel helpless?
?
The passage argues that making God's power identical to His essence would be impossible. Are there ways you have inadvertently collapsed God's attributes into His essence in your mind, making Him less personal or less able to act? What will you do to maintain the distinction?

The Eternity of Divine Power
Sanusi gives two proofs for the eternity of divine power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's power must be eternal and not temporal, and how the impossibility of eternal impotence and infinite regress support this.

Core Idea
God's power is eternal because its opposite would be eternal, and a temporal power would require an endless chain.

After establishing that God is powerful with a distinct attribute, Sanusi now proves that this attribute must be eternal. He gives two arguments: if power were temporal, its opposite (impotence) would be eternal, which contradicts the observed existence of creatures; also, a temporal power would need another power to bring it into existence, leading to an infinite regress. This lesson examines these proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English carefully. Note the two separate arguments separated by 'وأيضاً'.
The first argument uses the principle that the eternal does not cease.
2
Understand the first argument (from opposite)
If power were temporal, then before its existence, impotence would be eternal. Since the eternal cannot cease, impotence would never cease, meaning God would never be powerful, but creation shows He is powerful.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand the second argument (infinite regress)
If power were temporal, it would need another power to create it, leading to an infinite chain of powers, which is impossible.
This argument is used often to prove eternity of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Divine Power Encompasses All Possibles
Sanusi proves that divine power relates to all possible things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's power must be related to all possible things and why it cannot be limited to some possibles.

Core Idea
God's power reaches everything possible, because a limited power would need an arbitrary specifier.

The final part of Sanusi's discussion on divine power establishes its universal scope. He argues that God's power must relate to all possible things (جميع الممكنات). If it were restricted to only some, there would need to be a specifier (مخصص) causing that limitation, which would make power temporal—contradicting its eternity. This lesson unpacks this argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if limited... then specifier... then temporal'.
The phrase 'في حقيقة الإمكان' refers to the equal possibility of all possibles in themselves.
2
Understand the equality of possibles
All possible things are equally possible in themselves; there is no inherent difference that would make one more eligible for power's relation.
This is a key premise.
3
Analyze the specifier argument
If power related only to some possibles, something external (a specifier) must have chosen them. But that specifier would itself be temporal, making power temporal.
This leads to a contradiction because power is eternal.
4
Consider the alternative without specifier
If no specifier, then the limitation would be arbitrary, turning a possible limitation into an impossible one (since without cause, a possible cannot become actual).
This reinforces the need for a specifier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفضل الأول في وجوب القدرة وأحكامها ويلزم أيضاً أنْ يكونَ مُحدِثُ العالم قادراً - وإلا لَمَا أوجد شيئاً مِنَ العالم - بقدرة ؛ لأنَّهُ لا يُعقَلُ قادرٌ لا قدرة لهُ ، غيرِ مُتَّحِدةٍ بذاتِهِ ، وإلا لزم كون الاثنين واحداً ، وهو محال لا يُعقَلُ ، قديمةٍ ، وإلا كان ضدُّها ـ وهو العجز - قديماً ، فلا ينعدم أبداً ؛ لما عرفت أنَّ القديم لا يقبلُ العدم، فيلزمُ ألا يقدر أبداً ، ومصنوعاته تشهد باستحالة ذلك ، وأيضاً : لو كانَتِ القدرة حادثة لاحتاجَتْ في إحداثها إلى قدرة أخرى ، ولزم التسلسل ويلزم أن تكون هذه القدرة مُتعلّقةً بجميع الممكنات ؛ إذْ لو تعلَّقَتْ ببعضها دونَ بعض لاحتاجَتْ إِلى مُخصص لاستوائها في حقيقة الإمكان ، فتكون حادثة ، وقد عرفت وجوب قدمها ، وإنْ فُرِضَ تخصيصها بغيرِ مُخصِّص لزمَ انقلاب الجائز مستحيلاً ذكر في هذا الفصل أربعة مطالب
The first chapter on the necessity of power (القدرة) and its rulings. It is also necessary that the originator of the world be powerful – otherwise he would not have brought into existence anything from the world – with a power; because a powerful one without power is inconceivable, not identical with His essence, otherwise two would be one, which is impossible and inconceivable; eternal, otherwise its opposite – which is impotence – would be eternal, so it would never cease to exist, because you know that the eternal does not admit non-existence, so it would follow that He would never be powerful, and His created things bear witness to the impossibility of that. Also, if power were temporal, it would need another power to bring it into existence, and an infinite regress would follow. And it is necessary that this power be related to all possible things; for if it were related to some to the exclusion of others, it would need a specifier due to their equivalence in the reality of possibility, so it would be temporal, and you have known the necessity of its eternity; and if its specification were assumed without a specifier, then the transformation of the possible into the impossible would follow. This chapter mentions four demands.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage opens the first chapter (الفضل الأول) on the necessity of power (القدرة) and its rulings. It presents a multi-step proof that the Creator (محدث العالم) must be powerful. First, otherwise He would not have brought the world into existence. Second, this power cannot be identical with His essence, else two would be one. Third, it must be eternal, because if it were temporal, its opposite (impotence) would be eternal and never cease, which is refuted by the existence of created things. Also, a temporal power would require another power, leading to infinite regress. Fourth, this power must be related to all possible things (جميع الممكنات), because if limited, it would need a specifier (مخصص), making it temporal and contradictory to its eternity. The passage concludes by stating that this chapter contains four demands (أربعة مطالب).

Key Terms

الممكنات
المخصص
حقيقة الإمكان
الحدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's power is limited to certain possibles, e.g., only those He actually creates.
Correction: Sanusi proves that if power were limited, it would need a specifier, contradicting its eternity. Thus, power extends to all possibles, even those not actualized.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why would a limited power need a specifier?
A) Because power is eternal.
B) Because all possibles are equal in possibility.
C) Because God is omnipotent.
D) Because power is one.

Key Takeaway
God's power is not partial; it encompasses every possible thing, because any limitation would be arbitrary or require a temporal specifier.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What does that reveal about your conviction of His universal power? This week, when a need arises, consciously remind yourself: 'Allah's power relates to all possibles, including this.'
?
The passage argues that any limitation on God's power would require a temporal specifier. Have you ever mentally 'limited' Allah by assuming He could not change a certain situation in your life—perhaps a broken relationship or a persistent sin? What would it look like to repent from that mental limitation and affirm His absolute power over all possibles? Make a specific dua today that reflects that affirmation.
?
If God's power encompasses all possibles, then no situation you face is outside His ability to transform. Yet you often rely on your own efforts more than on Him. Why? What fear or assumption drives that lack of reliance? Identify one area where you have been acting as if God's power were limited, and commit to a concrete act of tawakkul (reliance) tonight.

Proof that the Creator Must Be Powerful
Sanusi argues for four properties of divine power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that the Creator must possess eternal power distinct from His essence and universal in scope.

Core Idea
The Creator's power must be eternal, distinct, and unlimited because creation exists.

Sanusi now establishes that the Creator is not merely powerful in a general sense, but that His power has specific rational properties. The argument moves from the fact of creation to the necessity of a power that is eternal, non-identical to the essence, and universal. Read carefully to see how each claim is defended.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then the English translation. Notice the logical flow.
The passage is dense; don't worry about understanding everything immediately.
2
Identify the first claim: Creator must be powerful
Sanusi begins by stating the necessity of the Creator being powerful, otherwise creation wouldn't occur. This is the foundation.
The word 'qadir' (powerful) is key.
3
Follow the argument for eternality and non-identity
The passage argues that power cannot be identical to the essence (otherwise two become one) and must be eternal (otherwise its opposite, incapacity, would be eternal, contradicting creation).
Look for the phrase 'ghayr muttahida' (not identical) and 'qadima' (eternal).
4
Consider the final point about universal scope
The power must relate to all possible things, otherwise it would need a specifier, making it originated—contradicting its eternality.
This shows God's power is unlimited in scope.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويلزمُ أيضاً أنْ يكونَ مُحدِثُ العالم قادراً - وإلا لَمَا أوجد شيئاً مِنَ العالم - بقدرةٍ؛ لأنَّهُ لا يُعقَلُ قادرٌ لا قدرة لهُ، غيرِ مُتَّحِدةٍ بذاتِهِ، وإلا لزم كون الاثنين واحداً، وهو محال لا يُعقَلُ، قديمةٍ، وإلا كان ضدُّها ـ وهو العجز - قديماً، فلا ينعدم أبداً؛ لما عرفْتَ أنَّ القديم لا يقبلُ العدم، فيلزمُ ألا يقدر أبداً، ومصنوعاته تشهد باستحالة ذلك، وأيضاً: لو كانَتِ القدرة حادثة لاحتاجَتْ في إحداثها إلى قدرة أخرى، ولزم التسلسل. ويلزم أن تكون هذه القدرة مُتعلّقةً بجميع الممكنات؛ إذْ لو تعلَّقَتْ ببعضها دونَ بعض لاحتاجَتْ إِلى مُخصص لاستوائها في حقيقة الإمكان، فتكون حادثة، وقد عرفت وجوب قدمها، وإِنْ فُرِضَ تخصيصها بغيرِ مُخصِّص لزمَ انقلاب الجائز مستحيلاً.
It is also necessary that the Maker of the world be powerful—otherwise He would not have brought anything of the world into existence—with a power; because a powerful being without power is inconceivable, which is not identical with His essence, otherwise the two would be one, which is impossible and inconceivable; eternal, otherwise its opposite—which is incapacity—would be eternal, so it would never cease to exist; because you have known that the eternal does not admit non-existence, so it follows that He would never be able, and His created things bear witness to the impossibility of that. Also: if the power were originated, it would need another power for its origination, leading to infinite regress. And it is necessary that this power be related to all possible things; because if it were related to some without others, it would need a specificatory cause, given their equality in the reality of possibility, so it would be originated, and you have known the necessity of its eternality; and if it is supposed to be particularized without a specificatory cause, it would entail the transformation of the possible into the impossible.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of divine power and its attributes. The author begins by stating that the Creator of the world must be powerful—otherwise He could not have created anything. He then argues that this power cannot be identical to His essence (otherwise two things would become one, which is absurd) and must be eternal (otherwise its opposite, incapacity, would be eternal and never vanish, but the existence of created things shows that incapacity does not obtain). If power were originated, it would require another power, leading to infinite regress. Finally, the power must relate to all possibles, because if it related only to some, it would need a specifier, making it originated—but it is already established as eternal. Without a specifier, particularization would turn a possible into an impossibility.

Misconception: God's power is identical to His essence.
Correction: The passage argues that if power were identical to essence, then essence and attribute would be one, which is inconceivable and impossible.

Checkpoint

B) Because if it were originated, its opposite (incapacity) would be eternal and never cease, contradicting the existence of creation.
C) Because eternal things cannot be caused.
D) Because power must be related to all possible things.

Key Takeaway
The Creator's power must be eternal, distinct from His essence, and encompass all possible things.

Why Tasalsul Alone Suffices
Sanusi explains why he only mentions tasalsul and not dawr when proving God's eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi limits his discussion to tasalsul (infinite regress) and how this term encompasses dawr (circularity) in the context of proving God's eternity.

Core Idea
Tasalsul includes dawr, so one term covers both impossibilities.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a common question: Why does he only mention infinite regress (tasalsul) when proving God's eternity, but not circularity (dawr)? He clarifies that he uses tasalsul in a broader sense that inherently includes dawr, and this is a standard practice among scholars. Understanding this methodological choice prevents misunderstanding and reveals the elegance of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully along with the full literal translation. Let the author's own words and structure guide your understanding.
Pay attention to the words 'al-amm' (general) and 'al-akhass' (specific).
2
Identify the relationship between tasalsul and dawr
Sanusi states that he takes tasalsul 'bi al-ma'na al-amm al-shamil li al-dawr' (in the general sense that includes circularity). Note that dawr is also a kind of tasalsul, but in finite matters. Understand this hierarchy.
3
Reflect on the implication for the creed
Consider why this matters: if someone thinks only mentioning tasalsul is a flaw, they are mistaken. The general sense covers both, so the proof remains complete. Be mindful of this point, as Sanusi emphasizes with 'fa-tanabbahu li-dhalika'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الأولى ، وهكذا أبداً ، ولزمَ التسلسل ، وقد علمت استحالة الدور والتسلسل في باب وجوب قدمِهِ تعالى ووجوب بقائه (۱) . وإِنَّما اقتصر هنا في العقيدةِ على التسلسل ؛ لأنَّه أخذه بالمعنى الأعم الشامل للدور ، ولأنَّ الدَّور تسلسل أيضاً ، لكن في أمور متناهية ، ولهذا كثيراً ما يقتصرُ بعضُ العلماء على التسلسل في بابِ القدمِ وغيرِهِ ممَّا يلزمُ فيهِ الدَّورُ أو التسلسل بالمعنى الأخص ؛ وهو الذي يكونُ في أمورٍ غير متناهية ، فيتوهَّمُ القاصر نقصاً في كلامهم ، وليس فيه نقص ؛ لما عرفت الآنَ في معنى التسلسل الأعم ، فتنبه لذلك
In the first, and so on forever, and infinite regress would be necessary, and you already know the impossibility of circularity and infinite regress in the chapter of the necessity of His eternity, may He be exalted, and the necessity of His permanence. He only restricted his discussion here in the creed to infinite regress because he took it in the more general sense that includes circularity, and because circularity is also an infinite regress, but in finite matters. And for this reason many scholars often restrict themselves to infinite regress in the chapter of eternity and other matters where either circularity or infinite regress in the specific sense would be required—the latter being in infinite matters—so the deficient person might imagine a flaw in their speech, but there is no flaw in it, because of what you have now understood about the more general meaning of infinite regress. So be mindful of that.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage is a methodological clarification within the proof of God's eternity. The author has argued that if God were not eternal, an infinite regress would result. Here he explains why he only mentions 'tasalsul' (infinite regress) and not 'dawr' (circularity). He states that he takes 'tasalsul' in a more general sense that includes 'dawr'. In fact, circularity is a type of regress but within a finite set of terms. Many scholars restrict themselves to the term 'tasalsul' in this context, even when both might be implied, because it is comprehensive. A student might think there is a deficiency in limiting to one, but no deficiency exists because the general meaning covers both. The author ends with 'fa-tanabbahu li-dhalika' (so be mindful of that), emphasizing the importance of this point.

Key Terms

دور dawr
القدم qidam
البقاء baqa'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that mentioning only tasalsul without dawr is a deficiency in the proof.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that tasalsul is taken in a general sense that includes dawr, so no deficiency exists.

Checkpoint
Why does the author limit his discussion to tasalsul (infinite regress) when proving God's eternity?
A) Because dawr (circularity) is impossible.
B) Because tasalsul in the general sense includes dawr.
C) Because the scholars only mention tasalsul.
D) Because dawr is not relevant.

Key Takeaway
In kalam, tasalsul in the general sense includes dawr, so mentioning only tasalsul is sufficient and not a deficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a scholar who seems to omit an important point, do you immediately assume deficiency, or do you pause to consider that they may be using a broader term? How can you train yourself to adopt the charitable assumption this lesson teaches?

The author ends with 'so be mindful of that.' In your study of 'aqeedah, how often do you truly pause to be mindful of subtle distinctions? What one habit could you change to deepen your reverence for the precise wording of the scholars?
?
This lesson shows that a single word can carry profound meaning. Are there areas in your life—relationships, work, or worship—where you take words in a narrow sense and miss the full weight of what is being said? Will you commit to listening more carefully, starting today?

The Impossibility of an Infinite Regress of Impediments
Sanusi argues that assuming an infinite chain of impediments leads to impossible events without a first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's argument that an infinite regress of impediments is impossible because it implies an endless chain of events.

Core Idea
Infinite regress of impediments leads to impossible endless events.

Sanusi presents a concise but crucial argument: if we try to push the cause of the world's existence back through an infinite chain of impediments, we end up with a series of events with no beginning. This is rationally impossible, reinforcing the need for a First Cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the original passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation carefully. Notice the logical structure: assumption leads to consequence, which is deemed impossible.
The argument is extremely condensed; each word carries weight.
2
Identify the key assumption and its consequence
The assumption is that the world's existence depends on the absence of an impediment, and that impediment itself depends on the absence of a prior impediment, and so on forever. The consequence is an infinite series of events (حوادث لا أول لها).
3
Understand why the consequence is impossible
An infinite series of events with no first event is impossible because it would mean that each event is preceded by another ad infinitum, which cannot actually occur. Sanusi references earlier proofs for this impossibility.
This argument is a standard refutation of infinite regress in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلا إذا فُرِضَ أَنَّهُ توقَّفَ على عدمِ مانعِ آخر قبلَهُ ، ثم كذلك ، فيكونُ هذا الفَرْضُ مستحيلاً أيضاً ؛ لما فيهِ مِنْ لزومِ حوادث لا أَوَّلَ لها ؛ لأنَّهُ حينَئِذٍ يكون وجود كل حادث منها مسبوقاً بارتفاع حادث آخر إلى غير نهاية ، وإنَّهُ محال ، وقد تقدَّمَ بعضُ براهينه .
Unless it is supposed that it depended on the absence of another impediment before it, then likewise, so this assumption is also impossible; because of the implication of events without a first; because then the existence of each event would be preceded by the cessation of another event ad infinitum, and that is impossible, and some of its proofs have preceded.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage presents a brief logical argument against the possibility of an infinite regress of impediments. The author argues that if one assumes that the existence of the world depends on the absence of an impediment, and that impediment itself depends on the absence of another prior impediment, and so on, then this leads to an infinite series of events (حوادث لا أول لها). Such an infinite series is impossible, as previously proven. The argument relies on the principle that an infinite regress of dependencies is untenable in kalam proofs for the contingency of the world.

Key Terms
توقف
مانع
حوادث لا أول لها

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the consequence of assuming that the impediment itself depends on another prior impediment?
A) The assumption is valid if the chain is infinite.
B) It leads to an infinite series of events, which is impossible.
C) It proves the eternity of the world.
D) It disproves the existence of a creator.

Key Takeaway
Assuming an infinite regress of impediments leads to an impossible endless chain of events, so the world must have a first cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?

The Proof from Comparing Past Events (Flood Argument)
Sanusi presents a clever proof using sets of past events to show an infinite past is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how comparing two sets of past events demonstrates that the past cannot be infinite.

Core Idea
The flood argument shows an infinite past leads to contradiction.

Sanusi presents a clever logical proof that the past must be finite. By comparing the set of events up to the flood with the set up to now, he shows that an infinite past would require the larger set to be equal to a proper subset, which is impossible. This proof is a classic example of reasoning about infinite sets in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Understand the setup
Identify the two sets: Set A = past events up to the flood, Set B = past events up to now. Note that Set B includes Set A plus extra events.
The flood is an arbitrary example; any point would work.
2
Align the ends
Place the end of both sets at the present moment. Then consider the other ends (the beginnings). There are two possibilities: either the beginnings match or they don't.
The comparison is between the two starting points once the ends are aligned.
3
Analyze the two possibilities
If no difference appears at the start, then Set A and Set B have the same size, which is impossible because Set B is larger. If a difference appears, then Set A must have a beginning, making it finite, and Set B is finite as well because it adds only a finite amount.
Both possibilities lead to the conclusion that the past is finite.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since the past must be finite, any assumption of an eternal or temporal impediment (like an infinite chain of events) is impossible.
This proves that the world must have a beginning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومِنْ أظرف ما استُدِلَ بهِ على استحالةِ حوادث لا أَوَّلَ لها : أنَّا إذا أخذنا الحوادث الماضية إلى زمن الطُّوفانِ جملةً واحدةً ، ثم أيضاً الحوادث الماضية إلى زماننا جملةً أخرى ، ثم طبقنا نهاية الجملة الأولى على نهاية الجملة الثانية .. فلا يخلو : إِمَّا أَنْ يظهر التفاوتُ مِنَ الجانب الآخر ، أو لا يظهرَ ؛ فإن لم يظهر فمحال ؛ لاستحالة أنْ تكونَ الجملةُ الناقصةُ مثل الجملة الزائدة ، وإِنْ ظهر لزم انقطاع الجملة الأولى وهي الجملة الناقصةُ ، فتكون متناهيةً ؛ لحصول مبدأ ومنتهى فيها ، وإذا تناهَتِ الجملةُ الأولى لزم تناهي الجملة الثانية أيضاً ؛ لأنَّ الثانية إنَّما زادَتْ على الأولى بقَدْرِ متناه ؛ وهو المقدار الذي مِنْ زمنِ الطُّوفانِ إلى زماننا ، والزائد على المتناهي بقدرٍ متناه يكون متناهياً ضرورةً . فقد ظهرَ لكَ بهذا : أنَّ تقدير المانع مطلقاً ـ أعني : قديماً أو حادثاً ـ مستحيل
And among the most elegant things used as evidence for the impossibility of events without a first is that we take the past events up to the time of the flood as one set, and also the past events up to our time as another set, then we align the end of the first set with the end of the second set. Then it is unavoidable: either a difference appears from the other side, or it does not appear. If it does not appear, it is impossible because it is impossible for the deficient set to be like the increased set. And if it appears, then the first set, which is the deficient set, must be cut off, so it is finite because it has a beginning and an end. And if the first set is finite, then the second set is also necessarily finite because the second set only exceeded the first by a finite amount, which is the amount from the time of the flood to our time, and that which exceeds a finite thing by a finite amount is necessarily finite. So it has become clear to you by this that the assumption of an impediment absolutely—that is, whether eternal or temporal—is impossible.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage contains a famous kalam proof against an infinite temporal past. The author compares two sets of past events: Set A from the beginning up to the time of Noah's flood, and Set B from the beginning up to the present. Since Set B contains Set A plus the interval from the flood to now, Set B is larger. If both sets were infinite, they would be equal in size, which contradicts Set B being larger. If they are not equal, then Set A must have an end, making it finite, and the finitude of Set A implies the finitude of Set B since the extra amount is finite. The conclusion is that the assumption of an eternal impediment (whether eternal or temporal) is impossible. This is a clear application of reasoning about infinite sets.

Key Terms
حوادث لا أول لها
الجملة الناقصة
الجملة الزائدة
متناه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof only works because the flood is a specific event.
Correction: The proof works for any arbitrarily chosen point in the past; the flood is just an example.

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of comparing the events up to the flood with the events up to the present?
A) To prove that the flood actually occurred.
B) To show that the past cannot be infinite.
C) To determine the date of the flood.
D) To demonstrate the superiority of the present era.

Key Takeaway
The assumption of an infinite past leads to logical contradictions when comparing sets with different endpoints.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Why the Maker Cannot Be an Eternal Nature
Sanusi refutes the claim that the world arises from an eternal nature via a future condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the supposition that the world arises from an eternal nature leads to impossible consequences, thereby proving the Maker must be a free agent.

Core Idea
An eternal nature with conditions leads to an infinite regress, which is impossible.

Sanusi presents and refutes a second objection against the necessity of a free Creator. The objector suggests that the Maker could be an eternal nature from which the world arises once a missing condition is fulfilled. Sanusi shows that this line of reasoning forces an infinite chain of conditions, resulting in contradictions that cannot be resolved. This lesson walks through that refutation and its crucial conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, focusing on the objection that the Maker is an eternal nature with a missing condition. Note how it mirrors the first objection but differs in the type of impediment.
2
Identify the refutation's first move
Sanusi responds by transferring the discussion to the temporality of the condition itself. Understand why this is necessary: the condition must be created, yet the nature is eternal.
This forces the objector to explain the condition's delay.
3
Follow the two possible responses
The objector can either posit an eternal impediment or another temporal condition. Trace how each leads to impossibility or infinite regress.
The eternal impediment makes the condition impossible; a new temporal condition requires another condition ad infinitum.
4
Understand the three impossible outcomes
Sanusi summarizes that a non-free cause leads to one of three impossibilities: pre-eternity of the world, infinite regress of simultaneous conditions, or infinite successive events. Grasp why each is impossible.
This is the core argument: all three are absurd, so the cause must be a free agent.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Connect the refutation back to the lesson's objective: the only coherent alternative is that the Maker is an agent by choice, not an essence or nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني مِنَ الاعتراضين : أنَّا نقدِّرُ الصانع طبيعة أزلية ، وإنَّما لم يُوجَدْ مَعَها العالم في الأزلِ لتوقُّفِ وجودِهِ على شرط لم يكن في الأزلِ ، وحينَ وُجِدَ الشرطُ فيما لا يزالُ وُجِدَ العالم عنِ الطبيعة وهذا الاعتراض قريبٌ مِنَ الأَوَّلِ ؛ إلا أنَّ المانع في الأَوَّلِ وجودي ، وفي الثاني عدمي ، وهو عدم الشرط وجوابه : أنَّ هذا الاعتراض فاسد أيضاً ؛ لوجوب نقلِ الكلام إلى حدوث ذلك الشرط مع أن الطبيعة المؤثرة فيه وفي غيره قديمة . فإن أجاب عن تأخُرِهِ أيضاً بتقديرِ مانع أزلي .. لزمه ما سبق ؛ وهو ذلك المانع الأزلي ، فيستحيل وجود الشرط المتوقف على عدمه ، ويلزم أن يستحيل أيضاً وجود العالم الموقوفِ على وجودِ الشرط الذي اتضحَتِ استحالته . استحاله عدم وإنْ أجابَ بتقديرِ شرط آخرَ حادثٍ نقلنا الكلام إليه ، ولزم فيهِ ما لزمَ في الأَوَّلِ ، وذلكَ يُؤدّي إلى تسلسلِ شروط لا نهاية لها مجتمعة كلها في آن واحدٍ ؛ لأنَّه يلزم احتياج كلّ شرط إلى شرط مقارن له إلى النهاية ، وهذا خلافُ ما يلزمُ في تقدير الموانع الحادثة ؛ فإنَّ اللوازم فيه حوادث متعاقبة لا أوَّلَ لها ، وليست تجتمع في آن واحد ، كما لزم ذلك في تقديرِ الشروط الحادثة . وبالجملة : فاللازم في تقديرِ مُوجِدِ العالمِ مُوجِداً بالذاتِ علَّةً أو طبيعة ، لا فاعلاً بالاختيار . . أحد أمورٍ ثلاثة : إمَّا قدم العالم، أو التسلسل معَ الاقتران ، أو حوادث متعاقبة لا أوَّلَ لها والثلاثة مستحيلة على القطع ، فكونُ مُوجِدِ العالمِ مُوجِداً
The second of the two objections: that we suppose the Maker to be an eternal nature, and the world did not exist with it in pre-eternity only because its existence depended on a condition that did not exist in pre-eternity, and when the condition came into existence in the future, the world came into existence from the nature. And this objection is close to the first, except that the impediment in the first was existent, while in the second it is non-existent, namely the absence of the condition. Its answer: that this objection is also invalid, because it is necessary to transfer the discussion to the temporality of that condition, while the nature that is effective in it and in other things is eternal. If he answers about its delay also by assuming an eternal impediment, then what preceded is necessary for him, namely that eternal impediment, so the existence of the condition that depends on its non-existence becomes impossible, and it also necessitates the impossibility of the world's existence which depends on the existence of the condition whose impossibility has become clear. And if he answers by assuming another temporal condition, we transfer the discussion to it, and what was necessary in the first case is necessary for it, and that leads to a chain of conditions with no end, all of them coexisting at one instant; because each condition would need a simultaneous condition ad infinitum, and this is contrary to what is necessary in the assumption of temporal impediments, for the necessary implications in that are successive events without a first, and they do not coexist at one instant, as was necessary in the assumption of temporal conditions. And in summary: the necessary consequence in supposing the efficient cause of the world to be an efficient cause essentially, a cause or a nature, not an agent by choice, is one of three things: either the pre-eternity of the world, or an infinite regress with coexistence, or successive events without a first. And the three divisions are absolutely impossible, so that the efficient cause of the world is an efficient cause...
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the second objection that the world could be produced by an eternal nature (طبيعة أزلية) rather than a free agent. The objection claims that the world did not exist eternally with the nature because a condition was missing, and once that condition arose, the world emanated from the nature. The author responds that this argument forces an infinite regress of conditions. If the condition's delay is explained by an eternal impediment, that leads to impossibility; if by another temporal condition, that condition itself requires another condition, leading to a simultaneous infinite chain of conditions. The author then summarizes that assuming a non-free cause (nature or essence) leads to one of three impossible outcomes: pre-eternity of the world, an infinite regress of simultaneous conditions, or an infinite series of successive events. Since all are impossible, the maker must be a free agent (فاعل بالاختيار).

Key Terms

شرط
مانع
تسلسل
فاعل بالاختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An eternal nature with a missing condition can explain the world's origin without requiring a free agent.
Correction: The condition itself must be created, leading to an infinite regress or other impossible outcomes, so a free agent is necessary.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude is the necessary consequence of supposing the maker to be an eternal nature rather than a free agent?
A) The world must be eternal.
B) One of three impossible scenarios must follow.
C) The nature must be temporal.
D) The world does not exist.

Key Takeaway
Assuming an eternal nature as the cause of the world leads to an impossible infinite regress, proving the Maker must be a free agent.

What will you do today to acknowledge His personal choice in your life?
?
The refutation shows that any attempt to explain the world without a free Creator leads to impossible regresses. Are there areas in your life where you try to explain away God's personal involvement—such as attributing your successes to mere chance or your failures to impersonal fate? How will you actively replace those thoughts with trust in His free will?
?
Sanusi proves that the world's existence depends on a condition that only a free Agent can provide. If you truly believe that your very being is contingent on God's free choice, does your prayer life reflect that dependence? When was the last time you prayed with the raw awareness that you could cease to exist at any moment if He did not sustain you? What will you change in your connection with Him starting today?

Proof that the Creator is a Free Agent
Sanusi refutes the objection about a missing condition and concludes that the Creator must be a free agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the objection about a missing condition and proves that the Creator must be a free agent by showing that the alternative leads to impossibilities.

Core Idea
If the Creator were a necessary cause, the world could not exist—only free will makes creation possible.

This passage is the climax of Sanusi's proof that the Creator is a free agent. Having earlier refuted the objection of an eternal obstacle, he now deals with a second objection about a missing condition. By systematically dismantling the alternatives, Sanusi shows that only the conclusion that the Creator acts by choice is rationally sound.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: objection, answer, summary.
2
Identify the two types of objection
The first objection had an existential obstacle; this one has a non-existential obstacle (absence of condition). Follow the refutation.
3
Trace the two branches of the refutation
The opponent can posit either an eternal obstacle or another temporal condition. Each leads to impossibility or infinite regress.
4
Understand the three impossible implications
The summary lists three possible outcomes if the Creator were a necessary cause: eternal world, infinite regress of simultaneous conditions, or infinite successive events. All are impossible, so the Creator must be free.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا الاعتراض قريبٌ مِنَ الأَوَّلِ ؛ إلا أنَّ المانع في الأَوَّلِ وجودي ، وفي الثاني عدمي ، وهو عدم الشرط. وجوابه : أنَّ هذا الاعتراض فاسد أيضاً ؛ لوجوب نقلِ الكلام إلى حدوث ذلك الشرط مع أن الطبيعة المؤثرة فيه وفي غيره قديمة . فإن أجاب عن تأخُرِهِ أيضاً بتقديرِ مانع أزلي .. لزمه ما سبق ؛ وهو ذلك المانع الأزلي ، فيستحيل وجود الشرط المتوقف على عدمه ، ويلزم أن يستحيل أيضاً وجود العالم الموقوفِ على وجودِ الشرط الذي اتضحَتِ استحالته . وإنْ أجابَ بتقديرِ شرط آخرَ حادثٍ نقلنا الكلام إليه ، ولزم فيهِ ما لزمَ في الأَوَّلِ ، وذلكَ يُؤدّي إلى تسلسلِ شروط لا نهاية لها مجتمعة كلها في آن واحدٍ ؛ لأنَّه يلزم احتياج كلّ شرط إلى شرط مقارن له إلى النهاية ، وهذا خلافُ ما يلزمُ في تقدير الموانع الحادثة ؛ فإنَّ اللوازم فيه حوادث متعاقبة لا أوَّلَ لها ، وليست تجتمع في آن واحد. وبالجملة : فاللازم في تقديرِ مُوجِدِ العالمِ مُوجِداً بالذاتِ علَّةً أو طبيعة ، لا فاعلاً بالاختيار . . أحد أمورٍ ثلاثة : إمَّا قدم العالم، أو التسلسل معَ الاقتران ، أو حوادث متعاقبة لا أوَّلَ لها. والأقسام الثلاثةُ مستحيلة على القطع ، فكونُ مُوجِدِ العالمِ مُوجِداً بالذاتِ علَّةً أو طبيعةً مستحيل على القطع ، فتعيَّنَ أَنَّهُ فاعل بالاختيار وهو المطلوب ، ﴿ وَرَبُّكَ يَخْلُقُ مَا يَشَاءُ وَيَخْتَارُ مَا كَانَ لَهُمُ الْخِيَرَةُ سُبْحَانَ اللَّهِ وَتَعَالَى عَمَّا يُشْرِكُونَ ﴾ [القصص : ٦٨] .
And this objection is similar to the first, except that the obstacle in the first is existential, and in the second is non-existential, namely the absence of the condition. And its answer: that this objection is also invalid, because it is necessary to transfer the discussion to the occurrence of that condition, despite the nature affecting it and other things being eternal. For if he answers about its delay also by positing an eternal obstacle, then what preceded follows - namely that eternal obstacle, so the existence of the condition dependent on its non-existence becomes impossible, and it follows that the existence of the world dependent on the existence of the condition whose impossibility has been established is also impossible. And if he answers by positing another temporal condition, we transfer the discussion to it, and what followed in the first follows in it, and that leads to an infinite regress of conditions all simultaneously together, because it necessitates each condition needing a concomitant condition ad infinitum, and this differs from what follows in positing temporal obstacles, for the implications in that are successive events without beginning, and they do not all exist at once. In summary: what follows from positing that the originator of the world is an originator per se, a cause or nature, not a free agent, is one of three things: either the eternity of the world, or an infinite regress with simultaneity, or successive events without beginning. And the three divisions are absolutely impossible, so the existence of the originator of the world as an originator per se, a cause or nature, is absolutely impossible. Thus it is determined that He is a free agent. That is what is sought. "And your Lord creates what He wills and chooses; they have no choice. Exalted is Allah and high above what they associate [with Him]." [Al-Qasas 28:68].
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection and a decisive proof that the Creator must be a free agent (fā‘il bi’l-ikhtiyār) rather than a necessary cause (mutillah). The author first identifies the objection: it is similar to a previous one but with a non-existential obstacle (i.e., the absence of a condition). The answer is that the objection fails because we can demand the occurrence of that condition, and the nature (al-ṭabī‘ah) that would cause it is eternal. If the opponent tries to delay the condition by positing an eternal obstacle, that obstacle would itself be impossible to remove, making the condition impossible and thus the world impossible. If the opponent posits another temporal condition, we push the discussion to it, leading to an infinite regress of simultaneous conditions (tasalsul ma‘a al-iqtirān). This is distinct from temporal obstacles, which lead to an infinite series of successive events without beginning. The author then summarizes: positing that the world's originator is a cause per se yields three impossible outcomes: (1) the eternity of the world, (2) an infinite regress of simultaneous conditions, (3) an infinite series of successive events. All three are absolutely impossible, so the originator must be a free agent.

Key Terms
فاعل بالاختيار
علة أو طبيعة
تسلسل
مانع أزلي
شرط حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a necessary cause could produce the world without free will.
Correction: This argument shows that a necessary cause leads to impossibilities like infinite regress or eternal world, so the Creator must act by choice.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, if the creator were a necessary cause, which of the following is NOT one of the three possible implications?
A) The eternity of the world
B) An infinite regress with simultaneity
C) An infinite regress of successive events without beginning
D) The world being created out of nothing

Key Takeaway
The Creator must be a free agent because the alternative—being a necessary cause—leads to logical impossibilities.

?
The argument eliminates any possibility of the world being eternal or caused by blind nature. Do you ever catch yourself living as if this world is ultimate or purposeless? How can this logical certainty reshape your priorities today?

The Universality of Divine Will
Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazila on divine will and asserts its universality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari position holds that Allah's will is universal for all events, including those He does not command.

Core Idea
Allah wills everything that occurs; nothing in His dominion happens without His will.

Sanusi begins by addressing a misinterpretation of the verse by the Mu'tazila, who restrict Allah's will to what He commands. He then establishes the Ash'ari principle that all events are created by Allah alone, leading to the necessary conclusion that His will is universal. This section refutes a key Mu'tazila error and clarifies a foundational tenet of Sunni theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mu'tazila claim
Read the opening sentence carefully. The Mu'tazila interpret the verse as 'I only intended worship' because they believe Allah's will aligns with His command, thus denying that He wills disbelief and sins.
2
Understand the Ash'ari refutation
Sanusi argues that since all events occur only by Allah's creation, with no effect from anyone else, His will must encompass all of them. This is a logical deduction from the principle of divine power and creation.
The universality of will parallels the universality of power.
3
Grasp the conclusion
The passage ends with a declaration of divine transcendence: Allah is exalted above having in His dominion what He does not will. This is the direct refutation of the Mu'tazila position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التقدير أيضاً: وما أردتُ بخلق الجن والإنس إلا العبادة، على أصلهم الفاسد أيضاً في أن إرادة الله تعالى على وفق أمره، فما لا يأمر به من الكفر والمعاصي فليس بمراد له عندهم. وإذا حققت: أن الحوادث كلها إنما وقعت بخلق الله تعالى، لا أثر لمن سواه في شيء منها .. عرفت وجوب عموم إرادته تعالى لجميعها، كما وجب عموم قدرته تعالى لها، فتعالى أن يكون في ملكه ما لا يريد. ويصح أن تجعل اللام في قوله تعالى: "ليعبدون" للتعليل مجازاً على طريق الاستعارة التبعية; بأن يشبه الأمر بالعبادة في ترتب وجوده على خلق الجن والإنس.. بالعلة الغائية في ترتب وجودها على معلولها، فأدخلت لام التعليل على العبادة لتدل على ذلك. والعلة الغائية في الاصطلاح: هي ما يبعث بحسب تصوره على فعل شيء وإن كان يتأخر وجوده على ذلك الشيء; كالربح للتجارة; فإنه علة غائية لها; إذ هو الحامل باعتبار تصوره على التلبس بالتجارة وإن كان في الوجود يتأخر عنها. وبالجملة: فالعلة الغائية هي فائدة الشيء، وهي أبداً تتقدم ذهناً وتتأخر وجوداً في الخارج، وهي التي تريد الفلاسفة بقولهم: أول الفكر آخر العمل، فهذه العلة الغائية الباعثة بها شبه الأمر بالعبادة. (١) في هامش (ح): (وجه الشبه: مطلق الترتب) (٢) انظر أدب الكاتب لابن قتيبة (ص ٨).
And also the estimation: 'I did not intend by creating the jinn and mankind except worship,' according to their corrupt principle that Allah's will is in accordance with His command, so what He does not command of disbelief and sins is not willed by them. But if you investigate that all events occur only by the creation of Allah Almighty, no one else has any effect on any of them, you would know the obligation of the universality of His will for all of them, just as the universality of His power for them is necessary. So exalted is He above having in His dominion what He does not will. And it is permissible to make the lam in His statement 'liya'budūn' for purpose metaphorically by way of subsidiary metaphor (isti'āra taba'iyya); by likening the command to worship, in the dependence of its existence upon the creation of jinn and mankind, to the final cause (al-'illa al-ghā'iyya) in the dependence of its existence upon its effect. So the lam of purpose was inserted onto worship to indicate that. And the final cause in technical terminology is: that which, by virtue of its conception, motivates the doing of a thing, even if its existence is posterior to that thing; like profit for trade; for it is a final cause for it, since it is the motivator, by virtue of its conception, to engage in trade, even though in existence it is posterior to it. In summary: the final cause is the benefit (fā'ida) of a thing, and it always precedes in the mind and is posterior in external existence. It is what the philosophers mean by their saying: 'The first of thought is the last of action.' So this final cause that motivates is what the command to worship was likened to.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila on the issue of divine will and an alternative metaphorical interpretation of the verse. The author begins by restating the Mu'tazila position: they interpret the verse as 'I only intended worship' because they believe Allah's will follows His command; thus, they deny that Allah wills disbelief and sins. The author then refutes this by stating the Ash'ari principle: all events (ḥawādith) occur only by Allah's creation, with no effect from anyone else. Therefore, Allah's will must be universal for all events, just as His power is universal. So it is impossible for something in His dominion to occur that He does not will. This refutes the Mu'tazila view that He does not will sins. Then the author offers a permissible metaphorical interpretation of the lam in 'liya'budūn'. It can be taken as lam of purpose but metaphorically (isti'āra taba'iyya). The analogy: the command to worship is compared to a final cause (al-'illa al-ghā'iyya). Just as a final cause (like profit for trade) is a mental motivator that precedes in conception but is posterior in existence, the command to worship is something that the creation leads to in terms of outcome. So the verse means that the creation of jinn and humans results in the fact that they are commanded to worship, not that worship was the purpose. The author then defines the final cause as that which motivates by its conception, even if it comes later in existence. Example: profit motivates trade but comes after trade. He concludes that the final cause is the benefit of a thing, always preceding mentally but coming later in external existence, as the philosophers say. This interpretation avoids attributing a real purpose to Allah while still making sense of the verse.

Key Terms
إرادة الله
عموم الإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Allah's will is limited to what He commands, and that He does not will sins or disbelief.
Correction: In Ash'ari theology, Allah's will is universal; He wills all that occurs, including sins, because nothing happens outside His creation and permission.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why must Allah's will be universal for all events?
A) Because the verse explicitly says so
B) Because all events occur only by Allah's creation, so He must will them
C) Because the Mu'tazila agree
D) Because philosophers deduced it

Key Takeaway
Allah's will is universal; He wills everything that occurs in His dominion, including acts of disobedience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every event, including your own sins, occurs only by Allah's will, how does that change the way you view your past mistakes? Do you see them as part of a divine plan, or do you still feel they were outside His control? What does this mean for your repentance?
?
You are tempted to sin right now. Pause and ask yourself: Is this temptation occurring without Allah's will? If you submit to it, are you acting against His will or within it? How does the truth of universal divine will affect your sense of personal responsibility?
?
The passage says Allah is exalted above having anything in His dominion that He does not will. Look at a difficulty or grief you are facing today. Can you truly accept that it was willed by Allah? If not, what does that reveal about your understanding of His lordship? Will you now surrender to His will in this matter?

Metaphorical Interpretation Using Final Cause
Sanusi provides a metaphorical interpretation of 'liya'budūn' using the final cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verse 'liya'budūn' can be interpreted metaphorically through the concept of final cause, preserving Allah's transcendence.

Core Idea
The command to worship is like a final cause: it motivates but comes after the creation.

Having established the universality of Allah's will, Sanusi now addresses the apparent purpose in the verse. He offers a metaphorical interpretation that avoids attributing a real purpose to Allah, using the philosophical concept of final cause. This interpretation allows the verse to be understood in a way consistent with Ash'ari theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the metaphor
Sanusi says the lam in 'liya'budūn' can be taken as a lam of purpose metaphorically. He uses a subsidiary metaphor (isti'āra taba'iyya) likening the command to worship to a final cause.
A subsidiary metaphor involves transferring a word from its original meaning to a related meaning.
2
Understand the definition of final cause
The final cause is that which, by its conception, motivates an action, even though its existence comes later. The example is profit: it motivates trade but occurs after the trade.
3
Grasp the philosophical maxim
Sanusi cites the philosophers' saying: 'The first of thought is the last of action.' This captures the nature of final cause—conceived first but realized last.
4
Connect to the verse
The command to worship is likened to this final cause: the creation of jinn and humans leads to the command to worship as its outcome, not that worship was the literal purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التقدير أيضاً: وما أردتُ بخلق الجن والإنس إلا العبادة، على أصلهم الفاسد أيضاً في أن إرادة الله تعالى على وفق أمره، فما لا يأمر به من الكفر والمعاصي فليس بمراد له عندهم. وإذا حققت: أن الحوادث كلها إنما وقعت بخلق الله تعالى، لا أثر لمن سواه في شيء منها .. عرفت وجوب عموم إرادته تعالى لجميعها، كما وجب عموم قدرته تعالى لها، فتعالى أن يكون في ملكه ما لا يريد. ويصح أن تجعل اللام في قوله تعالى: "ليعبدون" للتعليل مجازاً على طريق الاستعارة التبعية; بأن يشبه الأمر بالعبادة في ترتب وجوده على خلق الجن والإنس.. بالعلة الغائية في ترتب وجودها على معلولها، فأدخلت لام التعليل على العبادة لتدل على ذلك. والعلة الغائية في الاصطلاح: هي ما يبعث بحسب تصوره على فعل شيء وإن كان يتأخر وجوده على ذلك الشيء; كالربح للتجارة; فإنه علة غائية لها; إذ هو الحامل باعتبار تصوره على التلبس بالتجارة وإن كان في الوجود يتأخر عنها. وبالجملة: فالعلة الغائية هي فائدة الشيء، وهي أبداً تتقدم ذهناً وتتأخر وجوداً في الخارج، وهي التي تريد الفلاسفة بقولهم: أول الفكر آخر العمل، فهذه العلة الغائية الباعثة بها شبه الأمر بالعبادة. (١) في هامش (ح): (وجه الشبه: مطلق الترتب) (٢) انظر أدب الكاتب لابن قتيبة (ص ٨).
And also the estimation: 'I did not intend by creating the jinn and mankind except worship,' according to their corrupt principle that Allah's will is in accordance with His command, so what He does not command of disbelief and sins is not willed by them. But if you investigate that all events occur only by the creation of Allah Almighty, no one else has any effect on any of them, you would know the obligation of the universality of His will for all of them, just as the universality of His power for them is necessary. So exalted is He above having in His dominion what He does not will. And it is permissible to make the lam in His statement 'liya'budūn' for purpose metaphorically by way of subsidiary metaphor (isti'āra taba'iyya); by likening the command to worship, in the dependence of its existence upon the creation of jinn and mankind, to the final cause (al-'illa al-ghā'iyya) in the dependence of its existence upon its effect. So the lam of purpose was inserted onto worship to indicate that. And the final cause in technical terminology is: that which, by virtue of its conception, motivates the doing of a thing, even if its existence is posterior to that thing; like profit for trade; for it is a final cause for it, since it is the motivator, by virtue of its conception, to engage in trade, even though in existence it is posterior to it. In summary: the final cause is the benefit (fā'ida) of a thing, and it always precedes in the mind and is posterior in external existence. It is what the philosophers mean by their saying: 'The first of thought is the last of action.' So this final cause that motivates is what the command to worship was likened to.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila on the issue of divine will and an alternative metaphorical interpretation of the verse. The author begins by restating the Mu'tazila position: they interpret the verse as 'I only intended worship' because they believe Allah's will follows His command; thus, they deny that Allah wills disbelief and sins. The author then refutes this by stating the Ash'ari principle: all events (ḥawādith) occur only by Allah's creation, with no effect from anyone else. Therefore, Allah's will must be universal for all events, just as His power is universal. So it is impossible for something in His dominion to occur that He does not will. This refutes the Mu'tazila view that He does not will sins. Then the author offers a permissible metaphorical interpretation of the lam in 'liya'budūn'. It can be taken as lam of purpose but metaphorically (isti'āra taba'iyya). The analogy: the command to worship is compared to a final cause (al-'illa al-ghā'iyya). Just as a final cause (like profit for trade) is a mental motivator that precedes in conception but is posterior in existence, the command to worship is something that the creation leads to in terms of outcome. So the verse means that the creation of jinn and humans results in the fact that they are commanded to worship, not that worship was the purpose. The author then defines the final cause as that which motivates by its conception, even if it comes later in existence. Example: profit motivates trade but comes after trade. He concludes that the final cause is the benefit of a thing, always preceding mentally but coming later in external existence, as the philosophers say. This interpretation avoids attributing a real purpose to Allah while still making sense of the verse.

Key Terms
الاستعارة التبعية
العلة الغائية
أول الفكر آخر العمل

Key Takeaway
The 'lam' in the verse is metaphorical; the command to worship is likened to a final cause, indicating that worship is the intended outcome, not a literal purpose.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you worship, do you ever catch yourself expecting some immediate 'profit'—like worldly peace or material gain—instead of worshipping solely because Allah commanded it? How does the final cause analogy expose that tendency in your own heart?
?
If the command to worship is not a literal purpose but a metaphorical final cause—an intended outcome—does that change how you view your own role in the universe? Are you ready to live as one who was created for worship, not because Allah needed it, but because that is the very benefit and perfection of your own existence?
?
The philosophers say: 'The first of thought is the last of action.' In your life, what is the 'last of action'—the ultimate outcome you are actually striving for? Is it truly worship of Allah, or something else that has subtly taken its place?

The Design of the Eye as Proof of Divine Wisdom
Sanusi details the intricate design of the eye to refute chance and affirm divine wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the intricate design of the human eye, as described by Sanusi, serves as a rational proof for the existence and perfect wisdom of God, and why attributing such design to chance is irrational.

Core Idea
The eye's design is so intricate that only a Wise Creator could have made it—chance is irrational.

Sanusi presents a detailed argument from design, focusing on the human eye. He enumerates multiple features—placement, eyelids, eyelashes, tears, layers, and the optic nerve—each with a clear purpose. This argument aims to demonstrate that such sophisticated design cannot be the product of coincidence or human making, but must come from a Wise Creator. Before we dive into the specific features, Sanusi sets the logical stage: denying this necessity is to leave the realm of rational discourse.

Guided Path
1
Read and identify the logical claim
Read the passage once, noting the initial strong claim: anyone who thinks these wonders could come by coincidence has left rationality.
This sets the stakes for the entire argument.
2
Trace the enumerated design features
Go through the passage and list each design feature Sanusi mentions, from the eye's placement to the optic nerve. Notice the pattern of 'then' (ثم) introducing each new feature.
There are about eight distinct features, each with a specific purpose.
3
Reflect on the cumulative argument
Ask yourself: why does Sanusi list so many details? The cumulative effect is meant to overwhelm the reader with evidence, making chance attribution absurd.
The argument is not just from complexity but from the sheer number of coordinated features.

فالفَوقانيُّ منهما يمنعُ ما يأتي مِنَ الهباء إلى العينِ مِنْ جهةِ العلوّ ، والآخرُ منهما يمنع أيضاً مثل ذلك مما يلي مِنَ جهةِ السُّفْل. وجعل تعالى أيضاً عليها مثلَ دُرْبُوزِ فوقها، وعلى حاجبها شَعَرُ The intellects are incapable of comprehending the least of them. And whoever deems it possible that those wonders, with their abundance and being beyond enumeration, should issue from an ignorant person by way of coincidence, is an obstinate opponent of truth, a denier of the necessary, and his argumentation is nullified because he has exited the realm of rational people. And whoever contemplates the human being, whose proportion to the rest of the world is like nothing, and looks at the smallest organ of his organs, such as his eye, for example, sees in that the most amazing wonder. So let him reflect upon it: how God Almighty placed it in the top of his head and in its front, so that he may see with it the far and the near, and He did not make it protruding on the surface of his face as He did with his nose, but rather He, glorified, placed it in a corner of it, so that harm would reach it less, because of its intense receptivity usually, due to its moisture, polish, and clarity, and similar attributes with which God Almighty composed it. Then He Almighty placed over it two fleshy, moist, soft coverings that close over it and polish it, and sweep away what attaches to it of dust, which if it accumulated in it would normally harm it. And the human moves them by God's power and will, opening and closing, throughout the passage of times and succession of hours, without intention or seeking, and without effort in most general situations. Then He Almighty, with His perfect wisdom, placed at the edges of each one of them woven, thick hair, with each one of them having a length, and extended them outward from them to an extent according to their usual need. So the upper one prevents what comes of dust to the eye from above, and the other one also prevents similar from below. And He Almighty also placed over it something like a ledge above it, and on its eyebrow woven hair also, because the abundance of harm usually comes from above it. Then He Almighty supplied it with salty water intrinsic to it, to preserve it in that case, and to reduce its acceptance of decay with it usually, unlike what He placed in the mouth from the gushing source of fresh water constantly. And He also made the place of perception in it in its middle, which is the amount in which the observer sees himself due to its intense clarity and polish. Then He made it having seven layers, each one different from the other, and arranged them in a particular arrangement, with each layer a benefit usually not found in another, nor in a different arrangement. And likewise He placed in it three moistures of different forms: thick, thin, and intermediate. And more wondrous and subtle than all this is what God Almighty supplied it with from the brain, which is called, according to the terminology of the naturalists, the visual power: that is, in the eye there is a vein connecting from it to the brain, thinner than a spider's web, which no one sees except
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is a proof from design (itqan al-san' or al-nizam), specifically focusing on the intricate construction of the human eye to argue for the existence and perfect wisdom of God. The author, Sanusi, begins by asserting that minds cannot fully grasp even the least of these wonders. He then makes a strong claim: anyone who claims that such abundant and uncountable wonders could emanate from an ignorant person (a human) by mere coincidence is an obstinate opponent of truth and a denier of what is self-evident. Such a person's argument is invalid because he has left the realm of rational beings. This sets up the conclusion before the detailed evidence. After this, Sanusi invites the reader to contemplate the human being, whose smallness relative to the universe is negligible, and then focus on a tiny organ, the eye. He lists numerous design features in sequence. First, the eye is placed at the top and front of the head for optimal vision of far and near. It is not protruding like the nose but set in a socket to reduce harm. Second, the eyelids are described as two fleshy, moist, soft coverings that close, polish, and sweep away dust. Their movement is by God's power and will, without effort. Third, eyelashes are woven thick hair at the edges, with the upper lashes blocking dust from above and lower lashes from below. Fourth, there is a ledge above the eye and an eyebrow with woven hair, because most harm comes from above. Fifth, the eye is supplied with salty tears to preserve it and prevent decay, unlike the fresh water in the mouth. Sixth, the center of the eye is the locus of perception, polished and clear enough for an observer to see himself. Seventh, the eye has seven distinct layers, each with a unique benefit, arranged in a specific order, and three types of moisture (thick, thin, intermediate). Finally, the most wondrous is the connection from the eye to the brain via a vein thinner than a spider's web, which carries the visual power. This detailed enumeration of features, each with a clear purpose, is intended to demonstrate that such design cannot result from chance or human ability; it necessitates a Wise Creator. The argument follows the standard kalam approach of making the unseen (God) known through the seen (creation).

القوة الباصرة
الإحاطة
الاتفاق
الهباء
شعر منسوج
طبقات سبع

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument for God's existence from the eye's design relies solely on its complexity, but Sanusi emphasizes that the wonders are beyond enumeration and that attributing them to chance is irrational—not just improbable.
Correction: The argument is not merely about complexity; it is about the impossibility of such coordinated, purposeful features arising from coincidence. Sanusi considers this a logical necessity, not just a probabilistic one.

Key Takeaway
The eye's intricate, purposeful design—from its placement to the optic nerve—cannot be explained by chance; it demands a Wise Creator.

?

Proof of Divine Unity and Meaning of the Shahada
Sanusi proves God's necessary unity and interprets the shahada as negation of creaturely attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses the shahada to prove God's unity and what it negates from creation.

Core Idea
The shahada negates all creaturely attributes from God, affirming His unique divinity.

This passage builds on earlier discussions to present the proof of God's necessary unity. Sanusi explains that the shahada is not just a denial of other gods but a comprehensive negation of all temporal attributes from Allah, leaving only His eternal, necessary existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the flow from proof to explanation of the shahada.
The passage has some OCR issues; focus on the main ideas.
2
Identify the proof structure
Find where Sanusi states the conclusion and why he delayed this topic. Understand that the proof relies on prior concepts.
The delay is because the proof depends on many preceding topics.
3
Analyze the shahada's composition
Notice that the shahada has two parts: negation and affirmation. Determine what is negated and what is affirmed.
What is negated is divinity and its properties from other than Allah.
4
List the negated attributes
From the passage, list the specific meanings included in the shahada's negation: no eternal but Allah, no necessary of continuation but Allah, no different from temporal things but Allah, and the explanation of that difference.
The list includes being a body, subsisting in a body, direction, modality, etc.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليل على وجوب الوحدانية له جل وعلا و وجوب استناد الكائنات كلها إليه ابتداء بلا واسطة آلة له منها ولامعين وأنه ليس في الوجود إلا الله سبحانه وأفعاله إنَّما أَخَرَ الكلامَ في الوحدانية إلى هذا الموضع ؛ لتوقف برهانها على كثير مما سبق (١) وأيضاً : فكلمة التوحيد - وهي : ( لا إله إلا الله ) ـ لمَّا كَانَتْ مُركَّبَةً مِنْ نفي وإثباتِ ، ولا شك أنَّ الذي نُفِيَ عن غيرِ مولانا جلَّ وعز ، وأُثبِتَ لهُ تعالى على سبيل الحصر هو الألوهية وخواصها ، حتى يتضمَّنُ قولنا : ( لا إله إلا الله ) معنى : لا قديم في ذاتِهِ وصفاتِهِ إلا الله تعالى ، ولا واجبَ البقاء فيهما إلا الله جل وعلا ، ولا مخالف للحوادثِ كلّها إلا الله تعالى ؛ بمعنى : أَنَّهُ ليسَ بجِرْمٍ ولا قائم بالجِرْمِ ، ولا في جهةٍ مِنَ الجهات ولا له جهةٌ ، ولا يُكيَّفُ ولا يُتوهم ، ولا قائم بنفسِهِ - بمعنى : أَنَّهُ لا غني عن المحل (١) إذ حقها أن تذكر مع صفات السلوب
The proof of the necessity of unity for Him, the Mighty and Majestic, and the necessity of all contingent things being attributed to Him initially without any instrument from them or any assistant being a mediator, and that there is nothing in existence except Allah, the Glorious, and His actions. He only delayed the discussion of unity to this place because its proof depends on much that preceded. And also: the word of tawhid, which is 'La ilaha illa Allah', since it is composed of negation and affirmation, and there is no doubt that what is negated from other than our Master, the Mighty and Majestic, and affirmed for Him, the Most High, in the way of exclusivity, is divinity (al-uluhiyya) and its properties, such that our saying 'La ilaha illa Allah' includes the meaning: there is no eternal in essence and attributes except Allah, the Most High; and no necessary of continuation in both except Allah, the Mighty and Majestic; and no different from all temporal things except Allah, the Most High; meaning: He is not a body nor subsisting in a body, nor in a direction from directions nor does He have a direction, and He is not qualified (by modality) nor imagined, and He is not self-subsisting - meaning: He is not independent of a locus. (1) For it is proper that it be mentioned with the attributes of negation.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage provides the proof for God's unity and explains the meaning of the shahada. It begins by stating the conclusion: that God is necessarily one, and all things are dependent on Him directly without intermediates. The author then explains why the discussion of unity was delayed: because its proof relies on many prior topics. Next, he analyses the phrase 'La ilaha illa Allah', noting it is composed of negation and affirmation. What is negated from other than God and affirmed for Him exclusively is divinity (al-uluhiyya) and its properties. This phrase includes several meanings: negative attributes such as that only Allah is eternal in essence and attributes, only He is necessarily perpetual, and only He is different from all temporal things. This difference is explained as: He is not a body, not subsisting in a body, not in any direction, not qualified by modality, not imagined, and not self-subsisting (though the explanation given for self-subsisting is textually problematic; it states 'not independent of a locus,' which contradicts standard Ash'ari doctrine. This is likely an OCR error, and the intended meaning is that He is not in need of a locus). The footnote indicates that this discussion properly belongs with the negative attributes.

Key Terms
الوحدانية
كلمة التوحيد
لا إله إلا الله
نفي وإثبات
الألوهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The shahada only denies the existence of other gods.
Correction: Sanusi explains that the negation in the shahada is broader: it negates all creaturely attributes (eternity, necessity, corporeality, etc.) from anyone other than Allah.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what does the negation part of 'La ilaha illa Allah' primarily negate from other than Allah?
A) Existence of any other being.
B) Divinity and its properties.
C) Power to create.
D) Eternity.

Key Takeaway
The shahada is a comprehensive declaration of God's unity that negates all creaturely attributes from Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'La ilaha illa Allah', do you truly negate every creaturely attribute from Allah in your heart, or do you still imagine Him in some form? Take a moment to examine your inner state and ask Allah to purify your tawhid.
?
The shahada means Allah is not like any created thing. Is there any aspect of your worship where you treat Allah as if He is like a creature, such as expecting Him to act in a human way? Repent and renew your understanding.
?
If you were to die today, would you be certain that your shahada is free from any mental image of Allah? Ponder the negations listed in this lesson and ask yourself: do I truly accept them?
