The Proof for Divine Oneness and Its Deferral
Sanusi states the proof for divine oneness and explains its deferred placement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's proof for God's oneness and the methodological reason for discussing it at this point in the text.

Core Idea
Creation's direct dependence on God proves His oneness.

Sanusi now presents the rational proof for tawḥīd (divine oneness). He argues that all beings depend directly on God without any instrument or assistant. He also explains why this proof was deferred until now—because it relies on several prior discussions about God's attributes. This lesson unpacks the proof and its placement.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style of the Arabic.
2
Identify the three components of the proof
The passage includes three claims: the obligation of oneness, the obligation of direct dependence of all creatures on God, and the exclusivity of God and His actions. Find each one.
3
Reflect on the reason for deferral
Sanusi says the proof was delayed because it depends on much that preceded. Consider what prior knowledge (like God's attributes) is necessary for this proof.
This shows the logical order of the book.
4
Ask: what does this proof imply?
If everything depends on God directly, then nothing else is independent. This strengthens the concept of tawḥīd.
Think about how this connects to the belief in one God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليل على وجوب الوحدانية له جل وعلا و وجوب استناد الكائنات كلها إليه ابتداء بلا واسطة آلة له منها ولامعين وأنه ليس في الوجود إلا الله سبحانه وأفعاله

Indeed, he deferred the speech about oneness to this place because its proof depends on much that preceded. (1)

Commentary
This passage states the proof for the necessary oneness (al-wahdaniyya) of God and the necessary dependence of all creatures on Him directly without any medium or assistant. It then explains why the discussion of oneness was delayed to this point: because its proof depends on much that was previously established, such as the attributes of God. The footnote (1) indicates that this topic should have been mentioned with the attributes of negation. The passage thus serves as an introduction to the argument for tawhid, showing the logical order of the work.

Definition of al-Uluhiyya and Its Properties
Sanusi defines al-uluhiyya and lists its exclusive properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-uluhiyya (divinity) and its exclusive properties as taught by Sanusi.

Core Idea
Al-uluhiyya means God is self-sufficient and everything else depends on Him.

Sanusi begins by defining the core concept of divinity (al-uluhiyya) in terms of necessary existence and absolute independence. He then enumerates the properties that exclusively belong to God, showing that divinity encompasses both lordship and the right to be worshipped. This passage is fundamental for understanding the Islamic conception of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to get a general understanding of Sanusi's definitions.
Pay attention to the two alternative definitions Sanusi offers.
2
Identify the two equivalent definitions
Notice that Sanusi gives two formulations for al-uluhiyya: (1) God's existence is necessary and self-sufficient, everything else is in need; (2) God's independence from others and others' need for Him.
These are two ways of saying the same truth.
3
List the four properties of al-uluhiyya
Sanusi lists four exclusive properties: management of the world, creation of bodies, deserving of worship, and pre-eternity of the self-subsistent essence. Identify each one.
These are not shared by any other being.
4
Understand the relationship between uluhiyya and rububiyya
The passage concludes that al-uluhiyya includes al-rububiyya (lordship) and they are inseparable. Reflect on why this matters.
This means true divinity implies lordship over creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والألوهية : عبارة عن كون وجود مولانا واجباً غنياً عن الفاعل ، وأن كل ما سواه مُفتقِرٌ إليه ، وإن شئتَ قلت : الألوهية : هي استغناء مولانا جل وعزَّ عن غيره ، واحتياج كل ما سواه إليهِ
وبالجملة : فهي عبارة عن كونه خالقاً وليس بمخلوق ، ولا نزاع بينَ أهلِ الإسلام في أنَّ تدبير العالم كله وخلْقِ الأجسام ، واستحقاقِ العبادة(۱)، وقدم الذاتِ القائمة بنفسها .. كلُّها مِنْ خواص الألوهيّةِ (۲) .
(1) أي : كونها له تعالى حقاً ، لا من الاستحقاق بمعنى الحاجة إلى الشيء . صفاقسي » ( ٢/ ٩ ) (۲) أي : لا توجد إلا لمن اتصف بالألوهية ، فمن ليس بإله لا يصح في العقل أن يتصف بشيء منها. « صفاقسي ) ( ۹/۲ ) ، وبهذا تعلم تضمن الألوهية للربوبية ، واستلزام الربوبية للألوهية ، فلا انفكاك بينهما ألبتة شرعاً

And if you wish you can say: al-uluhiyya is the independence of our Master, Exalted and Majestic, from other than Him, and the need of everything other than Him for Him. In summary: it is an expression of His being Creator and not created, and there is no dispute among the people of Islam that the management of the entire world, the creation of bodies, the deserving of worship (1) – i.e., that it is a right due to Him, not in the sense of needing something – and the pre-eternity of the self-subsistent essence, all of these are among the properties of al-uluhiyya (2) – i.e., they exist only for one who is described with divinity, so whoever is not a god cannot rationally be described with any of them. By this you know that al-uluhiyya includes al-rububiyya (lordship) and that al-rububiyya necessitates al-uluhiyya, so there is no separation between them at all in terms of the law.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage defines al-uluhiyya. The author gives two equivalent definitions: (1) God's existence is necessary and self-sufficient, and everything else depends on Him; (2) God's independence and others' need. In summary, it means He is Creator and not created. The author then lists properties that are exclusive to al-uluhiyya: management of the world, creation of bodies, right to be worshipped, and pre-eternity of the self-subsistent essence. The footnotes clarify that 'deserving of worship' means it is a right due to Him, not a need, and that these properties exist only for one who is truly divine. The passage concludes by noting the mutual implication of al-uluhiyya and al-rububiyya (lordship): they are inseparable in Islamic theology.

Proof of the Impossibility of Composition and Separate Multiplicity in God
Sanusi proves the impossibility of composition and separate multiplicity in God's essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that God cannot be composed of parts nor can there be multiple gods, based on the principles of pre-eternity and the need for a specifier.

Core Idea
God's oneness excludes both internal parts and external equals.

Sanusi now presents a powerful rational argument to safeguard God's oneness. He shows that both composition (multiple parts within God) and separate multiplicity (multiple gods) lead to contradictions. This proof relies on the principle that any distinction among like things requires a specifier, which would make God contingent. Understanding this argument strengthens one's conviction in tawhid and answers the objections of polytheists.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the proof.
Notice the use of conditional reasoning (imma... aw).
2
Understand the two possibilities for internal composition
Sanusi presents two possibilities if God were composed: either each part is a god, or only one part has divine attributes. Identify these.
The first leads to multiple gods; the second to a non-God eternal being.
3
Note why each possibility is impossible
The first is impossible because multiple gods are impossible (proof to come). The second contradicts that only God is eternal and introduces the need for a specifier, which implies contingency.
The need for a specifier is a key principle in kalam.
4
Apply similar reasoning to separate multiplicity
If there were another god, it would either share divine attributes or not. Both lead to contradictions: either multiple gods or need for a specifier.
This extends the same logic to any claim of multiple gods.
5
Reflect on the underlying principle
The proof relies on the principle that any distinction between similar things requires an external specifier, which introduces contingency. This is impossible for God.
This principle is central to establishing God's uniqueness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأوَّلُ مستلزم لأنْ يكونَ كلُّ جزء إلهاً مستقلاً ، وذلك محالٌ ؛ لما ستعرفُ مِنْ برهان استحالة تعدد الآلهة (١) . والثاني يستلزم أن يكون ذلك الجزء الذي قامَتْ به صفاتُ الألوهية وحده هو الإله ، ويكون الجزء الآخرُ ذاتاً قديمة وليست بالله ، وقد عرفت فيما سبق أن لا قديمَ مِنَ الذواتِ إلا الله تعالى . وأيضاً : فاختصاص أحدِ الجزأين بأن يكون محلاً لصفاتِ الألوهية دونَ الآخر المماثل له .. يوُجِبُ الاحتياج إلى الفاعل المخصّص فتكون تلك الصفات حادثة ، وقد عرفت فيما سبق وجوب قدمِ الإله ووجوب قدم صفاته . وبمثل هذا تعرفُ استحالة التعدُّدِ المنفصل في ذات الإله ؛ لأنَّه لو كانَ ثَمَّ ذات أخرى مثلُها لم يخلُ أيضاً : إمَّا أَنْ تَتَّصفَ بمثلِ صفاتِها فيلزم تعدُّد الإله ، وسيأتي برهان بطلانه(۲) ، أو لا تتصف بمثل ذلكَ ، فيلزم احتياج صفاتِ الإلـه إلى المخصص ، ويلزم الحدوث على ما عرفت فيما مضى .
The first [possibility] necessitates that every part be an independent god, and that is impossible due to what you will know from the proof of the impossibility of multiple gods (1). And the second [possibility] necessitates that that part which possesses the attributes of divinity alone is the god, and the other part is an eternal essence not being God, and you have already learned previously that there is no eternal essence except God Most High. Also: the specification of one of the two parts to be a locus for the attributes of divinity to the exclusion of the other similar to it requires the need for a specifying agent, so those attributes would be contingent (haditha), and you have already learned previously the necessity of pre-eternity (qidam) of God and the necessity of pre-eternity of His attributes. And by similar [reasoning] you know the impossibility of separate multiplicity in the essence of God; because if there were another essence like it, it would not be free [from two possibilities]: either it would be described with similar attributes, so multiplicity of gods would follow, and the proof of its invalidity will come (2), or it would not be described with similar [attributes], so the need for a specifier for God's attributes would follow, and contingency would follow as you have previously learned.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage is a proof by contradiction (burhan) demonstrating the impossibility of composition (tarkib) in God's essence. The author first considers the case of internal composition (tarkib dakhili). He presents two possibilities: either each part is a god, which would imply multiple gods—impossible; or only one part has divine attributes, making the other part an eternal being distinct from God, contradicting the principle that only God is eternal. Additionally, even in the second case, the specification of one part to host attributes requires an external specifier, making the attributes contingent, contradicting the necessity of God's pre-eternity. Then the author applies similar reasoning to separate multiplicity (ta'addud munfasil): if there were another distinct god, it would either share attributes (multiple gods) or not, in which case the difference would require a specifier, leading to contingency. The proof relies on the principle that any distinction between similar things stems from a specifying agent, which introduces contingency.

Key Terms
تركيب (tarkib)
تعدد الآلهة (ta'addud al-aliha)
مخصّص (mukhassis)
حدوث (huduth)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God could be composed of parts without contradiction.
Correction: The proof shows that composition leads to contradictions or contingency, so it is impossible.

Checkpoint

A) That God is one
B) That God has no parts
C) That God is composed of parts
D) That God is eternal

Key Takeaway
God cannot be composed of parts nor can there be multiple gods, as both lead to logical contradictions or contingency.

The proof shows that any distinction among alleged gods would require a specifier, making them contingent. How can you actively redirect that dependence back to God alone?

This passage emphasizes that only God is eternal. How does this truth confront your fears about death and the temporary nature of this life? Are you living with the awareness that nothing besides Him lasts, or do your daily pursuits betray a love for what will perish?

The Impossibility of Multiple Gods: Unity of Attributes
Sanusi presents a proof that Allah is one in attributes, with a logical argument against multiplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the existence of two equal gods is impossible, based on the need for a specifier to distinguish them, which would imply contingency.

Core Idea
Two equals cannot be two without an external cause—so God must be one in essence and attributes.

Sanusi continues his proof of divine oneness by showing that not only is Allah one in essence, but also one in His attributes—He has no equal. The argument is subtle: if there were two gods exactly alike, each would need something to make them distinct, and that something would be an accident, which is impossible for the Necessary Being. This passage sharpens our understanding of what 'oneness of attributes' really means.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that the passage begins with a conclusion and then provides a proof. The key claim is that Allah is one in His attributes, meaning no like exists.
2
Identify the logical structure
The proof goes: if there were two likes, each would need a 'specifier' to be distinct. The specifier would be an accident. Needing an accident implies contingency (hadath). Contingency is impossible for God. Therefore, no likes.
This is a proof by contradiction: assume multiple gods, derive impossibility.
3
Understand the terms 'misl' and 'arad'
'Misl' (like) means something equal in essence and attributes. 'Arad' (accident) is a non-essential attribute. The argument shows that two exact likes cannot be numerically distinct without a differentiating accident.
This concept is crucial for tawhid.
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: If two gods were not exactly equal, they'd differ in perfection, so one would be deficient—also impossible. Thus, only one God can exist.
This reinforces the Islamic creed of absolute oneness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
محالات تلزم على القول بتعدد الآلهة ] ويلزم أيضاً : أنْ يكونَ تعالى واحداً في صفاته؛ بمعنى: أنَّهُ لا مثل له، وإلا لزم الحدوث؛ لاحتياج كلٍّ مِنَ المِثْلَينِ إلى مَنْ يُخصِّصُهُ بالعارض الذي يمتاز به عن مثلِهِ .
Impossibilities that follow from claiming multiplicity of gods.] And it also follows that He, exalted, is one in His attributes; meaning: that He has no like, otherwise creation would be necessary, because each of the two likes needs someone to specify it with the accident by which it is distinguished from its like.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage presents an impossibility that follows from the claim that there are multiple gods. It begins with the conclusion that Allah must be one in His attributes, i.e., He has no equal. The proof is: if there were two equals, each would need a 'specifier' (مخصِّص) to distinguish it from the other by an accident (عارض). Because if both are exact equals in essence, they could not be numerically distinct without a differentiating factor. That factor cannot be essential (otherwise they wouldn't be equal), so it must be an accident. But needing an accident to be distinct entails contingency (حدوث) – the need for a cause – which is impossible for God. Thus multiplicity is impossible.

Key Terms
تعدد الآلهة
مثل
عارض
تخصيص
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think this passage denies that God has attributes, but it actually denies that He has a like in those attributes.
Correction: The passage affirms God's attributes (He is one in attributes) and refutes any equal in those attributes.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi in this passage, why is it impossible for Allah to have a like?
A) Because two likes would each need a specifier to be distinct, leading to contingency.
B) Because God cannot have accidents.
C) Because two likes would necessarily differ in power.
D) Because the world is observed to be one.

Key Takeaway
Allah must be one in His attributes—having no like—otherwise multiple gods would entail contingency, which is impossible for the Necessary Being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The specifier in the argument implies that anyone who needs something outside themselves to be distinct is contingent. Reflect: In what areas of your life are you seeking distinction from others through accidental attributes (like wealth or appearance) rather than grounding your identity in Allah's oneness?
?
The proof shows that multiplicity leads to dependence. Are there any ways you have turned Allah's attributes into multiple independent realities in your mind, forgetting that His attributes are identical with His essence? How can you ensure your worship remains directed to the One who is absolutely simple and unique?

6 min
Second Argument: If Two Gods, One Must Be Incapable
Sanusi proves that a second god would necessitate incapacity, leading to the impossibility of any world existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why supposing a second god, even an agreeing one, leads to logical incapacity and impossibility.

Core Idea
A second god cannot exist because cooperation is impossible for a single undivided act.

Sanusi now presents a second, more subtle argument against polytheism. Even if the two gods were to agree perfectly, a single act cannot be split between them—so only one would act, rendering the other incapable. This leads to the absurd conclusion that nothing would exist, which contradicts what we observe.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Get a feel for the logical flow before diving into commentary.
This argument is tightly reasoned; take your time.
2
Identify the two cases
Sanusi covers two possibilities: the gods disagree or they agree. Identify where each case is mentioned and how it is handled.
The disagreement case is called 'obvious'; the agreement case requires more analysis.
3
Understand why a single act cannot be divided
Focus on the phrase 'الفعل الواحد يستحيل انقسامه' – a single act is impossible to divide. Ask yourself why this is a necessary truth.
This is the key premise that blocks cooperation.
4
Trace the conclusion to both gods being incapable
From one god's incapacity, Sanusi deduces the other's incapacity due to their similarity ('تماثل'). Notice how similarity transmits the property of incapacity.
This step is crucial: if one is incapable, the other must be also, because they are exactly alike.
5
Reflect on the final absurdity
If both are incapable, nothing can be created. But we clearly see a world exists. This contradiction proves the initial assumption (a second god) is false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً : لو كانَ معَهُ ثانٍ في الأُلوهِيَّةِ للزمَ أَنْ يكونَ ذلكَ الثاني عام القدرةِ والإرادة مثلَهُ، وذلك يؤدى إلى اتصاف أحدهما بالعجز ضرورةً، سواء اختلفا على التضاد ـ وهو ظاهر - أوِ اتَّفقا؛ لأنَّ الفعل الواحد يستحيل انقسامه، فلا يمكن أن يقعَ إلا مِنْ أحدهما؛ فيلزم عجز الآخر الذي لم يقع منه، وإذا عجز أحدهما وجبَ عجز الآخرِ : لتماثلهما، وذلكَ يؤدّي إلى ألا يُوجَدَ شيءٌ مِنَ العالم، والعِيانُ يُكذِّبُهُ
And also: if there were with Him a second in divinity, then that second would necessarily be equal in power and will to Him, and that would lead to one of them being necessarily described by incapacity, whether they differ in opposition – which is apparent – or agree; because a single act cannot be divided, so it cannot occur except from one of them; then the incapacity of the other from whom it did not occur follows, and if one is incapable, the other must be incapable due to their similarity, and that would lead to nothing of the world existing, but observed reality denies it.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage gives a second argument against polytheism. It assumes a second god equal in power and will. The argument covers two cases: either the two gods disagree (contradiction) or they agree. If they disagree, obviously one cannot prevail in creating a single act, so the other is incapable. If they agree, a single act cannot be split between them; it must come from only one, making the other incapable. Since both are exactly similar, if one is incapable, the other must be also. This would lead to no creation at all, contradicting observation.

Key Terms

العجز
الفعل الواحد
انقسام
تماثل
العيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two gods agree, they can cooperate to create the world together.
Correction: A single act cannot be divided; only one can perform it, making the other incapable. Cooperation is not possible for a single undivided act.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence of two gods agreeing on a single act, according to this passage?
A) The act would be split between them.
B) The act cannot be divided, so only one does it, making the other incapable.
C) They would both be capable but not act.
D) They would need a third to decide.

Key Takeaway
A second god is impossible because it would logically entail incapacity and the absurd non-existence of the world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The argument hinges on the impossibility of dividing a single act between two agents. Examine your worship: do you ever perform acts of prayer or charity partly for Allah and partly for recognition? How can this logical truth reshape your intention for your next act of worship?
?
If the world's existence proves there is only one God, what does your own existence say about His oneness? Are you attributing your successes to your own effort and your failures to others, rather than to Allah alone? What concrete change will you make today to consistently attribute all outcomes to Him?

First Proof Against a Like: The Distinguisher Argument
Sanusi presents the first proof against a like to Allah using the distinguisher argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that a like to Allah is impossible because sharing necessary existence would require a distinguishing attribute that cannot be necessary, leading to contingency.

Core Idea
If two beings share necessary existence, they cannot be distinguished except by a contingent attribute.

Sanusi presents a tight logical proof against the possibility of a being equal to Allah. The argument hinges on the concept of necessary existence and the principle that two distinct entities must have a distinguishing feature. This passage walks through the contradiction that arises if that distinguishing feature were necessary. Let's follow the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the gist of the argument.
Pay attention to the logical connectors: 'لو كان' (if it were) and 'للزم' (it would necessitate).
2
Identify the assumption and shared attribute
The proof assumes a like to Allah. The first consequence is that necessary existence would be shared. Why is that a problem?
Necessary existence is essential; if two share it, they are equals in essence.
3
Understand the need for distinction
Twoness requires a distinguishing attribute. The passage states 'امتناع الاثنينية بدون التمايز'.
This is a logical principle: two distinct entities cannot be identical in all attributes.
4
Analyze the impossibility of a necessary distinguishing attribute
If the distinguishing attribute were necessary, it would be essential to that being, so the other like would also have it, eliminating distinction. Therefore, it must be contingent.
Contingent attributes require a cause, making the being contingent, which contradicts necessary existence.
5
Draw the conclusion
Since a like would lead to contradiction, it is impossible. This is the first of two proofs Sanusi mentions.
This argument is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استدلَّ في هذا الكلام على بطلان وجودِ مِثْلِ ما لمولانا جل وعزَّ بدليلين : أحدهما : أَنَّهُ لو كانَ تعالى لَهُ مِثْلُ جَلَّ وتقدَّسَ عن ذلكَ للزمَ أَنْ يكون وجوب الوجودِ مشتركاً بينهما، ولزمَ أنْ يمتاز كلُّ واحدٍ منهما بصفة تُميَّزُهُ عن مثلِهِ الآخرِ؛ لامتناع الاثنينية بدونِ التمايز، ولا يمكنُ أن تكونَ هذه الصفةُ التي امتاز بها كلُّ واحدٍ منهما عن مثلِهِ واجبة له، وإلا لم يُميَّز بها، ويجب حينَئِذٍ أنْ يتّصف بها مثلُهُ (۱)؛ لاستحالة امتياز أحد المثلين بصفة واجبة عن مثلِهِ
He argued in this speech for the invalidity of the existence of a like to our Master, may He be glorified and exalted, with two proofs: one of them: that if the Exalted had a like – may He be glorified and exalted above that – then necessary existence would be shared between them, and it would be necessary that each one of them be distinguished by an attribute that distinguishes it from its like; because twoness is impossible without distinction, and it is impossible for this attribute by which each one is distinguished from its like to be necessary for it, otherwise it would not be distinguished by it, and then its like would necessarily be described by it; due to the impossibility of one of two likes being distinguished from its like by a necessary attribute.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two proofs against the possibility of a being like Allah. It argues: if there were a like, they would share 'necessary existence' (وجوب الوجود). For there to be two, there must be a distinction (تميز) between them. That distinction comes from an attribute. But this attribute cannot be necessary (واجبة) for one of them, because if it were necessary, it would be essential and the other would also have it, eliminating the distinction. Hence the attribute must be contingent, meaning each would need a cause for that attribute, implying contingency – impossible for a necessary being. The argument uses the principle that two equals cannot differ in essential attributes.

Key Terms
مثل
وجوب الوجود
تميز
صفة واجبة
اثنينية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof denies that Allah has any attributes.
Correction: The proof does not deny attributes; it argues that if there were a like, the distinguishing attribute cannot be necessary, so it must be contingent, which is impossible for a necessary being.

Checkpoint
Why cannot the distinguishing attribute between two necessary beings be necessary?
A) Because a necessary attribute must be shared by both likes, eliminating distinction.
B) Because necessary attributes cannot be known.
C) Because a distinguishing attribute must be accidental.
D) Because the attribute would then be part of the essence.

Key Takeaway
The first proof against a like to Allah shows that any distinction between two necessary beings would require a contingent attribute, which contradicts necessary existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Has there ever been a moment when you placed something or someone alongside Allah in your priorities, even if only in your heart? What does this proof demand of you regarding who or what you hold as the ultimate necessary being?
?
If the existence of a like to Allah is impossible, then your worship must be directed to Him alone. Do you truly worship Him alone, or do you allow others to share in your devotion? How will you actively guard your heart against hidden shirk?
?
The proof shows that any rival to Allah would require a contingent attribute, making it dependent. Reflect on your own dependencies: are you relying on anything that is not Allah? How can you make Allah the only necessary support in your life?

The Impossibility of a Second God
Sanusi's concise disproof of a second god.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that there cannot be a second god equal to Allah, based on the impossibility of dividing a single act.

Core Idea
A single act cannot be caused by two gods; therefore a second god is impossible.

Sanusi presents a concise but powerful argument against the possibility of a second god. The argument assumes that any second god would be equal in power and will, then shows that this leads to a logical contradiction. By examining the impossibility of a single act being performed by two agents, we see that a second god would necessarily be incapable, which contradicts the definition of divinity.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the flow of the proof.
2
Assume a second god
Notice the conditional 'if there were a second' and the immediate consequence that this second would be equal in power and will.
3
The problem of a single act
Consider why a single act cannot be divided between two agents. The author argues it must occur from one, making the other incapable.
4
Mutual incapacity conclusion
Since the two are similar, if one is incapable, the other must also be incapable, leading to a world with no acts.
5
Ocular refutation
Observe that we see actions in the world, which contradicts the conclusion, so the original assumption is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً : لو كانَ معَهُ ثانٍ في الأُلوهِيَّةِ للزمَ أَنْ يكونَ ذلكَ الثاني عام القدرةِ والإرادة مثلَهُ ، وذلك يؤدى إلى اتصاف أحدهما بالعجز ضرورةً ، سواء اختلفا على التضاد ـ وهو ظاهر - أوِ اتَّفقا ؛ لأنَّ الفعل الواحد يستحيل انقسامه ، فلا يمكن أن يقعَ إلا مِنْ أحدهما ؛ فيلزم عجز الآخر الذي لم يقع منه ، وإذا عجز أحدهما وجبَ عجز الآخرِ : لتماثلهما ، وذلكَ يؤدّي إلى ألا يُوجَدَ شيءٌ مِنَ العالم ، والعِيانُ يُكذِّبُهُ
Also: If there were a second [god] with Him in divinity, it would require that that second be equal in power and will to Him, and that leads to one of them being characterized by incapacity necessarily, whether they differ in opposition—which is apparent—or agree; because a single act is impossible to be divided, so it can only occur from one of them; thus the incapacity of the other from whom it did not occur follows, and if one of them is incapable then the other must be incapable due to their similarity, and that leads to nothing of the world existing, and ocular evidence refutes it.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage is a brief proof by contradiction of the impossibility of a second god. It begins by assuming a second god in divinity, then shows that such a second would be equal in power and will. Then it argues that with two gods, a single act cannot be divided between them, so the act can only occur from one. This necessitates the incapacity of the other. Since the two are alike, if one is incapable, the other must also be incapable (because likeness requires similar attributes). The result is that nothing in the world could exist, which is plainly false. The proof uses two key premises: (1) a single act cannot be performed by two agents simultaneously, and (2) two perfectly similar beings must share all attributes, including incapacity if one is incapable. The conclusion is the impossibility of a second god.

Key Terms
ثانٍ في الألوهية
العجز
الفعل الواحد
العيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think two gods could cooperate without conflict.
Correction: The argument shows that even agreement leads to impossibility because a single act cannot be jointly caused.

Checkpoint
According to the initial argument, if there were a second god, what would necessarily happen to a single act?
A) It would be performed by both gods together.
B) It would be divided between them.
C) It could only occur from one god, leading to the incapacity of the other.
D) Both gods would be incapable from the start.

Key Takeaway
The existence of a second god is impossible because a single act cannot be caused by two separate agents, leading to mutual incapacity and a contradiction with observable reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly internalize that a second god is impossible, how does that change the way you direct your worship? Is there any area of your life where you act as if there is another power sharing governance with Allah, such as relying on your own strength or fearing human judgment? What would it take to completely eliminate that assumption from your heart?
?
The proof uses observation of the world as evidence. Are there times when you ignore the clear signs of Allah's oneness because of your desires or distractions? How can you train yourself to see His unity in every event, even in difficulties?
?
Sanusi says that a single act cannot be divided between two agents. Reflect on your daily actions—your prayers, your work, your relationships. Are you performing them solely for Allah, or are you trying to please multiple 'gods' like status, wealth, or approval? What would it mean for you to make each act exclusively for Him?

First proof against a second god: distinguishing attributes lead to temporality
Sanusi presents the first detailed proof against a second god, showing that distinguishing attributes force temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's first rational proof that a second god is impossible because any distinguishing attribute would be accidental and temporal, entailing the temporality of the god itself.

Core Idea
If a second god existed, its distinguishing attribute would be temporal, making the god temporal.

Sanusi now presents the first detailed proof against the possibility of a second god. The proof is a logical chain: if two gods existed, each would need a unique attribute to be distinct. But that attribute cannot be necessary (otherwise it would be shared), so it must be accidental and therefore temporal. Since a being cannot be without its distinguishing attribute, the being itself becomes temporal—contradicting the necessary existence of God. This lesson walks through each step of that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Sanusi builds the proof step by step. Pay attention to the logical connectors like 'لزم' (it follows) and 'فإذا' (so then).
The footnote clarifies a key point about necessary attributes.
2
Identify the initial assumption and its immediate consequences
The proof begins by assuming a second god exists. This leads to two consequences: shared necessary existence and the need for a distinguishing attribute. Understand why 'twoness' requires distinction.
3
Analyze why the distinguishing attribute cannot be necessary
Sanusi argues that if the attribute were necessary, it would apply to both beings and thus not distinguish them. Grasp this crucial step.
The footnote reinforces this logic.
4
Trace the implications of the attribute being accidental
Since the attribute cannot be necessary, it must be accidental (عارضة) and contingent (جائزة). Accidental attributes are temporal and need a cause. This leads to the temporality of the god itself.
The principle: 'ما لا يخلو عن الحادث حادث' (whatever is never free from the temporal is temporal).
5
Reflect on the conclusion and the proof's strength
The proof concludes that each putative god would be temporal, which contradicts the necessary existence of God. Consider why this is considered one of the two foundational proofs of the creed ('برهاني العقيدة').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استدلَّ في هذا الكلام على بطلان وجودِ مِثْلِ ما لمولانا جل وعزَّ بدليلين : أحدهما : أَنَّهُ لو كانَ تعالى لَهُ مِثْلُ جَلَّ وتقدَّسَ عن ذلكَ للزمَ أَنْ يكون وجوب الوجودِ مشتركاً بينهما ، ولزمَ أنْ يمتاز كلُّ واحدٍ منهما بصفة تُميَّرُهُ عن مثلِهِ الآخرِ ؛ لامتناع الاثنينية بدونِ التمايز ، ولا يمكنُ أن تكونَ هذه الصفةُ التي امتاز بها كلُّ واحدٍ منهما عن مثلِهِ واجبة له ، وإلا لم يُميَّز بها ، ويجب حينَئِذٍ أنْ يتّصف بها مثلُهُ (۱) ؛ لاستحالة امتياز أحد المثلين بصفة واجبة عن مثلِهِ (۱) في هامش ( هـ ) عند قوله : ( ويجب حينئذٍ ) : ( أي : حين كون الصفة واجبة ) . فلزمَ إذاً أنْ تكونَ تلك الصفةُ المميّزة عارضةً لكلّ واحدٍ منهما جائزة له ، وذلك يستلزم حدوثها وافتقارها إلى الفاعل المخصص وإذا كانَتْ حادثةً لزمَ حدوث كلّ واحدٍ مِنَ الإلهين ؛ لاستحالةِ عُرُو كلّ واحدٍ منهما عن الصفة التي تُميّزه عن الآخرِ ، وقد وجبَ الحدوث لتلك الصفة التي ميَّزَتْهُ عن مثلِهِ ، فوجبَ حدوثه ؛ إِذْ ما لا يعرى عن الحادث حادث ضرورةً . هذا تقرير البرهان الأوَّلِ مِنْ برهاني العقيدة
He argued in this statement for the falsity of the existence of a like to our Master—may He be glorified and exalted—with two proofs: One of them: that if the Exalted had a like—glorified and exalted is He above that—then the necessity of existence would be shared between them, and it would be necessary that each of them be distinguished by a particular attribute to distinguish him from the other like him; because twoness is impossible without distinction. And it cannot be that this attribute by which each of them is distinguished from his like is necessary for him, otherwise it would not distinguish him, and then it would be necessary that his like be described by it; because it is impossible for one of two likes to be distinguished by a necessary attribute from his like. (Footnote: in the margin of (h) at his saying: 'and then it would be necessary': i.e., when the attribute is necessary.) So it follows that that distinguishing attribute is accidental to each of them, permissible for him, and that necessitates its temporality and its dependence on a specifying agent. And if it is temporal, then the temporality of each of the two gods follows; because it is impossible for each of them to be devoid of the attribute that distinguishes him from the other, and temporality has been established for that attribute that distinguishes him from his like, so his temporality is necessary; since that which is not devoid of the temporal is itself temporal necessarily. This is the formulation of the first proof of the two proofs of the creed.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage presents Sanusi's first detailed proof against the possibility of a second god. The proof proceeds in several steps: 1) If there were a second god like Allah, the attribute of necessary existence (wujub al-wujud) would be shared. 2) For two distinct beings to exist, each must have a distinguishing attribute (sifa mumayyiza) because numerical duality (ithnayniyya) requires differentiation. 3) This distinguishing attribute cannot be necessary (wajib) for either, because if it were necessary, it would be common to all such beings and thus would not distinguish them. 4) Therefore, the attribute must be accidental (aarida) and contingent (ja'iza). 5) Whatever is accidental is temporal (hadith) and requires a specifying agent (fa'il mukhas sis). 6) The temporal nature of the distinguishing attribute forces the temporality of the god itself, because a being cannot be devoid of what distinguishes it. 7) Since what cannot be free of the temporal is itself temporal, each putative god would be temporal, contradicting the necessity of existence. The footnote clarifies that if the attribute were necessary, it would not distinguish because it would apply to both. The proof ends with a clear conclusion that the first proof is thus established.

Key Terms
وجوب الوجود
الاثنينية
الصفة المميزة
العارضة
الجائزة
الحدوث
الفاعل المخصص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a distinguishing attribute could be eternal and still differentiate between two gods.
Correction: The proof shows that an eternal necessary attribute cannot be unique to one being; it would apply to all beings of the same kind, thus failing to distinguish.

Checkpoint
Why can't the distinguishing attribute in the proof be necessary (wajib)?
A) Because necessary attributes cannot be shared.
B) Because then it would not distinguish them, as it would apply to both.
C) Because necessary attributes are eternal and eternal things cannot distinguish.
D) Because the attribute would then be accidental.

Key Takeaway
The first proof against a second god demonstrates that any distinguishing attribute would be temporal, making the god itself temporal, which is impossible for a necessary being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that any distinction between God and another would render both temporal. Do you truly believe in the absolute oneness of Allah with your whole heart, or do you still act as if there are other sources of power in your life? What specific reliance on Allah alone will you practice today?
?
If you were to face a situation where you feel tempted to seek help from other than Allah—whether out of fear or desperation—how would this logical certainty prevent you from committing shirk in action? Write down one concrete scenario and your resolution.
?
The proof relies on the impossibility of sharing necessary existence. When you say 'La ilaha illa Allah,' do you understand that nothing deserves your ultimate love, fear, and hope except the One who alone has necessary existence? What change in your attachments needs to happen so your shahada is truly reflected in your life?

Refutation of Two Weak Gods Cooperating to Create
Sanusi refutes the hypothetical cooperation of two weak deities to produce a single atom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the possibility of two weak gods cooperating to create an atom, showing it leads to the same absurdities as the case of two independent gods.

Core Idea
Even weak gods cooperating face the same logical impossibilities as independent ones.

Sanusi now addresses a variant of the polytheist hypothesis: instead of two independent strong gods, suppose two weak gods cooperate to create a single atom. He shows that this refined hypothesis still collapses into logical absurdity, whether each has an effect or not. The proof is a model of precise dialectical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed, logical style.
2
Trace the two main possibilities
Identify that Sanusi splits the analysis into two cases: each power has some effect, or neither has any effect. Then note the subcases under the first branch.
This is a classic dialectical method.
3
Connect to earlier arguments
Notice how Sanusi references two earlier refutations: 'taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil' and 'division of the indivisible.' Recognize that the same contradictions reappear here.
This shows the consistency of his proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلتُ : لا خفاءَ في استحالة هذا الاحتمال ، وتناول الدليل له ؛ وذلكَ أنَّ القدرة الضعيفة لكلِّ واحدٍ مِنَ الإلهين في هذا الفَرْضِ لا تخلو عندَ فَرْضِ اجتماعِهما على إيجادِ الجوهرِ مِنْ أَنْ يُقالَ : هل كانَ لها شيء مِنَ التأثير أو لا ؟ فإن كان لها شيءٌ مِنَ التأثير لم يخلُ : إمَّا أن يكون أثر إحداهما هو عين أثر الأخرى ، فيلزم عليه تحصيل الحاصل كما سبق في الإلهين المستقلين ، أو يكون أثر إحداهما ليس عين أثر الأخرى ، فيلزم عليه كونُ الأثر الواحد - الذي هو وجود الجوهر الفرد - يرجع أثرينِ ، وهو معنى ما ألزمنا قبلُ مِنْ انقسامِ ما لا ينقسم . هذا كلُّهُ إنْ فُرضَ أنَّ لكلَّ مِنَ القدرتين شيئاً مِنَ التأثير عند الاجتماع . وأمَّا إِنْ فُرِضَ أَنَّهُ لا تأثير لكلِّ منهما في حال الاجتماع : فإنَّه يلزمُ حينَئِذٍ أَنَّ مجموعهما لا أثرَ لهُ ؛ إذ الصفةُ غيرُ المؤثرَةِ إِذا جُمِعَتْ إِلَى صفة أخرى غير مُؤثرةٍ ؛ كعلم مثلاً ضُمَّ إلى علم آخر.. لم يكن لمجموعهما أثر ، ثم مآلُ هذا الاجتماع المفروض أنه إثباتُ إلهين كلُّ واحدٍ منهما عاجز ! والعجز ينافي الألوهيَّةَ على ما سبق في صفات الإله
I said: There is no hiddenness in the impossibility of this possibility, and the proof encompasses it; and that is because the weak power of each of the two gods in this supposition, upon supposing their meeting to bring the atom into existence, is not free from being said: Did it have any effect or not? If it had some effect, it is not free: either the effect of one is the very same as the effect of the other, in which case repetition of the attained (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil) follows, as preceded in the case of the two independent gods; or the effect of one is not the very same as the effect of the other, in which case it follows that the single effect—which is the existence of the individual atom—becomes two effects, and that is the meaning of what we previously argued concerning the division of what does not divide. All this is if it is supposed that each of the two powers has some effect when they meet. As for if it is supposed that neither has any effect in the state of meeting: then it follows that their combined [power] has no effect; for a non-effecting attribute when joined to another non-effecting attribute—like knowledge joined to another knowledge—their combined [power] has no effect. Then the outcome of this supposed meeting is the affirmation of two gods, each of whom is incapable! And incapability contradicts divinity, as has preceded in the attributes of God.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage is a proof by contradiction refuting the hypothetical possibility of two weak deities cooperating to create a single indivisible atom (jawhar fard). Sanusi first states that the impossibility of this possibility is clear and that the earlier proof (presumably against two independent gods) also covers this case. He then analyzes the two logical possibilities for the weak power of each god upon meeting to create: either each has some effect, or neither has any effect.

In the first case (each has some effect), two sub-possibilities arise. First, if the effect of one is identical to the effect of the other, then this leads to 'taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil' (repetition of the attained), i.e., bringing about what already exists—an absurdity already refuted in the case of independent gods. Second, if the effect of one is different from the effect of the other, then it would require that a single effect (the existence of the atom) is actually two effects, which is the same as the earlier argument that an indivisible atom would have to be divided—another impossibility.

In the second case (neither has any effect when together), Sanusi argues that the combination of two non-effecting attributes (like two bits of knowledge) still yields no effect. Therefore, the combined power of the two gods has no effect, meaning each god is individually incapable (ʿājiz). But incapability contradicts divinity, as established earlier in the section on God's attributes. Thus, this hypothesis leads to affirming two incapable beings—which is absurd.

Key Terms
قدرة
تأثير
جوهر فرد
تحصيل الحاصل
انقسام ما لا ينقسم
عجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that two weak gods could together produce an effect without the problems of two independent strong gods.
Correction: Sanusi shows that even weak cooperation leads to the same logical impossibilities: either redundancy or division of the indivisible, or no effect at all.

Checkpoint
What are the two main possibilities Sanusi examines for the weak powers of two gods when they meet to create?
A) Each has some effect, or neither has any effect.
B) They are equal in power, or one is stronger.
C) They create simultaneously, or sequentially.
D) They create from nothing, or from pre-existing matter.

Key Takeaway
The cooperation of two weak gods to create a single atom leads to the same absurdities as two independent strong gods, proving the impossibility of multiple creators.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever allowed yourself to entertain the possibility of multiple creators as a casual thought experiment? How does this lesson's logical demonstration of its impossibility affect your appreciation for the absolute oneness of Allah? Will you now actively guard your mind against even hypothetical compromises on tawhid?
?
Sanusi refutes two weak gods cooperating, showing that incapability negates divinity. Do you sometimes live as if other forces—your job, your health, your relationships—have real, independent power alongside Allah? What would it mean to truly internalize that nothing has effect except by His will?
?
This proof uses logical contradiction to defend tawhid. Are you content to accept tawhid on blind faith, or do you own it intellectually? What is one area of your aqeedah where you have not demanded rational conviction? How will you pursue that clarity starting today?

Refuting the Analogy from Human Cooperation
Sanusi rejects the argument from human experience of joint lifting as invalid for divine action

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the analogy from human cooperation in lifting is invalid for divine action, and that God is the sole agent of all events.

Core Idea
Human cooperation does not prove divine cooperation; God alone is the agent of all actions.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a final objection that uses a common human experience—two people lifting a heavy object—to argue that two gods could similarly cooperate. He shows that the analogy fails because it wrongly assumes that human power has independent causal effect. As you read, notice how Sanusi both rejects the premise and reaffirms the core Ashʿarī position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic and English translation silently. Let the structure of the argument emerge naturally.
Pay attention to the opponent's example before Sanusi's refutation.
2
Identify the opponent's analogy
Notice how the opponent uses the common experience of two people lifting an object to argue that two weak gods might together produce an effect.
The opponent assumes that lifting is an effect of human power.
3
Understand Sanusi's refutation
Sanusi rejects the premise that lifting is an effect of our own power. He then states the correct view: God alone is the agent of all events.
The refutation is grounded in both reason and revelation (ʿaqlan wa naqlan).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما استشهدَ بهِ في تقرير هذا الاحتمالِ مِنْ أَنَّ الواحدَ منَّا لا يقدرُ على رفع شيء وحدَهُ ، فإذا انضم إليه غيرُه رفعاهُ : فبناءً على أنَّ الرفعَ أثر لقدرتنا الحادثة ، وذلك باطل عقلاً ونقلا ، وهذا الدليل الذي ذكرناه لإبطال هذا الاحتمالِ الذي قدَّرَهُ السائل .. متناوِلُ لإبطال ما استشهدَ بهِ أيضاً وإنَّما الرفعُ وغيرُهُ مِنْ جميع الكائناتِ لا فاعل له إلا الإله جلَّ وتعالى ، وقد يختارُ أنْ يفعل فعلاً معَ شيء ، ولا يختارُ فعلَهُ معَ شيء
And as for what he adduced as evidence in establishing this possibility—that one of us cannot lift something alone, but when another joins him they lift it—[that is] based on the assumption that lifting is an effect of our temporal power, and that is false both rationally and by revelation. And this proof that we have mentioned for refuting this possibility that the questioner supposed is also inclusive of refuting what he adduced as evidence. Rather, lifting and all other things among all existents have no agent except God, glorified and exalted, and He may choose to do an act with something, and may not choose to do it with something.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage is a final objection-and-answer in the refutation. The hypothetical opponent had cited an example from everyday experience: a lone person cannot lift a heavy object, but two people together can lift it. The opponent uses this to argue that two weak gods might together produce an effect that neither can produce alone.

Sanusi rejects this analogy by questioning its premise. The opponent assumes that lifting (al-rafʿ) is an effect of our temporal power (qudratunā al-ḥāditha). Sanusi states that this is false both rationally (ʿaqlan) and by revelation (naqlan). In Ashʿarī theology, created power has no real independent causal effect; all actions are directly created by God. Therefore, the human example does not apply to the divine case.

Sanusi then reaffirms that the earlier proof (the one given in the first lesson) is sufficient to refute both the original hypothesis and this attempted analogy. He then states the correct Ashʿarī position: all actions, including lifting and all other events, have no agent except God alone. God may choose to create an act with a means (i.e., through a human instrument) or without a means. Thus, the analogy from human cooperation is invalid because human 'cooperation' is merely God creating the effect through multiple human bodies, not an actual joint causal contribution.

Key Terms
رفع
قدرة حادثة
فاعل
عقلاً ونقلا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That human cooperation (e.g., two people lifting an object) proves that two weak gods could combine their power to create.
Correction: Sanusi argues that human power has no independent causal effect; all acts are directly from God. Therefore, the analogy does not apply to divine action.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why is the example of two people lifting a heavy object not valid as an analogy for two gods creating?
A) Because the object is not indivisible.
B) Because lifting is not an effect of human power; God alone is the agent.
C) Because the people are not gods.
D) Because the two people must be equal in strength.

Key Takeaway
The human example of cooperative lifting does not apply to God because human power has no real causal effect; God alone is the agent of all events.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult task, do you subconsciously rely on your own strength or do you recognize that all ability comes from God alone? How would your daily actions change if you truly lived knowing that you are not the agent of your own actions?
?
Have you ever caught yourself measuring God by human standards—thinking He might need help or that something is 'too much' for Him? What does this lesson reveal about the danger of such thinking?
?
If God is the sole agent of every event, including the small things you do with others, how should that affect your gratitude and your sense of partnership with others? Do you thank God for successes achieved through teamwork, or do you credit human cooperation?

The Argument from Mutual Incapacity (al-Tamānuʿ)
Sanusi proves that two gods willing opposites entails mutual incapability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument from difference (tamānuʿ) that proves the impossibility of two gods, based on the necessity of mutual incapacity when they will opposites.

Core Idea
Two gods willing opposites leads to both being incapable—contradicting divinity.

Sanusi presents a powerful logical argument known as the 'argument from mutual prevention' (tamānuʿ). By considering a concrete scenario where two supposed gods will opposite actions for the same body, he demonstrates that any outcome leads to incapacity in at least one, and by the principle of similitude, both. This argument is foundational in proving the oneness of God.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
Read the passage and imagine two gods wanting opposite things for the same body at the same time. This is the starting point of the argument.
The example uses motion and stillness.
2
Identify the logical possibilities
The author considers what can happen when both wills are set in opposition. List the possible outcomes: both wills not executed, or only one executed.
These exhaust the logical possibilities.
3
Analyze each possibility
For each outcome, trace why it leads to incapacity. Notice how the author uses the principle of similitude to extend incapacity from one god to the other.
This is the key move.
4
Conclude the argument
Since all possibilities lead to at least one god being incapable, and both are equal in divinity, they must both be incapable, which is impossible for a true god.
Thus, the supposition of two gods is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا إِنْ قُدرَ اختلافهما - كأن يريد أحدهما حركة جسم في زمان مثلاً ، ويريد الآخرُ سكونَهُ في ذلكَ الزمن - : فلا خفاءَ أَنَّ العجز هاهنا ظاهرُ اللزوم ؛ لأنَّ نفوذ إرادتهما معاً في هذا الفَرْضِ لا يمكنُ ؛ لما فيهِ مِنَ الجمعِ بين الضدَّينِ ، فلم يبقَ إلا عدم نفوذ إرادتهما معاً ، فيلزم عجزُهما معاً ، ويلزم أيضاً عُرُةُ الجِرْمِ عن الحركة والسكون ، أو نفوذ إرادة أحدهما دونَ الآخرِ ، وذلك أيضاً يستلزم عجزهما معاً ؛ أمَّا الذي لم تنفذ إرادته فظاهرٌ ، وأمَّا الذي فُرضَ نفوذُ إرادتِهِ فلانَّهُ مماثل للذي وَجَبَ عجزه ، فيجب عجزُهُ هو أيضاً ضرورةً .
As for if their disagreement is supposed – as if one of them wants the movement of a body at a time, for example, and the other wants its stillness at that time – then there is no doubt that incapacity here is clearly necessary; because the execution of both of their wills together in this supposition is impossible, due to what is in it of combining two opposites. Thus nothing remains except non-execution of both wills together, so the incapacity of both together is necessary, and also the nakedness of the body from motion and stillness necessarily follows, or the execution of one's will without the other, and that also entails both of them being incapable together. As for the one whose will was not executed, it is obvious; and as for the one whose will was supposed to be executed, it is because he is similar to the one whose incapacity was necessary, so his incapacity is also necessary necessarily.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage presents the argument from difference (ikhtilāf) between two supposed gods. The author sets up a concrete example: one god wants a body to move, the other wants it to be still at the same time. He states that incapacity (al-ʿajz) is clearly necessitated because both wills cannot be realized simultaneously—that would combine two logical opposites. The only possibilities are examined: (1) neither will is executed, which means both are incapable; (2) only one will is executed. In the second case, the god whose will was not executed is obviously incapable. However, the god whose will was executed is similar (mumāthil) to the one who is incapable, because they are equal in divinity, so by the principle of similitude, he is also necessarily incapable. Thus, in any scenario, the supposition of two gods leads to mutual incapacity, which contradicts the attribute of divinity.

Key Terms
التمانع
الضدان
العجز
الإلهين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if only one god’s will prevails, that god remains capable.
Correction: The argument uses the principle of similitude: since both are equally divine, the incapacity of one implies the incapacity of the other; thus both are incapable.

Checkpoint
What are the logical possibilities when two gods want opposite actions for the same body?
A) Both wills are executed and the body does both actions.
B) Neither will is executed, or only one is executed.
C) One will is executed and the other remains capable.
D) The body is destroyed to avoid conflict.

Key Takeaway
The argument from tamānuʿ shows that the existence of two gods logically necessitates incapacity in both, proving that God must be one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that only one God holds all power, would you fear anyone else? How does your fear of people's opinions reveal a hidden shirk in your heart?
?
When you face a situation where two desires pull you in opposite directions, do you remember that the only will that ultimately matters is Allah’s? What does that demand of you right now?
?
The argument shows that two equal gods would cancel each other. But in your life, are there 'gods'—ambitions, worries, relationships—that you treat as equally powerful with Allah? Are you willing to dethrone them today?

Refuting the Division of the World Between Two Gods
Sanusi refutes the objection of dividing the world and concludes the impossibility of multiple gods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the principle of mutual impediment (tamānuʿ) refutes any supposed coexistence of multiple gods, establishing that multiplicity itself necessitates their incapacity.

Core Idea
Multiplicity of gods logically forces each into general incapacity.

The objector suggests a scenario: two gods could simply divide the world into separate domains and never interfere with each other, thus avoiding conflict. Sanusi's answer, however, strikes at the root: the very fact of multiple gods, regardless of any agreement, entails that each is incapable of exercising universal power. This lesson unpacks that crucial rebuttal and clarifies why the earlier discussion of agreement and disagreement was only a tool to expose the logical flaw.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the objector's proposal: two gods dividing the world so each rules his own part without any opposition. Notice that this scenario tries to escape the conflict argument.
The objector concedes no conflict at all.
2
Follow Sanusi's core logical move
Sanusi's answer (qultu) states that multiplicity itself necessitates incapacity for each supposed god. Identify why: divinity requires a universal attribute of power/will, and two such universals must clash.
The key is the nature of divine attributes.
3
Understand the clarification
Sanusi emphasizes that incapacity is not limited to cases of agreement or disagreement—it is general and necessary. The earlier examples were only to reveal this hidden incapacity.
This corrects a common misunderstanding.
4
Grasp the final conclusion
The supposition of two gods leads to their incapacity and denial of divinity, entailing that nothing of the world would exist. This closes the case against multiple gods.
The argument is decisive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : بِمَ يُرَدُّ على تقدير أنَّ الإلهين اختارا قَسْمَ العالم بينهما في التدبير ؛ بحيثُ ينفرد كلُّ واحدٍ منهما بتدبير ما في يده ، ولا يمانعُهُ فِيهِ الآخرُ ، لا باتفاق ولا باختلاف ؟ قلتُ : تعدُّدُ الإله مستلزم عقلاً لعجز كلّ واحدٍ مِنَ الآلهة التي تُفَرَضُ. ودلنا على وجوبِ العجز العام لكلِّ واحدٍ منهما : ما قرَّرناهُ مِنَ التمانع في الشيء الواحد ، ولمَّا اتَّضح بذلك التقرير قيام صفة العجْزِ وصفةُ الإلهِ لا تكون إلا قديمةً عامة التعلُّق إن كانت من الصفات المتعلقة بكلّ واحدٍ منهم.. لزمَ ألا يقدر كلُّ واحدٍ مِنْ تلكَ الآلهة المتعدّدة على شيءٍ مِنَ الأشياء عموماً ، أزلاً وأبداً ، فلا تدبير لواحدٍ منهم ألبتة معَ فَرْضِ التعدُّدِ حتى يختارَ قَسْمَ العالم أو عدمَ قَسْمِهِ . وبالجملة : فذكْرُ المتكلمينَ حالةَ الاتفاق والاختلاف في الفعل الواحدِ إنَّما هو لينكشف بهِ لزومُ اتّصافِ كلَّ واحدٍ مِنَ الآلهة المفروض تعددها بالعجز ، ولا يكون ذلك العجز إلا واجباً عاماً ، وليس المعنى أنَّ الآلهة المتعدّدة لا تتصفُ بالعجز إلا في حالة اختلافها أو اتفاقها على فعل واحدٍ كما يتوهَّمُ مَنْ لا بصيرة له. فقد بانَ لكَ بهذا : أنَّ تقدير الإلهين يستلزم عجزهما ونفْيَ ألوهيتهما على كل تقدير ، وذلكَ يستلزم ألا يُوجَدَ شيءٌ مِنَ العالم ؛
If you say: How is it refuted on the supposition that the two gods chose a division of the world between them in administration, such that each one is alone in administering what is in his hand, and the other does not oppose him in it, neither by agreement nor disagreement? I say: Multiplicity of gods logically necessitates the incapacity of each one of the supposed gods. And what indicates to us the necessity of general incapacity for each one of them is what we established from mutual impediment (al-tamānuʿ) in the single thing. And when it became clear by that exposition that the attribute of incapacity arises—and the attribute of God is only eternal and universal in relation if it is among the attributes related to each one of them—it is necessary that each one of those multiple gods not be able to do any of the things at all, eternally and forever. So no administration for any one of them at all exists with the supposition of multiplicity, such that he could choose the division of the world or not. In summary: The mention by theologians of the case of agreement and disagreement in a single act is only to reveal by it the necessity of each one of the supposed multiple gods being described with incapacity, and that incapacity is not except necessary and general. And it is not the meaning that multiple gods are not described with incapacity except in the case of their disagreement or agreement on a single act, as someone without insight might imagine. So it has become clear to you by this: that the supposition of two gods entails their incapacity and the negation of their divinity in every supposition, and that entails that nothing of the world would exist.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector proposes that two gods could divide the world, each ruling a separate domain without interfering with the other, thus avoiding conflict. The answer (qultu) is that multiplicity itself (taʿaddud) logically necessitates incapacity for each supposed god. The reason is the principle of mutual impediment (tamānuʿ) established earlier: each god, by definition, must have an eternal and universal attribute of will/power. Since the attribute of divinity requires omnipotence, any being with that attribute must be able to act on everything. If there are two such beings, each must have universal power, which leads to conflict in any single thing. The tamānuʿ argument shows that they cannot both exercise universal power, so each is incapable of some things, contradicting divinity. Therefore, each is generally incapable of anything at all, eternally. The author clarifies that the earlier discussion of agreement and disagreement was only to expose this necessary general incapacity; it is not that they become incapable only in those specific scenarios. The final conclusion is that the supposition of two gods necessarily entails their incapacity and denial of divinity, and consequently, nothing of the world would exist (since creation requires omnipotence).

Key Terms
تقسيم العالم
التمانع
العجز العام
تعدّد الآلهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Multiple gods could coexist if they divide the world into separate domains without interfering.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the very attribute of divinity requires universal power; thus, even without interference, the existence of two beings with omnipotence is logically impossible, leading to each being generally incapable.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi's answer, what is the root problem with the supposition of multiple gods dividing the world?
A) They would eventually disagree, causing conflict.
B) Multiplicity itself logically necessitates each god's incapacity.
C) The division would be unequal and cause jealousy.
D) The world cannot be divided physically.

Key Takeaway
The mere supposition of multiple gods inevitably leads to each being generally incapable, negating their divinity and the possibility of a created world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument shows that even the hypothetical possibility of another god would strip each of the ability to act. Is there any area in your life where you behave as if someone or something else—money, reputation, your own plans—operates independently of Allah's will? How would your actions change if you truly believed that no power can function outside His sole authority?
?
Sanusi says multiplicity necessitates 'general incapacity'—not just in cases of disagreement. Does your heart ever harbor the notion that you can give partial allegiance to other 'gods'—status, comfort, relationships—as long as they don't openly conflict with your worship of Allah? What would it mean to let this lesson demolish every such compartmentalization?
?
If the very supposition of another god makes all divine action impossible, what does it say about the one phrase that defines faith: 'lā ilāha illa Allāh'? Ask yourself honestly: Do you live as if this statement is a logical necessity or just a ritual? What would change today if you accepted its absolute implication in every decision?

The Proof of Mutual Hindrance Against Multiple Gods
Sanusi explains the proof of mutual hindrance against multiple gods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof of mutual hindrance (tamānuʿ) and why the assumption of multiple gods necessarily entails their impotence, thus negating their divinity.

Core Idea
The assumption of multiple gods logically leads to their impotence.

In this passage, Sanusi presents the classical argument known as dalīl al-tamānuʿ. This proof is central to Islamic theology in establishing the oneness of God. By considering the logical implications of multiple gods, Sanusi demonstrates that such an assumption inevitably requires each to be powerless, which contradicts the very definition of divinity. Let's explore the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Begin by reading the Arabic text carefully. Notice the key terms: التمانع (mutual hindrance) and العجز (impotence).
The passage is dense, so take your time.
2
Understand the core argument
Identify the logical flow: multiplicity of gods leads to mutual hindrance, which implies each is powerless. The author uses the principle of tamānuʿ.
3
Examine the clarification
Sanusi clarifies that the argument is not limited to cases of agreement or disagreement; it applies universally to any assumption of multiplicity.
This addresses a common misconception.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The final conclusion is that the existence of multiple gods is impossible because it would imply their impotence and thus the non-existence of the world.
Think about why this argument is decisive in tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلتُ : تعدُّدُ الإله مستلزم عقلاً لعجز كلّ واحدٍ مِنَ الآلهة التي تُفَرَضُ ودلنا على وجوبِ العجز العام لكلِّ واحدٍ منهما : ما قرَّرناهُ مِنَ التمانع في الشيء الواحد ، ولمَّا اتَّضح بذلك التقرير قيام صفة العجْزِ وصفةُ الإلهِ لا تكون إلا قديمةً عامة التعلُّق إنْ بكلّ واحدٍ منهم كانَتْ مِنَ الصفاتِ المتعلّقة .. لزمَ ألا يقدر كلُّ واحدٍ مِنْ تلكَ الآلهة المتعدّدة على شيءٍ مِنَ الأشياء عموماً ، أزلاً وأبداً ، فلا تدبير لواحدٍ منهم ألبتة معَ فَرْضِ التعدُّدِ حتى يختارَ قَسْمَ العالم أو عدمَ قَسْمِهِ . وبالجملة : فذكْرُ المتكلمينَ حالةَ الاتفاق والاختلاف في الفعل الواحدِ إنَّما هو لينكشف بهِ لزومُ اتّصافِ كلَّ واحدٍ مِنَ الآلهة المفروض تعددها بالعجز ، ولا يكون ذلك العجز إلا واجباً عاماً ، وليس المعنى أنَّ الآلهة المتعدّدة لا تتصفُ بالعجز إلا في حالة اختلافها أو اتفاقها على فعل واحدٍ كما يتوهَّمُ مَنْ لا بصيرة له فقد بانَ لكَ بهذا : أنَّ تقدير الإلهين يستلزم عجزهما ونفْيَ ألوهيتهما على كل تقدير ، وذلكَ يستلزم ألا يُوجَدَ شيءٌ مِنَ العالم ؛
I say: The multiplicity of gods necessarily entails rationally the impotence of each one of the assumed gods. And what indicates to us the necessity of general impotence for each one of them is what we established of mutual hindrance (tamānuʿ) in one thing. And when by that elaboration it became clear that the attribute of impotence—and the attribute of God is only eternal and universal in attachment—if it were in each one of them from the related attributes, then it necessarily follows that each one of those multiple gods has no power over any thing whatsoever, eternally and forever. Thus there is no governance at all for any one of them with the assumption of multiplicity, such that He would choose the division of the world or its non-division. In summary: The mention by the theologians of the case of agreement and disagreement regarding a single act is only to reveal by it the necessary qualification of each one of the gods assumed to be multiple with impotence, and that impotence is only necessary and universal. And the meaning is not that the multiple gods are not qualified with impotence except in the state of their disagreement or agreement on a single act, as one who has no insight might imagine. So it has become clear to you by this that the assumption of two gods necessitates their impotence and the negation of their divinity on every assumption, and that necessitates that nothing of the world exist.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage presents the classical proof of mutual hindrance (dalīl al-tamānuʿ) against the possibility of multiple gods. The author begins by stating the conclusion: the assumption of multiple gods rationally necessitates that each assumed god is impotent. He then provides the evidence: the established principle of mutual hindrance in a single object. The argument is that if there were two gods, they would either agree or disagree on a single act. If they disagree, one would be unable to achieve his will, so he is impotent. If they agree, the other's will is redundant, but the key point is that the very possibility of disagreement exposes impotence. The author clarifies that the qualification of impotence must be eternal and universal, since God's attributes are eternal. He then concludes that no one of the multiple gods has any power over anything, eternally, so there is no governance at all. The theologians' mention of agreement and disagreement is merely illustrative; the true conclusion is that the assumption of multiple gods necessarily entails their impotence and thus negates their divinity, and consequently the world would not exist.

Key Terms
التمانع
العجز
الإله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the proof of mutual hindrance only shows that multiple gods would be impotent if they disagree or agree on the same act.
Correction: The author clarifies that the argument is general: the very assumption of multiplicity necessitates impotence regardless of agreement or disagreement.

Checkpoint
What does the argument of mutual hindrance (tamānuʿ) prove about the assumption of multiple gods?
A) That they could cooperate if they agreed.
B) That they are necessarily all-powerful.
C) That they are necessarily impotent, negating their divinity.
D) That they only become impotent if they disagree.

Key Takeaway
The proof of mutual hindrance (dalīl al-tamānuʿ) demonstrates that the assumption of multiple gods necessarily entails their impotence, which negates their divinity, and logically leads to the non-existence of the world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the assumption of multiple gods logically leads to their impotence, what does that mean for any form of polytheistic belief you might hold in your heart? Are there any 'gods' in your life—such as status, wealth, or desires—that you implicitly trust alongside Allah? How does this proof expose the ultimate powerlessness of those false gods?
?
Sanusi says that the assumption of two gods necessitates that nothing of the world exist. You are a part of this world. What does your very existence say about the reality of Allah's oneness? How can this truth make you more conscious of relying on Him alone in every moment?
?
The theologians used agreement and disagreement to illustrate the proof, but Sanusi warns not to misunderstand. Are there areas in your faith where you have reduced the proof of Tawhid to mere intellectual debate, rather than letting it transform your heart's devotion? What step can you take today to align your inner state with this external truth?
